# The Hollow Republic

# Chapter 1

Elise had learned to judge an office by what it tried to overpower. This one fought damp with orange peel. A strip of it browned on the little iron stove beside Inspecteur Vautrin's desk, sweetening nothing, only teaching the paper to smell more expensive. Rain ticked against the windows facing the river. His cuffs stayed clean. That was the other insistence of the room.

He had arranged two documents before she came in. The upper sheet was blue, folded twice, and stamped with the Charenton accounts seal. Beneath it lay the thinner gray file that carried her own name in a court clerk's hand, the letters narrow and mean. Vautrin always set his gloves down seam to seam before he spoke, as if leather needed discipline too.

"Sit, Mlle Valette."

She sat. The chair caned her through the wet skirt.

He touched the blue paper with one finger. "This arrived this morning."

"They wrote last week."

"Yes. Accounts offices are patient right up until they are not." He opened the fold and turned it toward her. "Your mother's bed, broth, linen, supervision, medicine when they bother with medicine. Twenty-eight francs due again on Monday. They remain accommodating because I have made them accommodating."

Charenton's name was enough. Elise did not need the rest of the print. She could have supplied it from memory: boiled sheets, old piss caught beneath soap, the iron bedstead that made her mother look already sorted into inventory. On good days her mother called her by her proper name. On bad days she called her sister, who had died at nine. On the worst days she had no use for names at all and clawed the blanket fringe until her nails tore.

Vautrin let her look just long enough for the ward to rise around her. Then he covered the bill with her gray file.

"And this," he said, "remains where it is because I prefer it there."

She did not touch that paper either. He enjoyed leaving the exact charge undescribed, as if vagueness made him generous. There had been theft in it, and forged copying in it, and one magistrate who preferred women useful rather than jailed when useful women were available. The file was not a sentence. It was a hand kept on the back of her neck.

"What do you want?" she asked.

Vautrin leaned back. He was heavy-lidded enough to look bored even when he was taking pleasure. The barbered neatness of him never failed. He kept a city of dirt under his authority and managed not to carry any of it home.

"A refuge under Montrouge," he said. "Old quarry work. We have had fragments for months. Printers. Couriers. A schoolmaster with opinions. Perhaps powder, perhaps only the people who make powder possible. In the present climate, the distinction is administrative, not moral."

Elise heard the word refuge and then the rest of it. "You want entry."

"I want clarity."

"That is not the same thing."

"It often is, if one writes the report properly."

Rain rattled harder against the glass. Somewhere beyond the pane, carriage iron rang over stone. Vautrin took a paper knife in hand and set it down again without using it.

"You will go this afternoon to rue de la Tombe-Issoire," he said. "There is a wine shop with a cellar that reaches where honest cellars do not. A courier named Henri Vasseur will meet you if the street remains quiet. He is expecting Jeanne Morel."

He slid a smaller card across the desk. On it, in his compact hand, were four facts and one lie broad enough to live in: Jeanne Morel, widow, copyist, lately turned out of lodgings near rue du Banquier, in need of work and willing to keep her mouth shut. Enough to stand on. Not enough to rest on.

"Widow?" Elise said.

"You wear black well."

"Copyist is better."

"Naturally. Your own hands improve the fiction."

Ink never wholly left the beds of her nails. She had scrubbed before coming in and carried the stain anyway.

"What does Vasseur know?" she asked.

"Only the alias and that he is to pass you onward if you do not present as an idiot."

"Pass me to whom?"

"A woman called Clemence Rigal, if fortune is kind. Others after that, if you do not waste the opportunity."

The name meant nothing yet. Elise fixed it in place all the same.

"And what do you want brought back first?" she asked. "Names? Layout? Printing capacity?"

"Yes."

She watched him.

He allowed himself the smallest smile. "Names first. Sleeping numbers. Entrances, if you can. The print equipment if there is any worth naming. Confirmation on Delatour if he is present."

"Lucien Delatour is tied to it?"

"There are suggestions. I am tired of suggestions."

She looked at the Charenton seal again. "If there is nothing down there but families and mutual pity?"

"Then you will give me the families and the pity. Shelter is infrastructure. Food is infrastructure. Laundry is infrastructure. A man who prints a tract is less dangerous alone than a woman who can hide him for three months and wash the blood out of his shirt. The laws have improved our ability to understand that."

There it was. Not a bomb crew, then. Not only that. He wanted walls, cots, bread, routes, the names of the people who made a hunted life possible. Something broad enough to drag a district through.

Elise said, "You are building a case before you have the crime."

"Association is a kind of crime now, when the company is poor enough and the papers are good." His tone remained cultivated, nearly patient. "You know this. Do not make me mistake you for sentimental."

Sentimental. He meant expensive.

She thought of Charenton's corridors again. The women with tied sleeves. The orderlies who lifted bodies under the armpits when bodies no longer cooperated. Her mother once had hands fit for lace. Now she scratched at lice, at memory, at whatever thread the ward left within reach. Elise paid to keep her indoors and out of the communal wards where women lay three to a bed when the city remembered its poor too late.

"How long?" she asked.

"The first useful report in three days. Faster if the opportunity is obvious."

"And the dragnet?"

That made him stiller. "What dragnet?"

"You don't send me under a district you already suspect unless other work is moving above it."

"Other work is always moving above everything."

It was not an answer, which was answer enough. He meant to start pulling threads while she was still learning where they ran. If the quarry broke open quickly, so much the better. If it did not, her names would help widen the sweep later. Either way the machine would eat.

Vautrin opened a drawer. From it he took a small round brass marker wrapped in cloth and three narrow slips of paper folded into near nothing.

"Your authorization," he said, indicating the brass. "Show it only if the alternative is prison or a body cart. If you show it casually, I will deny you with a clear conscience."

He touched the slips. "Milk ink. One-use substitutions. The chemistry is ordinary. The handwriting should not be." He pushed them toward her. "Do not keep them all in the same place."

The marker was warmer than the room from having sat in the drawer. One side carried the Surete's stamp, the other only a number. Not a name. Never a name.

"If a uniform recognizes this," she said, "I'm burned."

"If a uniform recognizes this and still obeys the chain of command, you are rescued. The world is full of ifs."

She slipped the brass into her glove and the papers into her reticule. "And if Vasseur is already taken?"

"Then you improvise. That is why I use you."

"Or why you can disclaim me."

"Those are not contradictory statements."

He gathered his gloves and stood. It was dismissal without the courtesy of pretending choice.

"One more thing, Mlle Valette. If you are admitted, do not fall in love with utility. These little nests of idealism grow persuasive when one is fed inside them. Bread, children, injured men speaking of justice. Domesticity has converted brighter people than you."

She almost laughed, which would have pleased him too much. "I know what bread costs."

"Exactly," he said. "That is what makes you suited."

He offered no hand. Neither did she.

In the corridor outside, the prefecture smelled of wet wool, lamp smoke, and the boots of men who did not know their names would remain off the reports. Elise stood beneath a window where the light was bad, opened the hem she had already loosened in her mourning skirt, and stitched the brass marker into it with black thread from her cuff roll. The metal dragged at the cloth once she let it fall. She divided the cipher slips: one into the whalebone channel of her bodice, one into the lining of her glove, one beneath the pasteboard sole of her left boot. By the time she finished, the rain had strengthened again.

On the omnibus south she built Jeanne Morel line by line and sanded her smooth. Widow, yes. Copyist, yes. Lodgings lost because arrears sounded common enough to pass without embroidery. Husband dead of chest trouble because half the city had coughed itself into the grave one winter or another. Not too tragic. Not too memorable. A woman useful with her pen and willing with her back when the pen failed her. The sort of woman nobody remembered until they needed to.

By the time she stepped down near Tombe-Issoire, the rain had thinned to a gray mist that kept every surface damp and undecided. Cart ruts held brown water. The wine shop Vautrin had named advertised ordinary red in wavering letters on the window. Sour wine leaked under the sill and met the street's chalky mud in a smell like rotten fruit ground into plaster.

Elise did not look at the door first. She looked at the men who were pretending not to look at it.

One leaned under a saddler's awning with his hands tucked too correctly in his sleeves. Another appeared occupied by a lame cartwheel across the street. Their coats were civilian enough. Their boots were not. Prefecture cobblers drove heel nails in a square that ordinary tradesmen seldom paid for, and these had the square. The heavier man kept his hat low and watched reflections in the wine-shop glass instead of faces. That was police work. Honest men met eyes or avoided them. Police preferred windows.

The seam in Elise's hem rasped her ankle as she shifted her weight. Vautrin had not denied the dragnet. He had simply declined to schedule it for her.

Henri Vasseur came up from the south carrying a sack on one shoulder and the smell of anise a pace ahead of him. He was thinner than she had expected, with a thickened left ear that looked as if a fight or a factory press had once tried to flatten it. He touched the knot of the sack every few steps to reassure himself the glass inside had not broken. The small private habit did more to convince her of him than any description could have. Men lied with slogans. They rarely lied with nerves.

He spotted her black dress and altered course.

Elise saw the policemen register him at the same moment.

She could still have walked away. If she turned now and took the next side street, Henri might feel the watch in time to drift past and save himself. Clemence Rigal, if she existed, would remain sealed off. Vautrin would still have her mother's bills. He would still have her file. Another route might come. Or not. The gray men under the awning were already starting to move.

Henri lifted two fingers to his cap as if greeting an ordinary acquaintance. "Mme Morel?"

Elise stayed where she was.

"Yes," she said.

His expression loosened. He had good teeth except for one chipped canine that caught when he smiled. "You made better time than I did. Come on, then. Street's filthy and Rigal gets foul when bread's late."

He shifted the sack down to free his hand for the door.

The heavy man crossed the distance in four strides.

"Henri Vasseur."

Henri did not run. That was the first thing that doomed him. The second was the half-turn of his head toward Elise, not with suspicion but with the natural need to place the danger. The policeman took his wrist and bent it high behind him. Glass struck stone inside the sack with a helpless clink. Henri made a shocked sound, small and private, before the pain had time to get its name.

The second officer came in low and hard. His shoulder drove Henri into the wine-shop shutter. Elise heard teeth hit wood. A paper-wrapped bar of soap burst from the split sack and skidded through gutter water. A loaf followed, then a packet of copybooks tied in twine.

"Police," the heavy man said to nobody who needed telling. "Keep your hands where I can see them."

Henri twisted once, not badly, trying more to save the sack than himself. That earned him a blow in the kidneys with the short cane the officer had kept up his sleeve. His knees went out. The man behind him hauled him back up by the collar rather than letting him fall cleanly.

Elise had room for three actions. She could shout. She could run. She could step in and show the brass, burn the alias, and start a life that smelled of prison straw instead of quarry damp. She did none of the three. She bent, quick and plain as any woman saving bread from muck, and caught the copybooks before the gutter took them.

Henri saw her then. Blood had opened his lower lip. Recognition arrived in his face, followed by the faster arithmetic of a courier who knew what a street was for. He swallowed whatever he had meant to say about names.

"Inside," he got out, thickly. "Go on, then."

The officer jerked his arm higher. Something in Henri's shoulder gave with a wet, ugly click.

Elise went.

The wine-shop door opened before she reached it. The woman in the doorway was broad through the shoulders and still wearing her work apron under a man's castoff coat. One boot was laced with blue cord instead of leather, and a child's red mitten hung from her belt by a safety pin, damp and forgotten there. Her hands were raw from lye to the wrist. She took in the street in one sweep: Henri folded against police grip, bread in Elise's arms, the packet of copybooks, the men under the awning who had stopped pretending to be anything else.

"Move," she said. "Unless you want the rest of them."

Elise stepped inside. The woman took the loaf from her, shoved the door with her hip, and stood listening for one beat while the arrest continued outside: Henri cursed once when they searched him, a brief bright curse that cut off when a fist or a palm found his throat.

The room behind the shop was close with sour cask wood, onion skins, and yesterday's mop water. No customers. No proprietor visible. Just a scarred table, two overturned crates, and a trap in the floor partly hidden under sacking.

The woman faced Elise. Up close, her face moved quickly even when the rest of her held still; suspicion crossed it the way sums crossed an abacus. She set the loaf on the table and kept one hand over it, as if bread itself might bolt.

"Who are you?" she asked.

Elise shifted the copybooks into the crook of one arm. "Jeanne Morel."

"That was not the question."

"It's the answer I was sent with."

The woman's mouth flattened. "Sent by who?"

"Henri Vasseur was told to expect me."

"By who?"

Elise let half a breath pass, as if measuring how much ignorance a frightened widow could admit. "A woman on the Bievre. She knew I had lost my room and could write a fair hand. She said there might be work if I kept quiet."

"Name."

"She did not offer one. I did not press."

"Convenient."

Outside, boots scraped. Someone shouted for a wagon. Henri was still close enough to hear if he screamed. He did not scream. Elise wondered if that was courage or the inability to get air back after the hit to the kidneys.

The woman said, "What did he tell you when he saw you?"

"That bread was late and you'd be angry."

One eyebrow moved. Not trust. Calculation.

"Anything else?"

"That the street was filthy." Elise looked at the wet copybooks in her hands. "He was right."

The woman stepped forward and took the packet from her. The twine had cut a red groove into the paper. She touched it, then Elise's sleeve, testing wetness, weight, truth.

"If you're police, you're badly dressed for it," she said.

"If I were police, I'd have left you the bread in the gutter and watched who came for it."

That bought her one second. The woman's eyes sharpened, not softened.

"Maybe," she said. "Show me your writing hand."

Elise did.

The callus along the middle finger was real. So was the faint burn scar inside her left wrist, paler than the rest of her skin. The woman's thumb brushed the side of Elise's forefinger where a pen rested longest. Raw lye hands. Fast-reading face. This had to be Clemence Rigal.

"You do copy work," Clemence said, not granting the whole alias, only the skill.

"Bills. Petitions. Anything people want made neat enough to be obeyed."

"And your husband?"

There it was. Elise kept her voice even. "Dead."

"That part I heard. Of what?"

"His chest went to rags over one winter. He worked in a counting room with a cracked window and called it a draft until he was spitting brown."

Clemence kept hold of her hand one beat longer than needed. "How long ago?"

"Eighteen months."

"No family?"

"Not with room to spare."

"Parish ladies?"

"They wanted gratitude before the bread. I couldn't always pay in the right tone."

That, more than the widowhood, seemed to satisfy something small in Clemence. Not belief. Recognition of a usable complaint.

She let go. "Take off your glove."

Elise did.

Clemence examined the nail beds, the lingering ink, the skin split at one knuckle. "Why come here?"

"Because I need work."

"There's laundress work aboveground. Seamstress work. Factory work if you don't mind losing fingers."

"Factory work minds me. Laundry asks references. Sewing doesn't pay if you're too slow and I'm too slow. Copy work is thin. Rent isn't."

"Where was the room?"

"Near rue du Banquier."

"How much?"

"Nine francs with no heat and the privilege of bedbugs."

"That all?"

"And the landlady's son trying the latch after midnight."

Clemence made a sound that could have been disgust or belief. "Why black if you're that broke?"

Elise looked down at the dress. "Because black dye costs less than a new life."

That got the first brief change in Clemence's face. Not amusement, exactly. A hard little acknowledgment, woman to woman, that money humiliated most cleanly when it had to pass for respectability.

Outside the door, wagon wheels ground close. Someone spat. A man's voice read a charge in the bored cadence of paper becoming flesh.

Clemence said, "Did you know they were on him?"

There was no safety in pretending stupidity. Not after the boots, not after the speed of the arrest. Elise chose the portion of truth that could survive daylight.

"I knew the street was wrong before he reached me."

"And you stayed."

"Yes."

"Why?"

Henri's shoulder had gone under the officer's hand with that wet click. Elise could still hear it. She set her bare fingers on the edge of the table where wine had raised the grain and said, "Because if I walked away, I'd still be hungry and he'd still be taken."

Clemence's gaze did not leave her.

"That's an ugly answer," she said.

"It's the answer I've got."

For the first time, Clemence seemed to make room for the possibility that Jeanne Morel, whoever she was, belonged to the same economy as the rest of them. Not safe. Not good. Merely expensive to discard.

She crossed to the trap in the floor and dragged the sacking clear. Damp air climbed up at once, chalk-cold and carrying old water with it. Somewhere below, a drop struck stone in patient intervals.

"Henri was due to bring this," she said, touching the loaf. "And paper. And lamp oil, though I suppose the bottle's smashed now. He was also due to carry two sacks farther in because the boy who should have done it is down with a cough and I don't have spare backs growing on the wall."

She glanced at Elise. "I also can't let you stand back up on the street now that you've seen this door and his face in the same minute."

"No," Elise said.

"No." Clemence reached for the copybooks, then thought better of giving them to herself and thrust them back at Elise. "Can you carry and keep your mouth shut at the same time?"

"Usually."

"Good. If you're lying, you'll lie lower down where I can watch you do it."

She bent, took the loaf and what remained of the soap, and started down. Elise followed with the copybooks and the sack, heavier now because the broken bottle had soaked everything it had not smashed.

The first steps were wood, steep and mean. The second flight turned to stone. By the third, the walls narrowed enough that Elise had to angle the sack to keep it from scraping. The air changed before the light did. Street damp gave way to chalk damp, colder and more intimate. Wine rot thinned out. Wet limestone, lamp oil, old wool, and breath took its place.

Clemence did not waste the descent.

"Who was the woman on the Bievre?" she asked over her shoulder.

"Dark hair. Forty maybe. Red wrists from lye. She was not in a mood to trade names."

"You are trying to flatter me by accident."

"No. I'm trying to tell you why I listened."

"Mmm."

They turned. The trapdoor's edge of daylight shrank above them.

"How much can you lift?" Clemence asked.

"Enough."

"Enough is a liar's unit."

"A full bucket in each hand if the floor's level. Less if it isn't."

"Can you read numbers upside down?"

"Yes."

"Can you keep a child occupied while her mother works?"

The question surprised her more than it should have. "If the child permits it."

"They never do." Clemence kept moving. "Can you gut a chicken?"

"Badly."

"Can you sew?"

"Buttons and hems."

"Can you hold your tongue when men start talking politics as if politics bakes bread?"

"Better than I can gut a chicken."

That won her a faint snort, the nearest thing to laughter Clemence had offered.

The passage leveled. A lamp had been left in a niche ahead, its flame turned low. White stone caught the light and gave it back grudgingly. Elise saw hand marks on the walls, soot on the ceiling, a groove worn by many shoulders brushing the same turn. The quarry did not open so much as continue, room by hidden room, like a thought the city had buried without killing.

Above them, muffled through all that stone, came the last blurred thud of the wagon moving off.

Henri Vasseur would be in it now with his wrists tied or ironed, his shoulder swelling under the coat, his lip drying black where the blood had started. By nightfall he would be a name on a charge sheet, or not even that if Vautrin decided paper could wait. Elise had watched the policemen take him and used the price of it at once.

Clemence lifted the lamp from the niche and looked back. In the changed light her lye-raw hands seemed almost flayed.

"Listen to me, Jeanne Morel," she said. "You're not admitted. You're useful for the next hour. After that we decide again. If anything in your story shifts when I push it, you'll wish I'd left you to the street. Do you understand?"

Elise adjusted the sack on her shoulder. Broken glass clicked somewhere inside against the neck of the surviving bottle. The brass marker in her hem dragged at her ankle like an extra bone.

"I understand," she said.

"Good. Keep up, then."

Clemence turned and led her farther in.

Elise put her free hand on the wall for the next bend. Chalk dust came away on her palm like flour. Somewhere ahead, beyond the turn she could not yet see, a child coughed, and a man's voice answered too softly to make out the words. She had entry now. She had the refuge Vautrin wanted, or the mouth of it. She had won it by standing still on a wet street while another body paid the cost of motion.

She went down after Clemence into the dark that smelled of lime, soap, and hidden people.


# Chapter 2

Elise woke with limestone grit at the corner of her mouth and the cold of borrowed blankets climbing through her coat. The quarry never went wholly dark. Somewhere down the passage a lamp kept a low orange bruise on the wall, enough to turn sleeping bodies into mounds of wool and hair silvered by dust. She lay still and listened because listening cost less than moving. Water dripped in the deeper chambers with patient regularity. Somebody coughed wetly two alcoves away. Far overhead, muffled by yards of stone, carriage wheels passed like distant thunder.

She counted eight sleepers in the gallery before she sat up. Nine, if the child under the table was not merely a pile of shawls.

Henri Vasseur was almost certainly in a cell by now, if he was still on his feet. Elise let that fact pass through her with the same hard flatness as the cold. Pity did not change bars into doors. It did not cancel what she had done in the street above. She tucked the blanket neatly, because untidiness was the kind of thing people remembered, and rubbed her hands over her sleeves to take the dust off.

"Good," a voice said from the mouth of the gallery. "You're up before I have to drag you."

Clemence Rigal stood with a lamp in one hand and a bucket in the other. In the bad light she still looked broad-shouldered enough to block the passage by herself. Her knuckles were roughened white from lye, the skin split in two places and healed badly. She had pinned her hair so tight it seemed to sharpen the planes of her face, but a curl had broken loose and stuck damply to her temple. Elise noticed, absurdly, that Clemence had taken the trouble to tie a faded blue ribbon around the handle of the bucket so Lucie would stop claiming all the pails belonged to the same kingdom.

"Take this to the wash barrel," Clemence said. "Then bring the clean rags from the line. The fever woman's soaked through two dressings in the night, and if you stand there staring much longer you'll owe us rent for the view."

"Where's the barrel?"

"If you can copy a page, you can follow a wall. Left at the saint with no face. Mind your shoulder at the squeeze. And don't put your fingers on anything that looks expensive. We don't own many expensive things, so it isn't hard to learn them all."

She thrust the bucket into Elise's hands and turned away without waiting to see whether Elise obeyed. It was a test, of course. Everything here was a test. The difference was that Clemence's tests smelled of soap, old cabbage, and human exhaustion instead of Vautrin's cologne and office coal.

Elise followed the wall exactly as instructed. The passage narrowed after the first turn until stone brushed both sleeves at once. Moisture pearled on the chalk and soaked through the cuff at her wrist. She kept her breathing even and let her eyes work the space the way she would have worked a stairwell aboveground: lamp niche, blind corner, crack in the floor large enough to catch a heel, side cut on the right carrying colder air. She did not count paces outright. Counting looked like counting. Instead she measured by the time it took the bucket to knock her knee.

The wash barrel stood in a widened chamber beside a rough trestle where shirts and bandages hung in dim rows, their hems stiff with drying lime. Elise filled the bucket from a cistern that tasted of stone even through the smell, then lifted the line's clean rags into the crook of her arm. Behind a blanket strung on wire, someone gave a low animal moan.

"That's not a door," Clemence said behind her. Elise had not heard her come back. "It's a woman with a bad lung and less patience than either of us. Hold the lamp."

Elise did. Clemence ducked through the blanket and Elise followed because refusal would have been stranger than curiosity. The clinic was only an alcove, not large enough for dignity. A woman in a man's undershirt lay on a pallet with her mouth open and her hair plastered to her cheeks. The bandage around her ribs had yellowed through at one side. A basin on the floor held pinkish water and a clot of phlegm dark as rust. Somebody had tried to sweeten the air with vinegar, but the body in the bed was stronger than vinegar.

"She was a seamstress in Batignolles before she was an inconvenience," Clemence said quietly, as if the sleeping woman might still care how she was spoken of. "Now pass me the strips."

Elise handed over the rags. Clemence's fingers, brutal-looking from work, moved with a tenderness that would have embarrassed either of them if named aloud. She loosened the wet bandage, cleaned the seep around the wound, and muttered, "Come on, my girl, don't be dramatic. If you mean to die, at least do it when the soup isn't on."

The woman made a sound that might have been laughter.

Elise had expected a hideout. A nest of men, perhaps, or a weapons store disguised as a cellar. She had not expected a sickroom where somebody remembered what the patient had done for wages before illness reduced her to a pallet and a basin.

When they stepped back into the passage, a child nearly collided with Elise's hip.

"You took the blue bucket," the girl said accusingly.

She could not have been more than ten. One stocking had slouched around her ankle, and both knees were ghosted white with quarry chalk. Her hair had been braided in haste and was already opening at the nape. She held half a crust in one hand and a length of red thread in the other, as if she had been interrupted in the middle of some private repair. Elise recognized her from the gallery now: not a pile of shawls after all.

"Lucie," Clemence said, "if you've lost another button, you'll sew it on after lesson."

"I didn't lose it. The cloth let go first." Lucie tipped her head to inspect Elise with the frankness only children and police ever used. "Can she write?"

"She says she can."

"Properly? Abel cheats at capitals when he's in a hurry."

"Abel cheats at everything when he's in a hurry," Clemence said. "Go wash your face."

"I did."

"Then wash it where everyone can see."

Lucie sighed with theatrical suffering and went, still clutching the red thread. Elise watched her step around a puddle without looking down. A child who knew the floor that well belonged to this place in the bones. So did the ribbon on the bucket. So did the seamstress behind the blanket. Elise fixed those facts where she fixed the side cut with colder air and the turn by the damaged saint.

Clemence jerked her chin deeper into the quarry. "You've got two good arms. Use them. Take these copybooks to the schoolroom, then tell Nadir the roller cloth isn't his handkerchief. If he ruins another one with ink, he can wipe the press on his own coat."

"And if he says no?"

Clemence's mouth twitched, not quite a smile. "Then you'll know he's feeling well."

The schoolroom lay beyond the sleeping gallery and short of the deeper chambers, a white limestone oval with a ceiling low enough to keep voices humble. The wall at the far side had been rubbed smooth by hands and shoulders until it looked almost deliberate, as if the quarry itself had agreed to stand in as slate. Someone had written on it in charcoal. The marks were not slogans or coded names. They were sums.

11 mouths / 3 onions = ?

Below that, in a smaller hand:

je mange  
tu manges  
il mange

Three children sat on crates with copybooks on their knees. Lucie had made it there first despite Clemence's order and was wiping her face with the cleanest corner of her sleeve. Beside her a boy with ears that stuck out like handles was trying to hide a yawn inside his fist. On the other side, a smaller girl traced the edge of a letter with a tongue-tip concentration that made Elise think of lockpicks.

The man at the wall stood with his back half turned, a charcoal nub between long fingers already dusted white. He was lean to the point of thrift, his coat shiny at the elbows, his hair silvering at the temples though his face was younger than the hair suggested. When he shifted to write the denominator more clearly, a cough took him short and hard enough to bend his shoulders for a beat. He waited it out, swallowed, and continued as if nothing worth discussion had happened.

"If eleven mouths share three onions," he said, "what do we call Abel's portion?"

Lucie's hand went up at once. "The part he steals before supper."

The man looked at her over his shoulder. Dry amusement lived in the line of his mouth. "That's a moral answer, not an arithmetic one."

"It's still true."

"Most true things are no use at all to fractions. Try again."

Elise did not need anyone to tell her this was Lucien Delatour. Authority ran toward him from every side of the room without requiring announcement. The children watched him because they wanted the next word. Clemence had sent the copybooks here because of course she had. Even his cough seemed incorporated into the daily business, not hidden, not pitied, merely accounted for like bad weather or lamp oil.

Lucie saw Elise first. "The widow's here."

Delatour turned fully then. His face was quarry-pale in a way city men never were unless they spent their days underground or indoors with illness. Fine lines had settled around his eyes. One shoulder rode a little stiff under the coat, as if old damage had learned the weather better than the rest of him.

"Then we should behave before she goes back above and reports us for barbarism," he said.

The children grinned. Lucie did not.

"She won't," Lucie said. "She hasn't even seen Abel eat."

Elise held out the copybooks. "Clemence said these were for the lesson."

"Then Clemence outranks all pedagogy." He crossed to take them, and up close she saw chalk in the seams of his knuckles and a patch at one cuff darned with thread too dark to match. "Lucien Delatour."

"Jeanne Morel."

His grip was dry, careful, and brief. No inspector's crushing assertion, no laborer's casual testing squeeze. A schoolmaster's handshake, Elise thought unwillingly, which was the kind of thought she could not have put into a report without hating herself a little.

"Can you add?" Lucie asked.

"Enough to know when I've been cheated."

"Good. Then you can sit by Abel later and slap him if he lies."

From somewhere down the passage a boy's voice answered, "I'm being slandered in rooms I haven't even entered."

Lucien lifted his chin toward the doorway. "That saves time. Come in and defend your honor properly."

Abel Saint-Clair arrived with two composing sticks tucked through his belt like dueling pistols and a smear of ink on one cheekbone. He was narrow, almost unfinished in the limbs, and his jacket sleeves had been let down so often the old stitch lines showed like scars. Two fingertips on his right hand shone pink and hard from old printer's burns. He bowed toward Elise with comic extravagance.

"Madame Widow. If Lucie has promised you violence, I ask only that you aim for the shoulder."

"Set down those sticks before you poke somebody blind," Lucien said.

"If I put them down, Nadir will claim I never worked at all."

"Nadir claims that anyway."

Abel's grin was quick and hungry. It vanished just as quickly when Lucien added, "Sit. You still owe me the plural of manger."

Abel groaned and folded himself onto a crate, knees jutting up. He stole a glance at Elise while Lucien turned back to the wall, the look half flirtation, half appraisal. She knew the type. Young men who wanted to be thought dangerous often looked at women as if every pair of eyes were a mirror they might improve themselves in.

A taller figure filled the doorway more quietly than Abel had. Nadir Belkacem stood slightly side-on even when still, as if he trusted only part of himself to any room. Ink had darkened the webbing of his hands and settled stubbornly into the lines of his nails. A nick cut pale across one cheek. He held a spoiled proof sheet in his fingers, not reading it now but folding it with patient precision into the body of a paper bird.

"Clemence says the roller cloth isn't your handkerchief," Elise told him.

He looked from the bird to her and then toward the ceiling, as if asking the Republic to witness the insult. "My reputation worsens hourly."

"Because you keep proving it," Clemence called from the passage without appearing.

Nadir's mouth bent. "You see. No due process." He finished the fold, pinched the paper beak sharp, and handed the bird to Lucie without breaking the line of his body. "For after verbs. Not before."

Lucie tucked it into the gap at her collar with serious care.

Elise's eyes went past Nadir despite herself. The print chamber opened behind him, deeper than the schoolroom and warmer by a degree from labor and lamp flame. She saw the copper shoulder of a hand press, dark type cases stacked like drawers in a countinghouse, rags gone black with ink, a drying line of handbills clipped overhead. One sheet had soup addresses in neat columns. Another looked like a wage appeal. A third was no more dangerous than a child's practice page.

Not a bomb workshop. Not today.

Lucien had begun the lesson again before she realized she was staring. "Three onions divided by eleven mouths," he said, tapping the wall. "The answer is ugly, but ugly answers still count."

"That's politics," Abel muttered.

"No," Lucien said. "That's dinner."

The room laughed, even Abel. Elise stood a moment longer than was wise, long enough to see how the white wall took charcoal and gave it back clearer than slate might have done, how Lucie mouthed the endings before writing them, how Delatour moved among the crates with the same attention Clemence had used on the bandage. No wasted gentleness. No theater. Only the assumption that children under a quarry still needed verbs and fractions.

She made herself turn away.

The passage beyond the schoolroom widened unexpectedly near a timbered post where lamp smoke had painted the ceiling black. Two sleeping alcoves opened off one side, each curtained with blankets. Deeper in, another branch sloped toward the press. Elise took in the split, the draft from somewhere farther below, the sound of water carrying from a second direction. A second exit, perhaps. Or a sump. She touched the wall as if steadying herself and marked the seam in the stone with her thumbnail.

By midday the quarry smelled less of night and more of people. Soap. Ink. Damp wool warming under bodies. Onion hitting fat in a pot somewhere ahead. Clemence put Elise to work at the trestle beside the ration room, slicing cabbage so fine her fingers numbed in the cold. The ration room itself was only shelves hacked into rock, but somebody had made it orderly: sacks of lentils tied shut with string, loaves wrapped in cloth, a wedge of cheese sweated dark at the rind, three jars of jam with the labels soaked off and reused. Clemence kept the room the way a banker kept a vault, except that bankers did not have to worry about children stealing sugar or men pretending not to be hungry.

"Not the core," Clemence said as Elise trimmed an onion. "We're poor, not stupid."

"I know how to cut an onion."

"Then cut it like you mean to feed people."

Lucie reappeared with the paper bird in one pocket and charcoal dust on her nose. "Citizen Lucien says I got the fraction right after all."

"Then citizen Lucien can scrape the pot if you're so pleased with him," Clemence said.

"He says three onions for eleven mouths is tyranny."

"He says too many things. Wash your hands."

Lucie leaned close to Elise and whispered, far too loudly, "He says it when he wants us not to notice we're hungry yet."

Clemence snorted. "See? A schoolroom only teaches children to betray you in complete sentences."

The pot thickened with lentils, barley, cabbage, onion, carrot, and the stubborn last goodness of a smoked hock that Clemence had boiled almost to surrender. Elise kept chopping, passing bowls, wiping the board clean with the side of her hand. Labor made time move differently underground. Above, an hour was a clockface. Here it was how long the dough had to rise near a lamp or how soon a fever dressing would soak through again.

When the meal was nearly ready, a man from the deeper gallery came to the trestle carrying a rolled map wrapped in oilcloth. He was older, his beard more salt than pepper, and one boot left a wet print at every step.

"Lucien wants to see whether the west cut's gone soft again," he said to Clemence. "He asked for the map."

"Then take it to him," Clemence said. "My hands are in the pot."

The man set the roll beside Elise for only a moment while he shifted his grip. Oilcloth had slipped at one corner. Beneath it lay linen backing, yellowed and repaired twice along an edge. Hand-drawn lines crossed in branching pale ink. Elise lowered her eyes at once, but the image stayed. Not enough to navigate blind. Enough to know a true map existed and that Delatour kept it close.

She had just passed him the roll when Lucien himself came in from the schoolroom, followed by Abel and Nadir carrying stacked crates for the table. He took the map without flourish and set it on an upturned barrel, weighting one curling corner with the heel of a bread loaf. Abel leaned in at once, impatient as a terrier. Nadir stayed back.

"It isn't a collapse yet," the older man said. "It's a leak and bad stone."

Lucien studied the marked branch, one finger moving along the line with practiced economy. "Then keep the children out of the west cut and move the coal sack tonight. If the wall swells further, we'll lose the dry shelf first."

"And if it doesn't?"

"Then we've moved a coal sack for our health."

No speech about sacrifice. No romance about hiding under Paris. A coal sack, a dry shelf, children kept from bad stone. Elise watched the others accept his word not because he demanded it but because he had answered in the units their lives actually used.

They ate in the sleeping gallery because it was the only chamber broad enough to take everyone without somebody balancing a bowl on their knees. Clemence insisted the sick woman and the man with the swollen ankle be served first. After that the children. After that whoever had been nearest the labor. Ceremony, Elise understood, existed here, but it was built from need rather than rank.

"Sit," Clemence told her, shoving a bowl into her hands. "If you wait for grace, the lentils will get there first."

Elise sat on a crate between Lucie and the wall. The bowl was hot enough to sting through the earthenware. Steam rose with the honest smell of onion and smoked meat. Somebody passed a heel of bread. Somebody else scraped mustard onto a plate with a fingertip because there was too little left to leave any behind. Lucie broke her crust in exact halves before eating either piece.

"You do count," Nadir said across from her, watching the division.

"Only when I need to."

"Everybody needs to," Clemence said. "Some of us simply admit it."

Abel tore bread with his teeth and spoke around it. "If she counts, she's one of us already. Nobody respectable counts lentils."

"Nobody respectable lives in a hole with you," Nadir said.

"That's a personal attack."

"It's geography."

The exchange shifted the room's weight by degrees, not much, but enough. Elise heard who teased whom, who answered Clemence at once and who pretended deafness until the second order. Lucie asked for more broth and received it after the man with the ankle had his share. Nadir wiped a drip of stew from the rim of the sick woman's bowl before passing it behind the clinic blanket. Abel, despite his noise, tore his own crust smaller to leave a piece for the boy with the handle ears. Nothing about the meal was generous by ordinary standards. That made its generosity harder, not easier, to ignore.

Lucien ate late, after the map had been rolled again and set out of harm's way. He took the last place free, opposite Elise, and thanked Clemence for the meal as if he knew thanks could not be assumed when women were this tired.

"You worked this morning," he said to Elise after a few mouthfuls. It was not quite a question.

"Clemence saw to that."

"She's reliable in these matters."

"So I've noticed."

His eyes rested on her bowl, then on her hands, as if they had a paragraph in them he was willing to read later. "Henri said you could fair-copy petitions."

The name moved once through the room and settled. Nobody asked where Henri was. Nobody needed the wound opened for the sake of conversation.

"I can copy cleanly," Elise said. "Receipts, letters, notices. Whatever pays."

"Whatever pays" made Nadir glance up. Clemence did not.

Lucien dipped bread into the broth. "There's always writing. The trick is to find the sort that doesn't hand your landlord a stick to beat you with."

"That rules out half the city."

"On a good day."

Lucie pointed her spoon at Elise. "Can you do capitals better than Abel?"

"A trained hen can do capitals better than Abel," Nadir said.

"You've built a whole doctrine of slander around me," Abel said. "It's impressive, in a small-minded way."

Lucien's mouth moved at one corner. "Finish your supper before you defend your legacy."

Then, because authority in the quarry apparently included mending what it could, he turned back to Elise. "If you're willing, the print room can use a clean hand tomorrow. Nothing dangerous. Subscription lists. A leaflet for a mutual-aid fund. Some school copy."

There it was: the press, the writing, the deeper access she had come to win. Elise should have felt the old inward click of work proceeding. Instead she felt the bowl hot against her palms and thought of the seamstress behind the blanket, Lucie dividing crusts, the map spread under bread, the equation on the wall turning onions into mathematics because there were not enough onions to leave arithmetic out of it.

"I'm willing," she said.

"Good. We need useful people more than brave ones."

Abel protested at once. "Speak for yourself."

"I generally do."

The room laughed again. Lucien coughed into his fist when the laughter died, not theatrically, only as a body interrupting a man who would rather have gone uninterrupted. He took a sip of water, and Elise saw, for one second, how tired he was under the wit and the steady voice. Not holy. Not abstract. A man holding together schoolroom, map, meal, and tunnel leak on a pair of lungs the quarry did not suit.

When the bowls were emptied and wiped with bread down to their last grease, people drifted to their separate duties as if the room had loosened a knot and tied it again. Nadir went back to the press with the quiet of somebody who preferred metal type to company but stayed for company anyway. Abel lingered to juggle spoons until Clemence threatened his ears. Lucie tucked the paper bird under her blanket before lesson copy and asked Elise, in a whisper meant to be confidential, whether widows ever stopped being tired. Elise said she did not know. It was the first wholly honest answer she had given underground.

Night came by thickening lamp smoke rather than any visible loss of light. In the alcove Clemence had given her, Elise sat with her back to the wall and arranged the day into the order Vautrin would want.

Primary entrance through the wine shop cellar on rue de la Tombe-Issoire. Sleeping gallery with at least four curtained alcoves and one common table. Secondary chambers include schoolroom, clinic alcove, ration room, and print chamber containing one copper hand press. Quarry map exists on linen backing, in Delatour's custody. Delatour identified as central authority by deference of residents and control over route decisions. Children present. Sick present. Domestic provisioning ongoing.

The report assembled itself cleanly. That was what frightened her. Every line was true, and every line would be used to turn boots toward these rooms. The schoolroom would become evidence of indoctrination. The press would become proof of conspiracy. The clinic would become sheltering fugitives. The sleeping alcoves would become a nest. Lucie would become collateral not worth writing down.

Elise pressed her thumb into the notch she had made in the wall seam earlier, a mark too small for anyone else to notice. She had come below to map a cell and name its master. By the end of one day she had mapped a household: a laundress who tied ribbon to a bucket so her daughter could keep order in a world built on shortage, a printer who folded birds out of spoiled proofs, a vain young compositor who still broke his bread smaller for a hungry child, a schoolmaster teaching fractions on stone because there was no slate and no sky, and a sick seamstress whose dressings were changed before anyone fed themselves.

Vautrin wanted conspirators. What he meant to crush was a whole social world that had learned to fit itself under rock.


# Chapter 3

The quarry had no honest morning, only a thinning of dark and the first sounds of other bodies returning to themselves. Water tapped somewhere beyond the white wall in a patient rhythm that made sleep feel borrowed. Someone turned in blankets two alcoves over. Someone coughed into wool and swallowed the rest. Elise lay on her side with the pencil stub warm in her palm and built the report sentence by sentence, as if neatness could make it less filthy.

Vautrin had not asked for impressions. He had asked for use. She could hear the word in his voice even underground, the flat weight of it, the way he made a human life sound like a tool laid back in a drawer. She had enough now to be useful. She could give him the wine-shop cellar on the rue de la Tombe-Issoire. She could give him Lucien Delatour, schoolmaster, organizer, the man the room bent around without appearing to bow. She could give him the hand press and the sleeping gallery and the ration room with its stacked brown loaves and sour smell of damp flour. She could give him the fact that no bomb materials had yet crossed her hands, only pamphlets, broth, chalk, cough medicine, and work.

What she could not give him cleanly was the shape of the place as it actually lived. A schoolroom meant children. A ration room meant somebody counted. Shirts came up from the gallery rank with sweat and quarry dust and came back folded. Somebody boiled soap, somebody fought the arithmetic of bread, somebody knew which blanket belonged over which narrow shoulders before the night cold settled. If she wrote Clemence Rigal beside the laundry line, Vautrin would not read laundress. He would read leverage. If she wrote Lucie, age ten, he would read door left unbarred.

She waited until the graphite point stopped trembling against the paper. Then she wrote in the smallest serviceable hand she had, the kind that looked like modesty until it turned up in court. She wrote that Delatour was confirmed in residence and exercised authority over instruction and printed matter. She wrote that the primary entrance remained the wine-shop cellar and that at least one deeper chamber held active press equipment. She wrote that the refuge housed more people than a single conspiratorial crew and likely served as a transit point as well as a workshop. She wrote that no explosives had yet been seen and that time was required to identify the full supply chain.

For the domestic arrangements she wrote one line and hated herself for its smoothness: Daily maintenance appears collective.

It was a lie precisely because it was almost true. Collective meant nobody. Nobody could be arrested.

She folded the paper into a strip no wider than a matchbox and slid it into the lining of her glove. The glove had split at one fingertip, and the rough edge snagged on the place inside her left wrist where old burn skin stayed shiny and pale. She sucked air between her teeth, more from the memory than the sting. Above her, from the passage mouth, Clemence's steps came down brisk and irritated, heels hard on stone.

"If you're awake, you can carry," Clemence said before she was fully in view.

She came into the gallery buttoning the same dark skirt she wore for work aboveground, a bent hairpin clenched between her teeth while she counted sheets by touch. She did not need much light for linen. Her hands went over the folded pile once, and she separated hotel cotton from private shirts, then shirts from diapers, then the better collars from the men who sweated through them like they were trying to punish the cloth. Soap had taken the top layer off her knuckles years ago. The skin there stayed pink and blunt and rough as if it had been filed.

"I was awake," Elise said.

"I can see that. Get the blue basket. Not that one, the handles are going."

Lucie popped up behind her mother with one braid finished and the other already escaping, a dented tobacco tin tucked under her arm. She kept clothespins in it and sorted them by size as if there were justice in the world of small wooden things. The bent ones were unlucky. She had explained that to Elise the night before with complete seriousness, then lined the crooked pins along the edge of her blanket so they would not spoil the straight ones.

"I counted thirty-two," Lucie said. "But two of them don't bite anymore."

"Then they're not clothespins, they're firewood." Clemence took the tin, shook it once by her ear, and handed it back. "Stay where I can see you today."

Lucie looked at Elise instead of answering. "Are you coming up too?"

"That's what your mother just told me."

"Good. You walk faster."

Clemence snorted. "That's because she hasn't been bent over washboards half her life."

The trust was still that narrow. She was a second pair of hands, someone who could be sent aboveground if watched and kept below if not. Two days earlier Clemence had measured every answer Elise gave as if she were buying meat by the gram. Now she was trusting her with linen and daylight. Elise had done worse things for less.

They climbed out through the wine-shop cellar while the barrels above them sweated sour red into the boards. Dawn had become a wet gray morning. The street stones held last night's rain in their seams, and the city smelled rinsed but not cleaned. Elise always forgot, in the quarry, how much Paris moved before breakfast. Cart wheels were already scuffing along the ruts. A baker's boy ran past with flour on his sleeve and a mouth full of song. Somewhere farther off a tram bell knocked at the air like a spoon against a pan.

The walk to the laundry yard took them along the Bievre where the river narrowed itself into an ugly working thing, more drain than water in places, cluttered with planks, dye runoff, and the stale breath of wet wool. Clemence worked there irregularly when the quarry's front addresses could spare the risk. She took in whatever came: hotel sheets, aprons from the cheap eating houses, shirts from clerks who pretended not to know where their laundry went as long as it came back white enough to fasten at the throat. Today she had a bundle from a widow who let rooms to medical students and paid late but paid.

Steam was already lifting from the washhouse sheds when they arrived. The yard rang with the slap of cloth against boards, women's voices raised over one another without ever quite becoming talk, and the cough of the river under the pilings. Elise helped unload the basket, set the bundled shirts under an awning, and kept her hand from touching the glove where the report waited. She had used this dead drop twice before on other errands, other names. Vautrin liked places that stank. Men with polished boots did not linger near privies or drainage runs unless they had business too ugly to admit.

Clemence untied the first sheet and frowned at a collar stain that had survived a previous wash. "These people think bluing is magic."

"Maybe it's magic," Lucie said. "It makes yellow lie."

"Only until the next wear."

Elise lifted a soap block from the basket. "Do we need more?"

"If you can get common lye and not be cheated, yes. If not, come back with what you have and I'll do the cheating myself."

It was permission and instruction both. Elise took the coin Clemence flicked at her, tucked the soap under her arm, and crossed the yard without haste. The privies stood against the far wall beside a cracked drainpipe that emptied into the river ditch. The stone there stayed perpetually wet, green in the joints. She stepped inside the nearest stall, bolted the door, and listened.

Outside, the washhouse went on beating and steaming. A child laughed. Somebody cursed a missing apron. No second set of feet paused at the door.

Elise pulled off the glove. The paper strip had softened slightly with the heat of her hand. She slid it into the broken mouth of the drainpipe, pushing until her fingernail could no longer feel it and only the cool slime of the masonry remained. If the pickup man did his work, the note would be gone by noon. If he had been rolled up already, or watched, or bought, the note would sit there like a confession tucked inside rot.

She kept her hand in the crack one second longer than necessary.

The missing lines rang in her head because she knew their proper form: Clemence Rigal, laundress, quartermaster, reads slowly but counts exactly; child present, female, approximately ten; names, ages, uses, points of pressure. She withdrew her hand and wiped the moisture on the underside of her skirt. The cloth came away streaked green.

When she stepped back into the yard the light had strengthened. Steam moved in torn white sheets between the sheds. Lucie was on an upturned crate, pinning a chemise to the line with elaborate care, tongue caught in the corner of her mouth. Clemence stood over a tub with both forearms buried to the elbow, working a shirt collar against the board with a violence that belonged partly to the stain and partly to the life around it.

"He wanted starch but paid for soap," she said when Elise returned. "That's the sort of man he is."

"Did you get it?" Lucie asked.

Elise held up the lye.

"Show me the coin back," Clemence said.

Elise put the remaining copper in her palm. Clemence counted without looking down, simply by weight against wet skin, then grunted approval.

For a quarter hour nothing happened except work. Elise rinsed sheets in brown water that went colder the longer her hands stayed in it. Lucie sorted pins and repeated yesterday's spelling lesson under her breath, not because anybody had asked but because she liked to say the difficult words twice once she owned them. "Republique," she murmured, then frowned. "That q shouldn't get to hide there. It's lazy."

"You'll tell the Academy when they come for inspection," Clemence said.

"I will."

Elise almost smiled. The morning had that treacherous quality some bad days had, the brief appearance of an ordinary shape before the blow arrived. A drover shouted at a mule. Two women argued over whose boiler had been moved. The river stink thickened as the heat rose from the vats. Across the lane a delivery boy flirted with a washerwoman twice his age and got laughed back into adolescence.

Then Elise saw the boots.

There was not one pair but four, then six, placed too evenly. Two uniformed agents came in from the north end of the lane while a closed police wagon rattled into view behind them, its horse blowing hard through the bit. At the south entrance plainclothes men were already taking their positions, hats low, hands free. One of them looked like a clerk until he turned and the cut of his coat showed the room it allowed for a truncheon. No brawl had brought them, and no theft complaint had either. This was a net.

The yard had not understood it yet. That was the advantage a sweep always wanted, the few stupidly full seconds when daily life kept going on its own momentum.

Elise did not think. She put the half-rinsed sheet into Clemence's hands and said, low and quick, "Leave the tubs. Take the child."

Clemence stared at her. "What?"

"Look at the street."

The first shout went up then. It was not panic yet, just authority sharpened for public use.

"Nobody leaves. Papers out. Hands where I can see them."

Another wagon rolled into place beyond the first. One of the plainclothes men pointed toward the wash sheds, and three agents broke that way at once, not running, because men who already owned the ground did not need to hurry.

Clemence's face changed without losing its hardness. Fear made her more exact, not less. Her eyes cut from the north wagon to the south entrance, then to Lucie on the crate. "How many?"

"Enough." Elise took Lucie down by the waist before the girl could ask the wrong question out loud. "Get her shawl. Bring the basket and nothing else."

"Those sheets aren't mine," Clemence said, still looking at the tubs. "If they go missing I pay."

"If you stay for them, you pay in prison."

The sentence landed. Clemence snatched the shawl off the line, wrapped it over Lucie's shoulders, and hooked the basket over her arm. One of the women at the far boiler had started to protest loudly, asking what law had been broken, whose order this was, what right they had to frighten paying customers away. The answer came as a hand on her sleeve and another on the back of her neck. She yelped when her face hit the damp wall.

The whole yard lurched awake at once.

A man bolted from the lane and was clubbed across the mouth before he made three steps. Somebody screamed for her boy. Wet shirts slid off lines into the mud. The horse on the first wagon threw its head, whites showing, while an agent cursed and yanked the reins short. The steam from the sheds trapped the shouts and flung them back. It sounded for one awful moment like the yard itself had started yelling.

Elise saw the pattern because Vautrin had taught her to see it and because once learned it stayed in the bones. They always closed the obvious exits first and checked the river side last. Water penned people. Fences did half the work. The gap would remain by the dye shed until the rear team came through the cooper's court.

"This way," she said.

Clemence caught her arm. "How do you know?"

"Move."

It was too sharp. She heard it herself. So did Clemence. There was no time to soften it.

Elise shoved a dripping sheet into Clemence's free hand. "Over the basket. Bend your head. You're angry at me because I ruined the wash, understand?"

"I don't need acting lessons."

"Good."

She put Lucie between them and drove them toward the dye shed with the purposeful, furious gait of a woman on the edge of a quarrel. The wet sheet hung over Clemence's arm like ordinary labor. Lucie's feet slapped the boards inside shoes already leaking river water. At the mouth of the shed two agents were hauling open a storage chest with so much ceremony it was obvious they expected pamphlets or guns or the one clean proof that would make the whole morning worth the price.

There was a side door beyond them, half blocked by barrels of indigo sludge. Elise had marked it when they came in because she marked all mouths and corners now whether she meant to or not.

"Down," she told Lucie.

The girl ducked instinctively. Elise took the basket from Clemence and banged it hard against a barrel. The lid flew open. Clothespins scattered across the planks and under the men's boots. Lucie gave a small involuntary cry, not for herself but for the pins. One of the agents looked down on reflex, swore, and kicked a straight pin into the sludge.

"Stupid cow," Elise said loudly to Clemence, pitching her voice for the room. "You'd trust a child with anything."

Clemence did not miss the offer. "Then don't drop it next time," she snapped back, full volume, shoving past her as if the whole emergency were domestic and personal and mean. The move carried all three of them through the side door before the agents had decided whether they mattered.

Outside, the cooper's court stank of wet oak and sour beer. A wagon tongue blocked part of the lane, but the horse had been unhitched and the space under it was open. Beyond that lay a narrow run between fence and wall that led to the tannery gutter and then, if memory held, to the alley behind the chapel storehouse. Elise had used that path once on a different errand. It was not a route a washerwoman would choose unless she had to. Today they had to.

Behind them the yard was breaking into distinct sounds: orders, crying, the smack of bodies against timber, one high furious woman still shouting about her employer's sheets as if debt could shame the state into backing away. It was a better speech than most deputies gave.

Lucie stumbled on the first cobble. Elise caught her by the back of the shawl before she went down in the tannery runoff. The cloth came away slick and cold in Elise's hand.

"Am I meant to run?" Lucie asked, breathless and affronted. "I can't tell if this is running."

"It's moving quickly," Elise said.

"Then yes."

They squeezed between the wagon tongue and the fence. Clemence went first, broad shoulders scraping both sides, the basket jammed against her hip. Elise lifted Lucie under the arms and passed her through, feeling the child's ribs light and birdlike under the wet wool. For one second Lucie clung to her neck without embarrassment, all bone and breath and river chill. Then she was down again, boots skidding.

At the mouth of the next alley a uniform turned the corner.

Elise stopped so hard her soles slid. The agent saw them, hesitated, and looked past them toward the noise from the yard. He had to decide if three wet women counted as quarry or clutter. Elise made the decision for him by hunching over the basket and saying to Clemence, in the flat exhausted voice of labor, "If you've lost the count that's not on me."

Clemence swung round with magnificent hatred. "Of course it's on you. You left the blueing open and then you dropped half my pins. Look at this mud."

She thrust the basket forward. The agent's eyes went to the wet linen, the shawl, Lucie's muddy stockings. His lip curled with the particular contempt respectable men reserved for poor women's work, which was often enough to save them for one more block.

"Clear the alley," he said. "And keep the child quiet."

"Gladly," Clemence said, and brushed past him so close her soaked sleeve left a dark mark on his coat.

They did not run until they had turned twice more and the shouts from the washhouse had thinned into city noise again. Then Clemence caught Lucie up by the wrist and dragged her through a butcher's passage, across a yard where rabbit skins hung like wet gloves, and into the lee of a shuttered coal shed. She let go only when the girl's breathing had turned ragged.

Lucie bent over, hands on her knees. "I lost four pins," she said.

"You can mourn them later," Clemence replied. She was breathing hard herself, but her first act was to look at the basket. "Soap, lye, shawl, two shirts, one sheet. Christ."

The lost laundry cost hit her face more clearly than the fright had, not because she loved linen but because linen turned into rent, into lamp oil, into soup thick enough not to shame a bowl.

Elise leaned against the coal shed and felt the whole route she had just taken throbbing in her hands, in her calves, in the bruise beginning where the basket handle had bitten her palm. She had not realized until then that she was still carrying one clothespin jammed in her fist. It had cut a square mark into the skin.

Clemence saw it. She saw everything when fear had passed and accounts began.

"You moved before they shouted twice," she said.

Elise opened her hand and let the clothespin drop into the basket. "I saw the wagons."

"So did everyone after a second."

"One second matters."

"To some people."

Lucie straightened and wiped her nose on the inside of her shawl. "Were they taking the wash or the people?"

Neither woman answered at once. The silence was long enough that the child looked from one to the other and understood the measure of the lie available to her.

"The people first," Elise said.

Lucie considered that. "Do they wash them in prison?"

"No," Clemence said. "They leave them dirty and call it the law."

She was looking at Elise when she said it. She was not accusing Elise yet, but the line had shifted. The quarry had room for hunger, coughs, politics, even the occasional stolen man. It had very little room for coincidence.

They waited until the lane settled back into ordinary movement. When they finally turned toward the refuge again, Clemence made Lucie walk between them and said almost nothing. At the wine-shop cellar she counted the rescued items aloud under her breath as if prayer might yet be made out of inventory.

"One sheet. Two shirts. Soap. Lye. Child." She shoved the basket into Elise's hands. "Next time you tell me to leave eight francs behind, you tell me why faster."

"If there's a next time," Elise said.

"There's always a next time for people like us."

Below ground the quarry air took the day's noise out of the ears by degrees. Lucie was sent to the schoolroom to dry her stockings by the stove. Clemence wrung the saved sheet so hard the water ran white with soap into the bucket, then hung it over a rope in the gallery without once looking at Elise. Trust had not broken. It had thinned to transparency.

By late afternoon a chalk mark waited on the underside of the wine-shop stair rail, small enough that anyone else would have mistaken it for scrape or dust. A vertical line crossed once, meaning pickup was complete and Elise was to present herself.

Elise stared at it for a full breath before she climbed on. The message might as well have been Vautrin's hand on the back of her neck.

She told Clemence she was going out for needles and lamp wick. This time Clemence did look at her.

"You've got long errands for a woman with no money," she said.

"Then I'll walk fast."

Clemence wiped both hands on her apron and held the stare a beat too long. "Do that."

The Prefecture offices on the Ile de la Cite always smelled of wet wool, sealing wax, and the human fatigue that paperwork produced when it was backed by cells. Clerks moved bundles of names tied in green ribbon from one room to another with the solemnity of men handling legal truth instead of guesswork sharpened by power. Elise was shown in through a side corridor because Vautrin preferred his deniable instruments to arrive like parcels, not visitors.

He was at his desk with her report laid open beside a tray of other papers. The room had already gone dim enough that the lamp had been lit, and the yellow circle it cast made the polished cuffs of his shirt look theatrical in the sour office light. He had not removed his gloves. He often did not when dealing with informants, as if skin itself might imply equality.

"Mlle Valette," he said. "Sit down."

When he used her real name he was either pleased or preparing to be disappointed. Today he sounded almost gentle.

Elise sat.

He touched the report with one gloved finger. "This is useful."

There was no comfort in him when he said useful. A dog might have felt praised.

"Delatour confirmed. Press confirmed. The cellar entrance confirmed. The refuge larger in social function than in operational purpose, at least on present evidence." He glanced up. "You write well when you are not sentimental."

"I wasn't aware I'd been sentimental."

"No? Then perhaps merely selective."

He slid the page toward her, though not close enough to touch. Her own tight hand looked cleaner on the desk than it had in the privy, more vicious for that reason.

"You give me a schoolroom," Vautrin said. "You give me instruction. You give me bread, sleeping alcoves, and daily maintenance. Yet you give me no quartermaster, no laundress, no old men, no children. It is an efficient little miracle you have discovered. Delatour appears to be instructing limestone and feeding his press on air."

Elise kept her face still. The chair seat was cane and one strand had snapped under the polish, leaving a sharp broken end that pressed through her skirt into the underside of her thigh. She welcomed the pain. It kept the rest of her from showing.

"I've been in place two nights," she said. "I sent what I could stand up in conversation if questioned."

"That is not the same as sending what is there."

"It's the same if you want me to stay there."

Vautrin sat back. He had the heavy-lidded patience of a man who liked making other people spend themselves first. "You are overestimating how delicate your position is and underestimating how tired I am of waiting for the obvious. There is always somebody who boils the water and counts the loaves. There is always somebody whose affection, hunger, or child can be used to loosen a tongue. If you do not identify those persons, I identify the district."

He opened the file beside her report. Inside lay warrants and street lists, each column ruled with such care it looked almost devotional. She saw laundry yards, print addresses, a widow's rooms near Croulebarbe, two wine shops, one rag dealer. Whole scraps of the neighborhood laid out for collection.

"This morning's sweep was not enough?" she asked.

"This morning's sweep was administrative housekeeping." He selected one sheet and signed it without hurry. The nib scratched softly. "What you have given me is structure without hinges. I prefer hinges. They are cheaper. In the absence of those, one takes doors off entire streets until a hand is caught."

He signed a second page.

"You widened it because the report was thin."

"I widened it because I will not permit a refuge of that size to remain illegible." He looked at her again, that cultivated paternal look that made every threat sound like policy. "Do not flatter yourself that omission is mercy. Omission simply redistributes cost."

For a moment the room seemed to tilt toward the desk, toward the signed sheets, toward the green ribbons waiting to bind other names. Elise saw Lucie in the washhouse yard asking if prison washing was different, and the image came not as pity but as a practical picture: the child's wet stockings, the square of mud drying on one knee, the basket lighter by four pins and most of a day's wages. Vautrin would not have bothered with Lucie first if given other leverage. He would have started with the women who worked, the renters, the old men with tabs at the coal merchant. She had known that. She had still hoped to route the damage.

"You wanted proof of a bomb plot," she said.

"I want a case that survives daylight." His voice did not rise. It became more precise instead, each word placed like a stamp on paper. "If Delatour is merely a philanthropic schoolmaster with poor taste in company, tell me that with evidence. If he is housing printers, fugitives, and future bomb men, tell me that with evidence. What I will no longer accept is architecture and mood."

He lifted another paper, but this one he did not sign. He let it rest between two fingers. "Charenton has sent a reminder. Twenty-eight francs due this month, and the arrears remain inelegant. I have continued to smooth that difficulty because your work has justified the inconvenience. Do not make me explain to the accounts office that my confidence was misplaced."

There it was: not anger, never the luxury of anger, only accounts.

Elise heard herself answer in the over-clean voice she used when rage needed laundering before speech. "What exactly do you require?"

"Names. Sleeping arrangements. The domestic authority you have declined to notice. Any alternate exits. Any storage site beyond printed matter. If there are children, I want the number and whose they are. If there are none, I want the reason for a schoolroom. And if Delatour proves to be no more than a charitable fool, I will make that distinction in the paperwork before he goes to prison."

The phrase was "before he goes to prison." Vautrin had a talent for dropping the essential obscenity into a sentence as if it were the least interesting noun in it.

"You have until the next collection," he said. "Do not send me another elegant half-truth."

He dismissed her with the same hand he had used to sign the warrants.

The corridor outside was crowded now, not with ideologues but with intake. A laundress from some other yard sat on the bench holding her apron bunched in both fists, lips moving as she counted under her breath what must have been missing pieces. A printer's assistant with ink ground into the cuticles stared at the opposite wall while a clerk copied his name wrong twice. A boy no older than the younger printer lads below ground had blood on one cuff where his mouth had split and dried there. The wet wool smell had been joined by soap, fear, cheap tobacco, menstrual iron, horse leather from the wagons outside. The state reduced people to paper only after first taking them through the body.

At the bottom of the steps two agents hauled in another woman still arguing. "Those sheets were on account," she shouted. "If they're spoiled you can pay Madame Sorel yourself, you sons of whores."

One of the agents laughed. The other twisted her arm higher until the curse broke open into a cry.

Elise stopped under the archway because if she kept walking too fast she would look guilty and if she stood too still she would become one more piece of furniture in Vautrin's building. Rain had started again, a thin cold spring rain that darkened the courtyard stones and made the horses stamp. A police wagon waited by the gate with its rear door open. Inside, wet hems and boot soles crowded the floorboards.

She had left two names off a page. By evening the omission had become a corridor full of damp aprons, split lips, and women trying to remember what the lost wash would cost.

When she turned back toward Montrouge, the city looked unchanged in the miserable way cities often did. Carts kept moving. Men still bought bread. Bells still struck the hour for whoever believed hours belonged equally to everyone. Under her glove the hand that had pushed the report into the drainpipe still smelled faintly of moss and rot.

By the time she reached the wine-shop cellar, that smell had mixed with soap.


# Chapter 4

The print chamber sat deeper than the sleeping gallery, where the chalk walls held the smell of ink and lamp oil the way old wool held rain. Elise came down the passage with a basket of rag paper braced against her hip and the same question working under everything else: where, precisely, did the bombs begin. In Vautrin's office the answer had seemed indecently simple. A quarry. Printers. Wanted men. Put those nouns in the right order and the Prefecture could call the rest conspiracy before anyone had lit a fuse. She wanted the proof now. She wanted something harder than the memory of Henri Vasseur taken at the curb while she kept walking under another name.

Nadir stood at the nearest case slightly side-on to the room, as if one shoulder expected a door to open behind it. Ink had gone so far into the creases of his hands it looked permanent. He had tucked a strip of spoiled proof into his cuff and, between one line and the next, was folding it with absent precision into a paper bird for Lucie, who waited in the passage with one stocking at her ankle and chalk dust caught in her lashes. Opposite him Abel worked the composing stick fast enough to make the metal click. He was narrow and unfinished, his cuffs too short, two fingertips glazed with old printer's burns. A violet carpenter's pencil rode behind one ear. Every few moments he pushed it back as though the gesture helped him think.

Clemence took the basket from Elise and dropped it on the table. Lye had roughened her knuckles into a red map, and one button on her skirt had been replaced with a loop of string. "If you're going to be useful, be useful with both hands," she said. "Nadir, give her the pied mess. Abel, stop courting the cases."

Nadir tipped a tray toward Elise. It held a ruin of letters, bent rules, wandering commas, and the little lead blanks that disappeared faster than bread. "Common vowels first," he said. "Otherwise you'll spend the hour blessing God over ampersands."
