"Does anyone bless God over ampersands?"

"Only Catholics and bad printers."

Lucie laughed because she liked the sound of adults pretending not to answer one another. Nadir handed her the paper bird without looking. She accepted it solemnly, checked that both wings matched, and went off toward the schoolroom with the bird balanced on her wrist.

The tray chilled Elise's palms through the metal. She began sorting by nick and weight, feeling for the notch with her thumb the way Abel had shown her the day before. The room answered itself in noises that did not belong in a police report: the throat of the press as it took pressure, Lucien's voice through the wall sounding children through syllables, Clemence counting sheets aloud because paper lost by one hand had to be paid for by another. A drip from the ceiling hit the same stone every few breaths. The lamp smoked whenever the draft shifted.

Instead of formulas or coded lists, the copy waiting beside the cases offered the humiliations of ordinary need. One handbill announced a subscription for the wives of men taken after a sweep near the river. Another listed two addresses willing to take in a seamstress and child if the rent came each Saturday in coins, not promises. There were notices for a communal soup pot, a request for boots with soles not yet split through, a page of prison visiting hours copied in letters large enough for old men with failing eyes, and four broad sheets of reading drills for the schoolroom: la, le, li, lo, lu; pain, lampe, porte. Elise had expected a hidden inventory of powder, a cache list, a date written in a hand too steady to be innocent. The quarry kept giving her bread, socks, and syllables.

She checked every margin anyway. Habits had their own appetite. She held damp proofs to the lamp, lifted blotters, turned over the scrap basket, slid her fingers under the drawer where spare sorts lay wrapped in paper. Nothing hid there except two onions, a pawn ticket for a winter coat, and a slate on which Clemence had set down the week's arithmetic: bread, eighteen loaves; lamp oil, three bottles; soap, seven bars if the price held. Beside Abel's name a ration line had been scratched out, written again, scratched out once more. He had missed suppers, then been restored to them. Clemence's mercy had the shape of revisions.

"You're not meant to search it with your whole body," Abel said.

He was close enough that she could smell metal on his fingers. He took a piece of type from between her thumb and forefinger, turned it, and dropped it into its square without glancing down. "Feel the nick. If you look first, you're late."

"I thought lateness was a sin only in schoolrooms."

"It's a sin anywhere other people are waiting on you." He nudged the case toward her. The pencil left a ghost mark on his temple when he scratched there. "And that comma doesn't live with the apostrophes. It only visits."

He slid a composing stick off the table, thumbed the measure smaller by a hair, and rebuilt in seconds a line Elise had taken a minute to spoil. A thin space here, a thicker one there, the whole thing suddenly even and inevitable under his hands. He had the unearned authority of nineteen and the earned authority of a boy who knew metal better than manners.

"What are you making?" Elise asked.

"A prison appeal. Two hundred copies if the press behaves."

"And when it doesn't?"

"Then we flatter it and curse it and flatter it again." He smiled before he could help it, quick and bright. The smile made him younger, almost painfully so. "You can make anything sing if the spacing's right."

Nadir did not look up. "He means anything can sound like a funeral if you leave him alone with it."

Abel's ears went pink. "I mean a page ought to have some blood in it."

"There," Nadir said. "He's started."

Lucie slipped back in for more spoiled sheets and stood on one scraped knee against the bench, reading the proof upside down with the grave concentration of a child testing whether letters stayed themselves when mistreated. One braid had loosened and was sticking out above her collar. "That's not pain," she said. "You've set plain."

"It's pain," Abel said.

"It has an l."

"It doesn't."

She thrust the sheet at Elise for judgment. Elise had to laugh, which still felt like spending money she had not meant to spend. "Lucie's right. You've given the bread an extra leg."

Abel snatched the proof, saw it, and swore softly enough that Clemence only clicked her tongue from across the room.

"Mind the child," Clemence said. "And make me thirty more of the laundry notices before noon. Mme Brissot says the old men by the river can't read anything smaller than a coffin lid."

"I'm not a child," Lucie said.

"Then act like an underpaid adult and fetch M. Delatour's chalk."

Lucie considered this, decided the exchange was fair, and went.

Elise took the spoiled proof from Abel and, because she wanted him talking, said, "You hate being corrected."

"I hate being corrected by ten-year-olds."

"I'm nearly eleven," Lucie called from the passage.

"You've been nearly eleven for months," Abel said back.

"That's because it takes time."

Nadir's mouth twitched. "Give her the point. It's the best argument you've heard all morning."

Abel tried not to smile. He failed. It altered him less than the first smile had. That one had been unguarded. This one knew it was observed.

Elise held the damaged sheet to the lamp. "Does the Republic require much saving from soup lists?"

Abel gave her a sideways look. "You say that as if soup lists are harmless."

"Aren't they?"

"To people who stay fed, perhaps." He tipped a fresh line into the galley, every movement exact. "You think a printed page only counts if it kills somebody. That's police thinking."

He said police without accusation, more with young contempt for any mind that sorted force into respectable and vulgar forms. Elise kept her face where she had trained it to stay: attentive, mildly abashed, useful. "I think a printed page counts according to who bothers to seize it."

Nadir looked down the stick and trimmed a line with his thumbnail. "Then you'll have noticed they seize recipes when poor people copy them in groups."

The answer ought to have pleased her. It did not. A room like this could be translated in three lines of administrative French until the onions under the drawer became supply storage and the reading cards a circulation network. Vautrin would never need to lie outright. He only needed nouns stripped of the hands that used them.

Nadir went to fetch more oil from the ration room, and Abel, relieved of the witness he respected, loosened into talk. He showed Elise the map of the case, how the common letters sat where the hand could fall to them quickest, how a hair space changed the whole temper of a line. When he forgot himself he was beautiful at it, and she disliked the thought as soon as it formed. Not because he was handsome; he was too raw for beauty and too hungry for it besides. But his work had the vanity of grace. He wanted the page to remember him.

From his apron pocket he drew a narrow strip of proof, already smudged by rereading. "This was better before Delatour got pious about it."

Elise took it. The appeal asked for coal, boots, and witness money for a cooper arrested in a sweep. Under the practical lines, in a sharper hand and darker ink, Abel had added: THE DEAD OF SEVENTY-ONE STILL WAIT IN THE STONE.

The words were not childish. That made them worse. They were the kind of line a boy wrote when he wanted grief to look back at him and choose him.

"He made you cut it?" she asked.

"He said it turned need into theater." Abel made Lucien sound older than forty-four, older than the quarry itself. "As if theater hasn't moved men since the first fool climbed a cart."

"Did it need the line?"

He bristled. "Everything needs the line if you want anybody to remember it."

Lucien came in before Elise answered. Chalk dust marked one sleeve; damp had curled the silver at his temples. He carried Lucie's primer under one arm and coughed once into his fist before he spoke. "What needs a line?"

Abel folded the strip too late to hide it.

Lucien held out his hand. "If you're going to sulk over a correction, at least let the rest of us admire it."

Abel gave over the proof with the bad grace of someone already composing his own defense. Lucien read it, and for a moment his face was unreadable except for the small tightening around the eyes that came with the cough he was refusing. Then he looked at Abel.

"It's not a bad sentence," he said.

Abel straightened despite himself.

"It's also not the cooper's wife asking for coal."

"People read what bites."

"People also freeze while boys practice sounding historic." Lucien laid the strip on the table between them. His voice stayed mild, which only made Abel's flushed silence look younger. "If you want to write something that survives, begin by letting the living take up their proper space."

Abel stared at the proof as if it had betrayed him by lying flat.

Lucien turned to Elise. "Has he taught you the difference between a thin space and a thick one yet, or only the pleasures of censorship?"

"I've learned that bread can accidentally grow an extra letter."

Lucien glanced at Abel. "Then the city is doomed."

That won him half a laugh from Clemence in the next room and a full one from Lucie, who had reappeared with chalk on her nose. Even Abel's mouth threatened mutiny for a second before pride dragged it straight again.

Lucien set the primer down beside the proofs. On the top page Lucie had written pain, lampe, mur in cramped, careful letters. "Set the appeal clean," he said to Abel. "The cooper's wife needs coal, not an anniversary."

"The anniversary's already in the walls," Abel muttered.

"Yes," Lucien said. "That doesn't mean you get to spend it."

He took Lucie back toward the schoolroom. She paused long enough to whisper to Elise, "He only gets ugly when the line's his," then ran after Lucien before Abel could hear the whole of it.

Abel worked in silence for a while, each piece of type put down a little harder than needed. Twice he touched the pocket where he had hidden the rejected strip, as if checking a pulse. Elise kept sorting and let the room settle around the little wound. She had found no formula, no target list, no timetable. The press yielded relief sheets, primers, ration notices, addresses for spare beds, and a hundred small acts of keeping one another alive through laws written to make such keeping criminal. The only thing in the chamber that leaned toward explosion was Abel himself. Not yet in deed. In appetite.

By afternoon her fingertips had taken the cold shape of metal. Ink sat in the half-moons under her nails and would stay there no matter how long she rubbed with sand soap. It was work she knew how to respect: exacting, repetitive, badly paid, ruinous to the back. It offended her, on a level deeper than politics, that Vautrin's kind of men could look at such labor and see only evidence waiting to be arranged.

But she had not forgotten why she was there. Respect did not absolve. She kept listening for the wrong emphasis, the sentence too eager for destruction, the practical discussion that would turn, at last, toward an actual target. Nothing came. Clemence wanted larger letters for the laundry notices because one old man by the Bièvre had begun pretending he could still read to save face. Nadir wanted the prison visiting sheet reset because a date had shifted. Lucien wanted fewer adjectives on the appeal because adjectives cost space and space cost money. Abel wanted the world.

There, at least, was something she could use.

Toward evening the damp thickened. Supper was cabbage water with a heel of cheese shaved through it and bread already a day nearer staleness than anyone admitted. Clemence had just begun dividing the loaf when footsteps came from the entrance passage: one set careful from habit, Lucien's, and another too loud for anyone born to hidden rooms.

The stranger ducked into the lamplight with rain beading on his moustache and cheap cologne fighting a losing war against stale sweat. He was broad across the chest in the way of men who let other people carry the patient work. Two knuckles on his right hand had healed crooked and made a ridge under the skin. Before he said a word he looked around the gallery the way some men looked into a wine shop, measuring not danger but comfort.

"God, that's cold," he said, stamping one boot and wincing. "I've had river water in my heel since noon. Tell me you don't drink chicory on purpose down here."

"Tell me your name first," Clemence said.

He took off his cap with a flourish too neat for the room. "Jules Ferrand. From Belleville by way of too many patrols and one bastard of a cabman."

Lucien came in behind him, rain dark on his shoulders. "He was sent with a name only two people here would know."

Nadir tore bread without offering any. "That's a sentence, not a guarantee."

Ferrand grinned as if he liked resistance because it gave him more surface to slide over. "I don't ask guarantees. I ask soup and somewhere to dry my socks before they rot off me."

"You'll get soup," Clemence said. "The socks depend on whether Belleville still remembers your face tomorrow."

Abel had already risen half out of his seat. Men who came in from above with rain on them carried the city's rumor along their sleeves. At nineteen he still believed rumor might select him. Ferrand saw it at once and turned enough of his grin in Abel's direction to count as an invitation.

Elise kept her bowl in both hands and watched Ferrand watch the room. No ink in the creases of his fingers. No chalk dust. No paper cuts. He had the quick readiness of a man used to entering places under names that only half belonged to him. That alone meant nothing. Half the quarry lived that way. But there was another familiarity in him, one she knew from police corridors and back rooms where informers waited to be paid: men whose courage began in other people's bodies.

Clemence gave him soup in a cracked bowl and a piece of bread smaller than the one Abel had hoped to hand over. Ferrand ate fast and talked while chewing, too easily at home. Two rooms gone in Belleville. A bookseller searched in Charonne. Patrols thick near the barriers. The police frisking carts for printed matter and taking down addresses from laundry tickets if they smelled the wrong politics on the woman carrying them. This was useful, precise enough to sound earned. Lucien listened. Even Nadir stopped glaring long enough to file it.

Then Lucien said, "What do you need besides a night's shelter?"

Ferrand wiped his mouth with the back of his hand. "Depends what sort of season you mean to have."

"I mean this week."

"This week, next week, May after that." He shrugged. "They're winding the screws for the marches already. Once a policeman starts imagining sparks, he sees them in flour sacks and hymn books. If you mean to answer, answer hard."

Clemence snapped the loaf in two. "Answer what?"

Ferrand waved the bread toward the room, making hunger itself into evidence. "The season. The dragnets. All this. Paper's decent work, I know. But if you want the boulevard to hear you, there's quarry powder to be had cheap outside Gentilly. Fuse too. Proper fuse, tarred against damp. Not that rag-wick rubbish boys buy and lose fingers to."

The room did not freeze; it narrowed. Elise felt it in the way sound changed, the lamp hiss suddenly louder than the bowls.

Nadir set down his spoon. "Interesting."

Ferrand looked at him. "What's interesting?"

"Lucien asked what you needed. You answered with powder."

Ferrand laughed, easy as spit. "I answered with what the police are preparing for. You don't answer a boot with a sermon."

"Sometimes you answer it with a better pair of boots," Clemence said. "If you know where anything is cheap, bring soap. Then coal. Then I might listen to the rest."

"Soap never made a deputy tremble."

"No," she said. "But dirty shirts keep children lousy, and that troubles me more."

Abel's eyes had not left Ferrand's face. "Proper fuse in damp matters."

Nadir turned on him. "To whom?"

Abel lifted one shoulder, already defensive. "To anyone who dislikes blowing off his own hand."

"Everyone dislikes it," Nadir said. "That isn't the same as planning it."

Ferrand leaned back, bowl balanced on his knee. "The boy has sense. That's all I said."

"And you say plenty," Nadir replied.

Elise broke her bread instead of eating it. "You name fuse quickly for a man who hasn't been told whether he's here for one night or ten."

Ferrand turned to her with the instantaneous familiarity of a man who treated every room as already won. "I name whatever the room pretends not to want."

"Mlle Morel," Clemence said without warmth.

Ferrand touched two fingers to his temple. "My apologies, Mlle Morel. I was only saying the city listens better when something cracks."

Lucien set down his bowl. "The city listens long enough to send carts."

"Then make the carts expensive."

Lucien's voice stayed quiet. "There are children in these rooms. Remember that before you start admiring cracks."

Ferrand raised his hands. "Who said anything about these rooms? Lord, schoolmaster, you hear a fuse where I only mention a market."

"A market in what?" Elise asked.

He smiled at her as if she had offered him flirtation instead of scrutiny. "In what's available."

"You said powder, fuse, and lost fingers. That's a tidy inventory for a man discussing the weather."

That made Abel glance from one to the other, suddenly aware that the exchange had edges sharper than the words. Ferrand's grin thinned but did not disappear.

"Paper makes me sneeze," he said. "Chemistry doesn't. Everyone has to excel somewhere."

Nadir barked a short laugh that held no amusement. "There. At last, a vocation."

Lucien rubbed the heel of one hand over his mouth, thinking. The cough wanted him; she could hear it waiting in the breath he was saving. He disliked paranoia because underground life produced enough of it without cultivation. Accuse the wrong man once and the room paid for it in ways no police file ever measured. Ferrand knew that too. He had chosen his first overstep carefully, large enough to be heard, small enough to be denied as mere talk.

"You'll sleep in the outer gallery tonight," Lucien said at last. "We'll discuss markets when there's daylight enough to look at one another properly."

Ferrand dipped his head as if accepting a gentleman's courtesy. "As you like."

"And if you want my attention tomorrow," Clemence said, "bring news of soap before news of heroics."

"I'll search the city for both."

"Search for soap first."

That won him the corner of a smile from Lucien and nothing from anyone else.

Supper resumed, though not at its former size. Ferrand told one story about a patrolman slipping in gutter mud and another about a cabman who thought every laborer in Paris was an anarchist if he tipped badly. He told them well. That was part of the trouble. His anecdotes had the loose fast quality of tavern speech, all elbows and confidence, skimming over the places where a careful listener might ask who had been present, when exactly, by whose invitation. Abel laughed first. Lucie did not laugh at all. She had come back in for her bread and now sat tucked against Clemence's side, watching Ferrand with the direct animal caution children sometimes brought to rooms that adults overexplained.

When the bowls were empty and the bread cloth folded away, Ferrand followed Abel toward the print chamber with the excuse that he wanted to see the press. "I've always liked machines," he said. "More honest than committees."

"Paper makes you sneeze," Nadir reminded him.

"Presses don't."

Nadir started to rise. Lucien stopped him with a slight movement of the hand, not because he trusted Ferrand but because he refused to stage a brawl out of intuition. Elise understood the choice. She hated it immediately.

The print chamber felt smaller with Ferrand in it. He stood too close to Abel's shoulder, smelling of wet wool, street tobacco, and the sweet cheap cologne that never quite hid a day's sweat. The press, still tacky with ink, gleamed dully in the lamplight. Ferrand ran a finger along the frame without touching the ink itself, like a man admiring a horse he had no intention of grooming.

"Good stock," he said. "Better than half the shops above."

"Nadir keeps it from dying," Abel said.

"And you?"

Abel lifted his chin. "I keep it from looking dead."

Ferrand laughed low in his throat. "That's the boy."

Elise took up the tray of damaged sorts and began resorting it again, though there was no need. She wanted an excuse to stay. Through the open door she could hear Lucien in the gallery, speaking softly to Clemence about bedrolls and morning routes. The ordinary sounds of hiding. Inside the print room Ferrand's attention moved over Abel with a patience he had not given the rest of them. That, more than the bomb talk, made Elise's skin feel rubbed the wrong way.

Abel, eager under the gaze and ashamed of eagerness, made a show of straightening proofs. The rejected strip slid from his pocket and landed on the table. Ferrand picked it up before Abel could snatch it back.

"Nothing," Abel said too fast. "Only a line Delatour thought too pretty."

Ferrand read it once with his lips moving slightly, as if he respected the act of reading enough to slow for it. Then he looked at Abel.

"The dead of seventy-one still wait in the stone," he said. "That's not for a soup appeal."

Abel's shrug carried too much force to count as indifference. "That's what I said."

"Delatour said it was theater," Elise said.

Ferrand turned the strip over between finger and thumb. "Maybe. But theater fills a square faster than arithmetic."

From the passage Lucien said, "Arithmetic keeps people alive long enough to reach the square."

Ferrand smiled without surprise. "I wasn't insulting arithmetic."

"No," Lucien said. "Only the people doing it."

For the first time Ferrand's expression showed a seam. Not anger. Annoyance, quickly papered. He set the strip down and spread his hands. "You're touchy for a schoolmaster."

"I'm tired," Lucien said. "There's a difference."

Abel looked from one man to the other, wanting the scene larger than it was, wanting someone to strike a table or name a doctrine or at least choose a side loud enough for history to hear. Nobody did. Clemence called from the gallery for Lucie to stop pretending sleep exempted her from washing her hands. Nadir banged a spoon against a bowl hard enough to register as opinion. The quarry returned, stubbornly, to its own scale.

Ferrand gave Abel back the proof strip with two fingers, privately, as if handing over a contraband pleasure. "It's a good line," he said. "It deserves a wall, not a basket."

Abel closed his burnt fingertips over the paper.

That was all. No pact. No whispered target. No map opened on the table. But Elise saw the current complete itself anyway. She had spent the day looking for violence in the press, in the drawers, in the copy, in the old chalk tunnels that the Prefecture already treated as an arsenal in waiting. The press had given her relief sheets, prison hours, children's lessons, and laundry notices. Ferrand had brought the first real odor of catastrophe with him, and Abel had turned toward it the way poor boys sometimes turned toward candles, knowing perfectly well what fire did and loving the promise of light all the same.

Later, when the lamp was turned down and the chamber went back to chalk, damp, and breathing, Elise lay awake on her blanket with the taste of cabbage still sour on her tongue. If she wrote the quarry cleanly for Vautrin, he would have enough names to break a shelter. If she wrote it falsely, she might save these rooms long enough for something worse to rip through them from the inside. Abel did not want merely revenge or justice. He wanted scale, witness, a sentence big enough to step into and disappear. Ferrand had heard that hunger within an hour.

It was the first thing all day that felt like evidence.


# Chapter 5

The paper in Elise's cuff had gone soft from rain and wrist sweat by the time she came back through the wine shop cellar.

She had found it where Vautrin liked to leave such things, folded under a chipped saucer near the privy sink, as if an order could pass for refuse if it lay beside enough dirt. The hand was not his. He never wasted his own. But the style was his down to the bones: NAMES. MEANS. PLACE. BEFORE THURSDAY NIGHT. DO NOT BRING ME PANIC INSTEAD OF FACT.

Aboveground the gutters on rue de la Tombe-Issoire were running brown, and the men pasting notices for the May Day marches had already been cursed twice by the police before noon. Belowground the quarry kept its own weather. Damp climbed the walls. Water clicked somewhere deeper in the chalk. The air smelled of lamp oil, wet wool, old stone, and the cabbage soup Clemence had stretched too far at supper.

Voices were carrying from the schoolroom before Elise had reached the last turn.

Not Lucien's voice. His never carried unless he meant it to. This one came loose and loud and sure of itself, a voice that wanted a wall to hit and a room to take. Ferrand.

Elise went in with the market basket on her arm and saw at once that the room had already been claimed.

Lucien's crate had been dragged near the wall where he taught the children their letters. Ferrand leaned against it as if it belonged to him, dark moustache damp at the edges, cheap cologne failing to smother the stale sweat in his collar. His satchel rested by his boot. The leather looked decent until one noticed the cracked strap and the buckle repaired with string. Abel stood half a pace from him, too intent to hide it, his narrow coat riding above his wrists and the printer's burns on two fingertips bright as little dead moons when he spread his hand to argue. Nadir had not sat down at all. He stayed side-on in the print-room doorway with a composing stick still in his pocket and a paper crane folded from spoiled proof perched on his knuckle like a joke he had not decided to make. Clemence was darning a stocking without looking at it, stabbing the needle through wool hard enough to punish somebody. Lucie had been sent to bed or should have been, but she was on the bench by the wall with one braid half-undone and chalk dust on both knees, copying capital letters with the tip of her finger into a patch of limestone flour.

Lucien looked more tired than the hour deserved. The damp had gone into his cough again. There was chalk on the cuff of his coat and a rent at the elbow he had not had yesterday. He lifted his head when Elise came in, took in the basket, and gave her the briefest nod toward the bench by the lamp.

"Jeanne. You may as well hear the rest of it," he said.

"Then hear it properly," Ferrand said before Elise could answer. "I'm tired of saying the same thing to people who flinch at their own shadow. The city will be thick with workers on Thursday. Thick with police too, yes, but that's the point. Noise, crush, horses, whistles, everybody looking the wrong way. If a thing is going to be done, that's when it's done."

Clemence pulled the stocking tighter over her fist. "If a thing is going to be done, you can name the thing before you start enjoying it."

Ferrand spread his hands. The grin on him said camaraderie. The eyes did not bother with the disguise. "A telegraph junction. A patrol wagon. A review stand if they build one stupid enough. Something that teaches them a march is not a parish outing."

Lucie looked up from the floor. "What's a review stand?"

"A thing that can mind its own business," Clemence said. "Off that bench. Go on."

Lucie did not move. She had her mother's fast face, all alertness and no patience for adult theater. "Will it blow the wall if he brings it here?"

"Nobody is blowing any wall," Lucien said.

Ferrand laughed. "There. That's exactly the trouble. Nobody is blowing any wall, nobody is breaking any gate, nobody is making the bastards feel the week in their bones. We print, we whisper, we pass a hat for rent, and the Prefecture sleeps like a baby."

Nadir flicked the paper crane once so that it turned and settled again. "The Prefecture has cells full of men who did not find it sleepy."

"Cells full of men who got taken one by one," Ferrand shot back. "That's what comes of being worthy. They pick you off tidy as laundry off a line."

Clemence's needle stopped. "If you've got something to say about laundry, you can earn the right with your own shirts first."

Abel smiled at that, quick and unwilling, then checked himself because Ferrand was watching. Elise set down the basket and kept her face as dull as the widow's should have been. The note in her cuff seemed to warm against her skin. Names. Means. Place. Vautrin wanted a usable account before the city filled with bodies and banners. What he wanted from her was shape. Not rumor. Not fear. Something the newspapers could be fed.

Ferrand was trying to give him exactly that.

Or trying to give the room the idea of it.

Elise sat on the bench by Lucie. The child smelled of chalk and onion skin. Someone had mended one of her stockings with thread too dark to match. Elise kept her hands still in her lap and looked at Ferrand as if she were only another frightened listener.

"You say telegraph junction like it's a loaf," Lucien said. "Which junction. With what powder. For what after. Who is fed by it, who is housed by it, who gets out when the district is closed and every rooming house searched. You speak as if consequence were a door that only opens one way."

Ferrand gave him a patient look so false it almost had polish. "Always the schoolmaster's questions. With enough powder, any junction. With enough nerve, any after."

"With enough boys, you mean," Nadir said. "Boys who can be buried cheap."

Abel flushed at once. "I'm not a boy."

"No," Clemence said. "You're a meal and a pair of boots I haven't finished saving from ruin."

Ferrand bent, scooped up his satchel, and dropped it onto the crate with a weight that made Lucie flinch. "Would you all like proof that I'm not talking out of my arse?"

Lucien's voice changed then. It did not rise. It went flatter, which was worse. "No."

Ferrand had already opened the flap.

Inside, under a rag and two folded sheets, lay a coil of green fuse wire, a paper-wrapped cylinder the length of Elise's hand, and a pistol so small it looked mean rather than impressive, a pocket thing for a close hallway and one frightened face. Abel stared at it with the pure hunger of the young, not for blood exactly but for the clean hard glamour of objects that decide a life in a second. Lucie's mouth opened. Clemence was on her feet before the child could speak.

"Are you out of your fucking mind?" she said.

Ferrand held up his free hand. "Easy. It's safe enough sitting still."

"So is a stove until a child falls on it. Shut that bag."

Lucien had not moved from his chair. Only his left hand had gone white on the edge of the table. "You'll take that out of this room."

"This room is why I'm here," Ferrand said. "You think I like carrying merchandise for the pleasure of it? The law is already written. The papers already call every one of us bomb-men. If they're going to make a story from our names, at least let it cost them."

Lucien coughed once, caught it back, and said, "The law is written. That part is true. Which is why I won't help them finish the paragraph."

Ferrand snorted. "By feeding widows? By teaching letters on a chalk wall while they widen the prison yard? Come off it."

"By keeping people alive long enough to outlast men's appetite for panic," Lucien said. "By making sure the women thrown from one room have another room. By printing bail appeals. By teaching a child to read the notice that cheats her mother. You call that small because it does not throw sparks. The state does not call it small. That's why it keeps sending men after us."

Abel had heard the speech before in different pieces. Elise saw the moment it failed him anyway. He shifted his weight, jaw working, eyes on the pistol again. He wanted Lucien to sound older and harder than the room had made him. He wanted Ferrand's cheap promises to be beneath him. But he was nineteen and tired of setting type for appeals nobody answered.

"And if outlasting isn't enough?" Abel said. "If all we do is make ourselves easier to count?"

Lucien turned to him. "Then we still have not improved matters by helping them slaughter a crowd."

"Who said crowd?" Ferrand asked lightly.

Nadir took the answer before anyone else could. "You did, when you said Thursday was the point."

Ferrand flashed his teeth. "No, friend, I said Thursday was cover. There's a difference."

"A difference visible from a hanging rope, perhaps."

Abel rounded on Nadir. "You always do that. You say one cutting thing and stand back as if you've built an argument."

"If I wanted to build an argument, I would ask him what charge he plans, what distance, what route out, and who taught him enough chemistry to keep from blowing his own hand off first."

Ferrand lifted the paper-wrapped cylinder. "You don't need chemistry to frighten a horse."

"No," Clemence said. "You need a street full of workers and one panic and then I'm washing brains out of coats till Pentecost."

Elise felt chalk grit under her palm from the bench. The room had narrowed around the satchel. It was not the bomb itself that did it, not yet. It was the way every eye had found its own use for the thing. Ferrand saw leverage. Abel saw vocation. Clemence saw blood in hems and on cuffs. Lucien saw the state with a gift in its hand. Lucie saw only the size of the blast against the wall where she practiced her letters. Elise saw Vautrin's clean cuffs, the neat office on the Ile de la Cite, the satisfaction with which he would slide an object like that across his desk and call it inevitability.

She made herself speak in the tone Jeanne Morel had built for the room: useful, careful, no edge exposed.

"If there is already talk outside," she said, "could it be police talk first? A rumor they want men to lean toward?"

Ferrand looked at her with quick interest. "You think they need to invent us?"

"I think they like saving themselves trouble."

"There," Lucien said softly. "There is the question."

Ferrand closed the satchel with one slap, but too late. The room had seen enough. "You all think like clerks. Manufactured, not manufactured, rumor, counter-rumor. While you sort your little drawers, the police sort theirs. They don't care whether the first whisper came from them or from us. They care whether, by Friday, the city remembers fear."

"The city remembers hunger more faithfully than fear," Lucien said. "Fear turns into headlines. Hunger sits down at your table."

"Only if you've got a table."

"Then build one."

Ferrand laughed outright. "With what wood?"

"With time," Lucien said. "With labor. With favors owed and repaid. With rooms that stay open. With women who know which landlord will wait three days. With men who can read a warrant. With a printer who works fast and keeps his mouth shut. That's how poor people survive the Republic."

He said it without grandeur. That was what gave it force. There was chalk on his sleeve, a cough he could not hide much longer, and no romance in the shape of him at all. Elise felt the room move a little toward him, not with applause, not with relief, but the way a bench settles back when the heavier body leaves it.

Ferrand saw it too. He moved at once, charm turning oily with urgency.

"Survive?" he said. "That's a priest's word for people too beaten to answer back. What do they remember now, Lucien? Tell the truth. They remember Vaillant because he made their deputies hear the noise in their own bones. They remember Henry because rich men finally understood a cafe could belong to anybody's grief. They don't remember the committees that passed the hat afterward."

Lucie whispered, mostly to herself, "I remember the hat."

Clemence's face changed for one second, as if someone had put a thumb into a bruise. The hat was a real hat, Elise knew, the one that had gone around after Lucie's father died under a cart axle and bought them two months in their room before the landlord started circling again. Ferrand had not known. That was the measure of him.

Lucien knew it too. He glanced at Lucie, then back at Ferrand. "Exactly," he said.

Abel stepped in before the silence could settle against Ferrand. "That's not fair."

Nobody answered. That made him speak faster.

"It's not fair to use her for this. We all know what he means. A hat keeps a room for a month. A pamphlet buys a day if it sells. Then what? Another room lost. Another arrest. Another speech about patience while the men with clubs write the future for us." His hands were shaking a little, whether from anger or the nearness of the satchel Elise could not tell. He hid it by grabbing the back of the crate. "Ferrand's right about one thing. They already call us monsters. They already search on rumor. They already make examples where there is nothing to prove. So what are we preserving? Respectability? It hasn't done much for us."

Lucien let him finish. That was his mistake, or perhaps his only chance. The room leaned toward the younger man now because youth did that to rooms, especially when youth was willing to burn itself where older people had learned the exact price of fire.

"We're preserving each other," Lucien said.

Abel's laugh came out raw. "That's a phrase, not an answer."

"It's the only answer that gets a second day."

"No," Abel said, and now he was looking straight at Ferrand as if the older man's approval had already become a kind of heat. "No. It's what people say after they've forgotten how to strike. You keep asking for names and routes and who gets out after. Fine. Ask me then. If there is a chance to hit them and not just beg to remain in one piece, I'm with him."

The words landed harder than the satchel had.

Clemence set the stocking down. "Abel."

But he was past hearing his own name in her mouth. Shame had made him brighter. Elise had seen boys do it in the cells beneath the Prefecture, talk themselves into courage because the quieter thing would be to admit they were frightened and small.

"I mean it," he said. "If hands are needed, mine are there."

Lucien's face did not break. Only the pause after Abel's last word showed how far it had gone in. He looked not at Ferrand but at the burns on Abel's fingers, at the wrists too thin for the coat, at the boy he had been trying to tutor past hunger and vanity both.

"Then your hands are promised to foolishness," he said. "And I'll say so in front of you, not behind your back."

Ferrand's grin vanished. Contempt showed clean at last. "Always this. If a younger man wants more than old men's caution, he must be vain, must be foolish, must be handled like crockery. Perhaps that's why he's tired of your school."

Nadir pushed off the doorway. The paper crane dropped to the floor between his boots. "Careful," he said. "You're beginning to sound as if you need him stupid."

For one second Elise thought Ferrand might swing at him. The badly healed knuckles on his right hand twitched. Instead he laughed again, shorter this time.

"I need nothing from any man who has not offered it," he said. "But I won't apologize for wanting spine in a room full of menders."

"Menders keep people alive," Clemence said. "And if you ever bring powder in here again, I'll put you out myself, spine or no spine."

Ferrand tipped two fingers to his brow in a mock salute. "Then I stand corrected. The republic will tremble before your wash paddle."

It was Lucien who stood then, finally. Not quickly. He set both palms on the table and rose with the care of a man whose lungs had begun bargaining with him. When he was upright the room altered again. Even Ferrand felt it enough to shut his mouth.

"Listen to me," Lucien said.

He did not raise his voice. He never had to. Carriage thunder passed somewhere overhead, dull through the stone like distant guns under mud.

"The state wants a pageant," he said. "It wants one broken body in the right street so it can call every hungry man a conspirator and every poor woman who lets him through the door an accomplice. It wants children in the papers as proof that corruption runs under the city like sewage. It wants panic because panic is cheap. We're expensive. Bread is expensive. Hiding men is expensive. Teaching a child her letters when half the district says she ought to go blind and obedient into the wash is expensive. That's why I spend myself there."

He put a hand flat on the chalk-marked wall beside Lucie's half-finished letters.

"If any man here wants to die in a blast, I can't stop him once he leaves my sight. But he won't call it strategy in this room. He won't call it care. He won't borrow the hunger of the people who sleep here and spend it on spectacle. Not while I'm standing."

Silence followed, but not agreement. Ferrand's face had gone still in the ugly way of a man who has misjudged how much charm the room would forgive. Abel stared at the wall rather than at Lucien. Nadir bent and picked up the paper crane, smoothed one wing with his thumb, and tucked it into his pocket. Clemence drew Lucie to her by the shoulder, needle still threaded between her teeth.

Elise knew then what she had come to know.

The threat was real in the sense that there was powder in the satchel and a boy willing to carry it. But it was being made in front of her too, coaxed and flattered into shape by Ferrand's appetite for a clean dramatic answer. There had not been a plot waiting in the quarry like a loaded gun. There was a room full of tired people, two children asleep in the next gallery, coal tabs unpaid, a cough that would worsen before summer, and one man trying to turn all of it into a headline with a fuse attached.

Ferrand broke the silence first because men like him always did.

"Fine," he said. "Keep your room pure. I know where to find volunteers who don't need blessing before they act."

Abel looked up at once. Lucien saw it. So did Elise.

"You won't recruit in my refuge," Lucien said.

"Then perhaps your refuge is smaller than you think."

He snatched up the satchel. For a moment Elise thought he would leave. Instead he swung the bag onto his shoulder and smiled directly at Abel, not even pretending subtlety now.

"Come have a cigarette later if you like," he said. "You can tell me whether you want your hands wasted on type for the rest of your life."

Abel did not answer. That was answer enough.

Ferrand ducked out through the narrow passage, carrying his cologne and his bad idea with him. The air in the schoolroom seemed dirtier after he had gone. Lucien coughed at last, a deep tearing thing he had held off too long. Clemence swore under her breath and shoved the mug of cold chicory toward him. Nadir went to the lamp and turned the wick lower as if less light might calm the room.

Lucie, who had hidden her face in Clemence's skirt for exactly three heartbeats and no more, said, "Will Thursday be bad?"

Clemence smoothed the child's braid with the same rough hand she used to knead bread. "Thursday will be Thursday. Hold still."

Lucien drank the chicory, grimaced, and wiped his mouth. "Thursday will be crowded," he said to Lucie. "That's different from bad."

"My shoe leaks in crowds."

That made Nadir laugh once, quietly, because it was the truest thing anyone had said all evening. Even Lucien's mouth moved. Abel did not laugh. He stood in the passage Ferrand had taken, caught between shame and longing so nakedly that Elise had to look away from him to keep from showing she saw.

Clemence handed Lucie the basket of onions. "Take those to the ration room and don't drop them."

Lucie made a face. "They smell."

"That's how you know they're ours."

The child went, carrying the basket against her narrow ribs. Elise rose before she had quite decided to. "I'll help her."

Clemence nodded without interest. She was watching Abel now.

The ration room lay beyond the sleeping gallery in a low chamber where shelves had been hacked straight into the limestone. Bread sat under cloths. Soap bars, wrapped in old newsprint, were stacked beside lamp oil and sacks of flour. The air held a permanent arithmetic: starch, grease, damp burlap, the mouse-sharp smell of stores counted too often because there were never enough of them. Lucie set down the basket and immediately began sorting onions by size as if she had invented the job.

"This one is rotten," she said.

"That one is half rotten," Elise said. "That's a different luxury."

Lucie gave her a suspicious look, then decided the line was worth keeping. "Half rotten," she repeated, pleased. "Like Ferrand's moustache."

Elise almost smiled. "Did you notice that yourself?"

"I notice lots of things. Mama says not to say them all."

"That's wise of her."

Lucie held up an onion the size of her fist. "Will there be marchers with red flowers?"

"Probably."

"Do they get to keep them if the police hit them?"

Elise thought of Vautrin's note, the way he had reduced a city to names and means and place. She thought of the satchel on Ferrand's knee, Abel's face turned toward it as if toward a lit stage.

"Sometimes," she said. "Go wash your hands. You've got onion on them now."

Lucie went with exaggerated martyrdom, muttering to herself about onions and Thursdays. When the chamber was hers alone, Elise reached for the household ledger out of habit more than plan. Clemence kept it on the middle shelf between the lamp oil and the beans, wrapped in a square of apron cloth. The book was there, blunt and swollen, its cover dark from old kitchen steam.

But another book sat behind it, narrower, brown-backed, the sort stationers sold cheap to men who wanted accounts to look respectable.

Elise drew it out.

At once she saw it was not Clemence's. Clemence wrote like she chopped roots: straight down, no ornament, numbers larger than words. This book held several hands. Nadir's neat compositor's script listed paper stock, ink, a hidden bundle of pamphlets above a cooper's shed on rue d'Alesia. Clemence's figures marked soap, beans, bread, lamp oil, one child's winter shawl paid in two parts. Then the later pages changed. The handwriting loosened, swaggering even in ink, letters tilting as if drunk. A different hand had added items the others had never needed to name together. Fuse, two lengths. Caps, six. Pistol, pocket. Powder parcel at Plantes cupboard. Spare room key, cabaret back stair. Thursday penciled twice in the margin and scratched over once, as if secrecy could be improved by indecision.

It was not a bomb plot written cleanly out. It was worse. It was the quarry's ordinary furtive life, the hidden bread and oil and forged papers that kept people housed, cut through now by Ferrand's additions like dirty water entering milk.

Elise turned one more page.

There, in the same loose hand, was a line that made the back of her neck go cold for reasons the quarry itself supplied. Not fear in the abstract. Not some floating pulse. The ledger edge bit her thumb. Damp from the wall found the opening at her collar. Somewhere nearby a drip hit stone with patient, counting regularity.

For review route.

No place named. No target either. Just the phrase, smug in its vagueness, as if any public crush could be enough if it gave the police something to print.

Vautrin could make a case from this book in an hour.

He could raid every cache named in the earlier pages, lift women for storing soap and blankets, take the cooper, the cabaret girl with the back stair key, whatever old printer had agreed to hide paper under his floorboards. He could point to the fuse and the pistol and tell a magistrate the rest was context. He would not be wrong by the standards that paid him.

And Lucien, if shown the book tonight, would see at last how far Ferrand had already put his hands into the quarry's life.

That should have made the decision simple. It did not.

The ledger was proof against the refuge, yes, but it was proof for Lucien too. Proof that Ferrand's talk had not been merely talk. Proof that Abel had been courted with real things. If Elise removed it from the room for Vautrin, she delivered the quarry's throat into the state's hand. If she put it in Lucien's palm now, she started a war inside the refuge before anyone was ready to win it. Abel would lie. Ferrand would vanish. Clemence would count losses while the police closed in. And if the wrong person saw Elise carrying it between chambers, her usefulness down here would shrink to nothing before dawn.

The twenty-eight francs for Charenton came to her with contemptible speed. One book against twenty-eight francs a month. Against her mother's blankets. Against the thin broth the attendants skimmed less when payments arrived on time. Vautrin had never needed to shout. He only had to keep accounts.

Footsteps sounded in the sleeping gallery.

Elise slid the brown ledger under her apron just as Lucie came back in drying her hands on her skirt.

"Mama says where are the small onions," the girl announced.

"In the basket."

"Not those. The smaller small ones."

Elise pointed. Lucie dug through the pile, found three the size of plums, and held them up in triumph. "These are for flavor only."

"That's a sentence Lucien taught you."

"He says flavor matters even when there isn't enough."

The girl's pride in repeating it made something mean and practical in Elise recoil. A child should not have to defend the existence of flavor. Not in a cellar, not to the state, not to a ledger full of hidden bread and bomb caps written in the same ink.

She said, "Take them to your mother."

When Lucie had gone, Elise waited long enough to hear Clemence answer from the schoolroom. Then she wrapped the narrow ledger in the apron cloth from the household book and slipped out the back way, not toward the entrance but deeper, where the quarry narrowed and old work marks survived on the walls like ghosts of arithmetic.

She knew a dry pocket three turns beyond the disused cistern. Lucien had once sent her there for kindling and told her to mind the floor where the stone broke sharp under the dust. It was not on the household routes children used. It was not quite on the map she had memorized either, only on the quarry itself, which was safer.

Her lamp made the chalk flare white and then yellow. Water ticked somewhere behind the wall. At one bend she had to turn sideways to pass, ledger pressed against her ribs, the stone grazing the burn scar inside her wrist through the damp sleeve. The quarry had a way of forcing every choice into the body. There was no clean thinking in it. A person carried what she meant to hide. A person scraped herself doing it.

Voices came from ahead.

Elise put out the lamp with her fingers and flattened herself into the dark recess before she had finished deciding.

Ferrand first. She knew him now by his easy breathing, his confidence in spaces he had not earned. Abel with him, quicker breath, boot sole catching once on loose grit and then pretending it had not.

"I'm not saying tomorrow," Ferrand was murmuring. "I'm saying you don't let an old man make a virtue of delay till the whole city has forgotten your dead."

"He's not old."

"He's old where it counts."

Abel said nothing.

Ferrand went on, voice lower, coaxing. "You saw their faces. Half the room's afraid of a noise before it's even made. Fine. Let them sew and stir soup. But if you want to matter, if you're tired of setting righteous little notices while they drag men off anyway, come have a look at the proper tools. You've got good hands. Quick hands."

"I know my hands."

"Then prove it to yourself."

They passed so near Elise could smell the cheap cologne again, sweet over the rank damp of the passage. She kept still until the sound of them thinned into the quarry's breathing. Only then did she relight the lamp. The match shook once against the box, not from some floating terror but because limestone grit had worked into the sweat on her fingers and made the wood slip.

The niche stood where she remembered, shoulder-high in the wall, a cut made long ago for a work lamp or a saint nobody had bothered to keep holy. A slab of broken limestone leaned against it. Elise set down her lamp, shifted the slab, and felt cold chalk mud under her nails.

She could still turn back.

She could take the ledger to the dead drop before dawn and buy herself another month of usefulness, another month of her mother's bed kept under her. She could bring it to Lucien and watch the room split open before Ferrand had time to bury the evidence. She could even burn it, though that would be the stupidest version of mercy, proof lost to everybody and danger left loose in men's pockets.

Instead she slid the wrapped ledger into the niche until her fingers could barely catch the cloth. She pushed it farther with two knuckles. The stone drank the sound. Then she set the slab back in place and rubbed chalk over the cleaner edge until it looked as old as the wall.

When she straightened, lamp smoke moved across the passage in a thin black seam.

That was the moment it became something more than omission.

Until now she had left things out. She had thinned reports, softened edges, kept Clemence and the child off paper because the alternative had turned her stomach. This was different. Vautrin had asked for fact, and she had put her hand directly over it. Not because the quarry was innocent. It was not. There was powder now, and a pistol, and a boy being led toward both by the need to feel large in history before history crushed him flat. Not because Lucien was right in every particular either. The refuge had its vanity as well as its care. But because the book in that niche contained bread beside fuse, shelter beside theater, and Vautrin would flatten all of it into the same charge without losing a minute's sleep.

Elise picked up the lamp and started back.

At the last turn before the sleeping gallery she could hear Lucien again, quieter now, reading something aloud to Lucie because the child would not sleep after the quarrel. His voice came braided with coughs and patience. Clemence interrupted now and then to correct a hem or a fact. Somewhere beyond them Abel was either with Ferrand or thinking about him. The choice in the wall sat between all those sounds like another hidden mouth.

When Elise reached the schoolroom, nobody looked up at once. That spared her.

She set the lamp down by the onions and said, "The small ones were under the basket."

Clemence grunted.

Lucie held out a scrap of paper on which she had written her own name badly and with full seriousness. Lucien was helping her shape the last letter with one ink-dark finger. Elise looked at the child's bent head, at the chalk wall, at the stocking Clemence had gone back to darning, at the empty patch of floor where Ferrand's satchel had rested.

Vautrin would get no ledger from her in the morning.

He would get something thinner, if he got anything at all.

And if he noticed the difference, if he put his neat hands on the missing weight and followed the absence back to her, then the cost had already begun. The book was in the wall. Abel was in Ferrand's reach. Thursday was coming whether she fed the machine or not.

She sat down beside Lucie and took up the needle Clemence had left on the bench, as if mending a stocking were the natural work of her hands.


# Chapter 6

By the time they hauled the first baskets out of the wine shop cellar, the morning had turned warm enough to wake the sourness in the Bièvre. The smell struck Elise before the light did: dye, horse piss, rotted greens, the mineral bite of lye. Then the sky opened over the yard and laid itself across her face so abruptly that she had to blink like a woman brought up from fever. Behind her the trapdoor thudded shut over the quarry's chalk cold. Ahead, steam lifted off the vats and drifted low under the young leaves, turning the whole laundry yard into something half weather, half workshop.

Clemence moved into it as if she had title to the place. Her hair was pinned under the same faded red kerchief she kept darning instead of replacing, and the right pocket of her apron clicked softly with the horn buttons she cut from shirts too far gone to save. She never threw out good buttons. Cloth died, seams burst, men vanished, but a button could still earn its keep. She dropped one basket by the trough, jerked another toward Elise with her foot, and said, "If you can carry paper, you can carry wet linen."

"That seems to be your answer to most questions," Elise said.

"It keeps the day moving."

Lucie came behind them with clothespins in a flattened sardine tin, shaking it just enough to hear the rattle. She had escaped the comb halfway through the second braid, so the left side of her head was neat and the right had already started its rebellion. There was a smooth white quarry pebble in her apron pocket. Elise had seen her thumb it at night when adults talked too low and too long. This morning the child was cheerful enough to forget it.

"Mama says Monsieur Delatour carries like a martyr," Lucie announced, "and wrings like a priest."

Lucien, coming through the gate with the next basket braced on one shoulder, said, "That's slander in two professions at once."

"No," Clemence said. "That's economy."

He laughed and set the basket down. In the yard, without a chalk wall at his back or a meeting pressing toward quarrel, he looked less like a figure someone might follow and more like a lean middle-aged man who had slept too little and still come to lift what needed lifting. His sleeves were rolled past the wrist. The skin of his forearms was quarry pale where the sun had not had it in months, freckled darker where it had. He tested the first length of clothesline with a quick pluck of one finger before trusting it with the weight, a habit so needless and so serious that Lucie immediately mimicked it on the lower line and grinned when it twanged.

Elise had meant to use the morning. Aboveground, away from the quarry's close passages and shared lamps, people let themselves spread. They forgot to watch where their voice carried. They stepped out of formation because there was no formation to keep. If Lucien had another face, a more dangerous one, she wanted it in daylight.

What she had not counted on was the work. Dirty sheets from the sleeping gallery were heavy as sodden sacks. Shirts stuck together at the cuffs with dried soap, chalk, and the human grease of too many nights in bad air. By the time she had sloshed the first load through hot water and lye, her skirts were damp to the knee and the old burn on the inside of her left wrist had started to sting. The pain was precise enough to be useful. It kept her hands honest.

Clemence stationed herself by the main trough and ran the yard like a quartermaster disguised as a laundress. She did not raise her voice unless someone wasted motion. "Not that vat, Jeanne, this one. That one is for the shirts going back to the Duprets' boarders. If you mix them, I'll hear about it till Pentecost. Lucie, pins go in the mouth or in the tin, nowhere else. I'm not fishing wood out of your gut for the sake of laziness. Lucien, stop trying to murder that sheet. Twist from the seam."

"I'm twisting from the seam."

"You're twisting from vanity."

Lucie cackled. Elise turned the sheet and felt Lucien take the other end. Together they wrung. The linen fought them, then gave all at once, cold water streaming over their knuckles. His grip was strong, but the left side yielded a fraction late, not weakness exactly, more an old injury remembered by the muscle before the mind admitted it. When he shifted, the collar of his shirt pulled open at the shoulder and she saw the pale hooked edge of scar tissue high against the bone before the cloth settled again.

"You see?" Clemence said. "You have one good shoulder and you insist on behaving as if Providence owes you two."

"If Providence owed me anything," Lucien said, "it would start with drier blankets."

"Get in line."

The line of other women at the yard looked over sometimes, curious at the man in shirtsleeves or at Lucie running pegs from basket to basket like a messenger in a very domestic war, but Clemence had the blunt competence that made strangers mind their own soap. She nodded to those she knew, paid for hot water with exact coins, and ignored the rest. Her authority here did not come from secrecy or politics. It came from the fact that she could get twenty stained shirts white enough to satisfy a clerk's wife and still have strength left to drag home the bedding of a hidden household.

Elise worked beside her and let the rhythm take over: soak, soap, beat, rinse, wring, lift. At intervals the sky went white in the vats, then broke into ragged strips as cloth came up dripping. The Bièvre slid past on the far side of the yard, thick and mean-looking, carrying scraps of color from the dye works downstream. Somewhere beyond the wall a carter cursed at his horse. Somewhere closer a woman began one verse of a song and forgot the rest. Under it all ran the slap of linen against plank and the wet squeak of rope tightening under load.

She watched Lucien while pretending to watch the line. He did not perform usefulness. That was the first thing. Men who wanted authority usually made a theater of small labor. They carried one bucket where two were needed or took the heaviest object when women were watching and then stood around breathing like saints in paintings. Lucien did what was at hand and submitted to correction without ceremony. When Clemence shoved a bar of soap at him and told him to scrub collars instead of discoursing, he scrubbed collars. When Lucie pinned a clothespin to the back of his cuff and said she was improving him, he left it there until she forgot about it herself.

Once, bending for another shirt, Elise caught herself smiling before she had decided the expression was safe.

That annoyed her enough to make her careless.

Clemence handed over a tin cup of bluing and said, "Easy with it. Poor cloth only needs the idea of luxury."

"That was my mother's rule," Elise said, because it was the kind of answer the morning invited, because everyone else had been speaking in remembered instructions and inherited economies, because for one soft second it felt easier to step where ordinary women stepped than to measure every word.

Lucie looked up from sorting pegs by size. "What did she call it?"

Elise had been watching the blue open in the water like ink in a glass. "What?"

"Not the bluing. The rule."

Clemence snorted. "Since when do rules have names?"

"Yours do," Lucie said. "You call them common sense when you like them and foolishness when somebody else says them first."

Lucien laughed outright at that. Elise did too, half a beat late. Then all three of them were looking at her, not suspicious, not even intent, only waiting for the sort of answer families and workyards waited for without thinking.

There should have been one. Jeanne Morel ought to have had a mother who washed, and that mother ought to have had sayings, and those sayings ought to have come up easy as breath after an hour among vats. Elise could invent a widow on command for Vautrin and make a magistrate believe her. It was this that thinned her out: not official questions, but the little domestic ones that came padded with laughter.

"She called it cheating the eye," Elise said at last. "Said poor people had to help clean things pretend."

"That's not bad," Clemence said.

"Where was that?" Lucie asked. "The place with your mother."

"Near Chartres."

Lucie frowned. "I thought you said it was at Montrouge."

The cup slipped in Elise's wet hand and tapped the side of the vat. Not enough to spill, enough to show she had heard.

Before she could answer, Clemence said, "Montrouge was the room after, idiot child. People move."

"I know people move." Lucie returned to her pegs with offended dignity. "I only asked."

"And if you keep asking every damn thing, one day somebody will charge by the question."

"You'd still ask them to explain the price."

Clemence gave Lucie a look that was almost a smile if you knew where to search for it. The moment dissolved. Work resumed. Yet Elise had felt it happen, a tiny seam pulling under the fingers. Lucien had not said anything. That was worse.

By noon the first lines were crowded with shirts, petticoats, child stockings, dishcloths gone nearly transparent at the fold, and the long white sheets from the sleeping gallery lifting and falling in the mild wind like signal flags nobody had agreed on. Heat rose from the stones. The steam thinned. Lucie was sent to fetch bread from a woman who kept a basket at the gate. She came back with a heel under each arm and a radish stolen or donated from somewhere along the way, dirt still at the root.

They ate standing because sitting down made it harder to rise again. Clemence wiped her hands on her apron, split the bread with a thumbnail, and handed the larger piece to Lucie without appearing to notice that she had done it. Elise bit into hers and tasted yeast, grit, and the clean metallic tang of the tin knife Lucien used to cut the cheese. Nearby, two laundresses argued about whether a client had shrunk a shawl with cheap soap and was now pretending it arrived that way. A dog wandered through on three good legs and received the radish greens from Lucie as if he had earned them.

"You laugh differently up here," Lucien said.

He said it quietly, almost to the bread, but Elise heard the placement of the sentence. Not flirtation. Observation.

"Do I?"

"Less politely."

"That sounds like criticism."

"It was meant as relief."

Clemence, close enough to hear and wise enough not to join, took the knife from Lucien and cut another sliver of cheese with savage neatness. Lucie was talking to the dog about martyrdom, having decided it needed a political education. The dog seemed unimpressed.

Elise brushed crumbs from her palm. "Perhaps the air improves me."

"The smell should prevent that."

"Then perhaps the labor."

"That I might believe." He looked at the wet cuff of her sleeve, the reddening skin at the wrist where lye had found the old burn. "Though the labor isn't doing you favors yet."

"I've survived worse employers than soap."

He inclined his head, accepting the edge without trying to blunt it. "I'm sure you have."

Again that soft exactness. He never pounced. He let a sentence rest where it landed and made her hear the shape of it for herself.

After they ate, Clemence packed Lucie and the first dry load toward the street that would take them back to the wine shop. "We're not making three trips if two will do," she said. "Delatour, leave the linens on the far line for another quarter hour unless rain comes. Jeanne, bring the pegs. And if either of you lets dust get on those sheets, I'll know and I'll make my displeasure tedious."

"That threat has depth," Lucien said.

"It has experience."

Lucie lifted the sardine tin in salute. "If rain comes, I'll tell the sky it's on your list."

"Tell it to pay what it owes first."

The gate shut behind them. The yard felt larger for the absence of the child and smaller for the absence of Clemence's voice. A little breeze came off the river, enough to set the sheet edges tapping against one another. Somewhere down the wall a woman started laughing hard enough to cough.

Elise gathered the empty cups and carried them to the water barrel. She could feel Lucien's attention without looking at him. It was not the heat of being stared at. It was cooler than that and harder to shake, like standing near a door you knew was open.

"You don't have to tell me where your mother washed," he said at last.

Elise set the cups down. "I didn't realize there'd be an examination."

"There's not."

"No?"

"No. Only a difference between being asked and being heard."

She turned then. He was stripping the dry corners from a line, folding the sheet inward over his forearms to keep it off the ground. His face was shaded, the light behind him. He looked more tired now that the laughter had gone out of the yard, older too, though not in any theatrical way. Just a man past forty with too much damp in his lungs and too many people depending on him to indulge vanity for long.

"And what exactly have you heard?" Elise asked.

"That the town changes when you're tired. That your dead husband changes trade according to what's needed in the moment. That widowhood stays because it answers questions before anyone asks the next one."

The dryness in her mouth had nothing to do with soap. "You've been keeping account."

"Of you? A little. I was a schoolmaster. It's an old sickness."

"A useful one for a man in hiding."

"Sometimes."

She waited for accusation, for demand, for the practiced softness men used when they thought a confession was near. He shook out the sheet once, clipped it at the corner, and only then looked at her full.

"I'm not asking who you are," he said. "If I meant to drag truth out of people, there are coarser ways and faster ones. I'm asking whether the question under all your questions is the same one you've been carrying since you came."

"Which question is that?"

"What the quarry is really for."

Elise folded her arms, then unfolded them when she realized the gesture made her feel like an arrested clerk. "And if it is?"

"Then I'll answer it."

He said it as simply as that. Not generous. Not self-sacrificing. Simply tired of being measured by rumors and by boys who wanted thunder more than shelter.

Elise bent to the basket between them and lifted the next sheet because work made conversation harder to lie in. "Answer it, then."

Lucien took the opposite end. They snapped the cloth once, sending a mist of river water and sun into the air between them.

"The first people below were not militants," he said. "Not in the way the Prefecture means the word, anyway. They were a woman put out of her room in November because the landlord had seen police on the stairs twice and decided panic was cheaper than decency, her little boy with a cough that never left him, and a compositor just out of prison who had nowhere to go because his sister's landlord had got nervous too. We found space for them under a cooper's shed for six nights. On the seventh the owner wanted his floor back."

He moved down the line as he spoke, clipping one corner, then the next.

"A week later two wives came back from visiting day with blisters through their stockings and no money for another bed. One of them had kept a linen tube of survey papers after her husband died because she could not bear to sell the last thing that had his hand on it. Old quarry maps. Municipal work. Worthless to anyone who wanted a proper life and useful to anyone who did not have one. There were galleries marked on them that the city had forgotten to fear."

Elise watched his fingers on the clothespins. He set each one squarely, not hurrying, not drifting.

"So you went exploring."

"Cautiously. With candles, bad boots, and more self-importance than sense the first night. The second night there was less self-importance because the floor sweated chalk, the roof sounded alive over us, and one of the children started crying from the cold. After that it was not romance. It was blankets, bread, dry corners, and keeping lamp oil from spilling. It was prison wives needing somewhere to wait till morning after the trains stopped. It was men coming out of cells with their papers stamped and their rooms already rented to someone cleaner. It was girls too old to sleep in alleys and too poor to rent anything with a bolt. It was Clemence counting soap. It was Nadir repairing type by lamplight because if the hand press stayed silent people went hungry in two neighborhoods instead of one."

He paused to cough into his wrist, turned his head, waited it out. The cough was drier aboveground but no prettier.

"People like Ferrand hear about a hidden place and imagine conspiracy first," he said when it passed. "That tells you more about them than about the place. A refuge is built from refusals. Refusal of the street. Refusal of the cell. Refusal of letting the state define every room a body can occupy."

"And the printing?"

"Necessary. Not pure." His mouth tightened a fraction. "Pamphlets raise money, warn people, make friends we haven't met yet. They also flatter men who like to hear their own certainty in public. I don't mistake one use for the other."

Elise shifted her grip on the sheet. Water had dried in salt at the base of her thumbs. She had wanted intention. Here it was, maddeningly unspectacular. Bread, blankets, lamp oil, prison wives, children old enough to need bolts on doors. Vautrin would call it infrastructure for sedition and not entirely be lying. What unsettled her was that Lucien would not entirely be lying either if he called it housekeeping.

"Then why keep Abel around Ferrand at all?" she asked. "If you can hear what men are made of, why let one of them teach the other how to want badly?"

Lucien gave her a look that admitted the justice of it before he answered. "Because people aren't laundry, Jeanne. They don't come marked for separate vats. Abel is nineteen. Nineteen is an age that thinks danger proves value. Ferrand knows how to feed that. So do newspapers. So does the Republic, when it needs examples. If I throw every reckless boy out the first time he mistakes noise for courage, I'm left with a cleaner conscience and a shorter list of living boys."

"That sounds tidy in theory."

"Nothing about it is tidy." He took the now-hung sheet from her hands and reached for the next. "You think I don't know the difference between shelter and theater. I know it too well. Theater is easier. Theater gives one night a shape. Shelter means the same labor tomorrow and the day after, and people still call it timidity because there is no smoke to point at."

The line of his shoulder under the shirt had gone taut with the effort of holding the basket steady against his hip. Elise caught it from him before he had to ask and set it on the stones between them.

"You say that as if you despise action."

"No. I despise vanity with detonators."

There was humor in the sentence, but it came in tired clothing. Elise found herself answering in the same register without meaning to.

"A narrow distinction, some days."

"A necessary one."

For a moment neither of them moved. The yard had emptied further. The other laundresses were finishing their own loads or leaving with them. Sunlight came through the hanging sheets in blanched rectangles, whitening his face whenever he stepped behind one, then giving it back in shadow. Elise thought of the quarry's white wall, the chalk under everybody's nails, the hidden ledger wedged in stone because she had not been able to hand it over. She had told herself that concealment was tactical, that feeling could be deferred until usefulness was complete. Yet here he was, offering not seduction, not ideology, only the humiliatingly intimate fact of what the place had been built to keep alive.

"Why tell me this?" she asked.

He stooped for the last of the pillowcases. "Because you're already there."

"That's not an answer."

"It's the answer. You're sleeping under those blankets. Lucie is asking you questions. Clemence hands you the ration book without counting fingers after. You keep looking at the quarry as if you're waiting for it to declare its true profession. I'm telling you there's no hidden nobility under the stone. There's only what people hauled down because no one above meant to let them keep it."

He straightened. The pillowcase in his hand fluttered once in the breeze.

"And because," he added, "most of us arrived with some patched story or half name. Debt does that. Prison does that. Shame does that. I don't need a perfect account of your past to know whether you can carry a basket or keep watch by a sickbed. I only need to know whether the part of you that's here is here."

It was the mercy that undid her, not the suspicion.

If he had pressed, she would have found resistance. Resistance had shape. It had training behind it, and wages, and the hard monthly number from Charenton that sat in her head more firmly than prayer. This offered no surface to strike. It left a space open and trusted her not to poison it.

Her name rose before she could examine the impulse.

Elise.

Not Jeanne, not Mademoiselle Morel, not widow, not the useful paper woman Vautrin had hired and disguised and sent underground. Elise, compact and dark-haired and mean enough to take state money when it kept her mother in clean sheets. Elise, who had watched a courier taken because timing mattered more than pity. Elise, whose real work was folded into the hem of her dress and the back of her teeth.

The word came as far as her tongue. She could feel the first shape of it forming, the clean start of the vowel. For one astonished second she wanted to give it to him with no bargain attached, no calculation ahead of it, just the stupid human reflex of answering truth with truth when someone set something living in your hands.

Then the rest of her caught up.

Vautrin's office, Charenton's bills, the brass marker sewn into the hem, the ledger in the chalk niche, the whole machinery of consequence came back in one hard turn, and what she said instead was, "That's a remarkable amount of trust to extend to a woman you've just accused of lying."

Lucien clipped the last pillowcase to the line. "Not trust. Not entirely."

"No?"

"Call it measurement corrected by experience."

"That sounds very close to trust from where I'm standing."

"Then you're standing in a generous spot."

Despite herself, she laughed. It came out rougher than before, with less caution in it. He heard that too. Of course he did.

"You make everything sound less sentimental than it is," she said.

"An old pedagogical flaw."

"Another schoolmaster's sickness?"

"One of several."

He bent to lift the basket and she caught the other handle before he could take the full weight. The wicker was damp against her palm. They started toward the gate together, the basket swinging low between them, clean sheets piled high enough to show over the rim like surrender flags in a better republic.

At the threshold he stopped to let an older laundress pass out with a load on her back. He stepped aside without fuss, hatless, courteous, exactly the sort of man Vautrin would describe as dangerous because he made decent gestures look like habit instead of propaganda.

"You never answered your own question," Elise said when they were in the street. "What the quarry is for now."

He adjusted his grip. "Now? The same as before, with more trouble attached."

"And after that?"

He thought for a moment. Not posing. Thinking. "If you mean whether I believe mutual aid and lessons and bad pamphlets are enough to outlive this government, no. Governments outlive more honorable things than those."

"Then what is the point?"

"That children still need soup while history is being arrogant. That men coming out of prison still need a room before dawn. That a woman who's been turned out by a landlord ought to find a blanket before she finds philosophy. Those aren't final victories. They're still obligations."

He glanced at her, then back to the street.
