"And because every so often," he said, "keeping people alive long enough to think becomes its own offense. The state seems to understand that very well."

There was no grandeur in it. That was what made it hard to refuse. He spoke of soup, blankets, waiting till dawn. Not purity, not poster language, not the clean geometry of cause and effect Vautrin preferred in reports. Elise had spent weeks telling herself the quarry might yet reveal its central lie, the selfish engine under the talk of aid. Perhaps it still would. Ferrand existed. Abel could still be turned. Hidden rooms could become anything under pressure. But Lucien's intention, the one she had come up into daylight to read, was not obscure now. It was domestic to the point of scandal.

They turned into the yard behind the wine shop. Someone upstairs rolled a cask across the floor, the sound a deep wooden complaint. Below them waited the trapdoor, the chalk turns, the shared air. Lucie was already there with Clemence and the first load, kneeling on the stones to rescue a broken clothespin with a concentration most adults wasted on money. She looked up when they came through.

"You took forever," she said.

"We were slandered by work," Lucien told her.

"Only because you deserved it."

Clemence reached for the basket handle, then looked at Elise's face and looked away again too quickly to be innocence. "Get those in before the clean smell leaves them," she said. "I didn't boil half the yard for the quarry to sleep in river damp."

They lowered the basket through the trapdoor. Cool air rose to meet them, mineral and close, already beginning to eat the sunlight off the cloth. Elise bent for the next bundle and Lucien's hand brushed the inside of her wrist where the old burn scar lay. Not deliberate. Not entirely accidental either. Just one body correcting the swing of weight with another near it.

"Careful," he said quietly.

At the mouth of the descent, with Clemence ahead and Lucie clattering below and the clean linen smelling briefly of sun before the quarry took it back, Elise had to put her teeth into the inside of her cheek to keep her real name where it belonged.

When Lucie called up for Jeanne to mind the low step, the alias struck her not as protection but as theft.


# Chapter 7

The packet of notes rode flat and hot inside Clemence's stays. Elise could tell where it sat by the way Clemence kept one forearm folded over her middle as they walked, not modest and not delicate, simply protective in the same practical way she shielded bread from rain. The paper bundle in Nadir's arms had taken the shape of his body already. He carried it turned a little sideways, as if he were making room for a blow that had not yet arrived, and every few streets he checked the inside pocket of his coat with the back of his ink-black fingers. A corner of folded proof stuck there. Lucie had begged him for a fox before they left the quarry, and he had promised one with a seriousness he would have mocked in anybody else.

Ferrand had come because Ferrand came where movement and money met. He said it was because he knew the lane and because a man looked less suspicious than two women and a compositor carrying wrapped stock. Elise had not corrected the count. Ferrand always spoke as if the world were already arranged to flatter him. Cheap cologne leaked off him in warm bursts when the wind shifted, trying and failing to cover a night's sweat. He had shaved in a hurry. One patch under his jaw still showed the dark rasp, and the knuckles on his right hand were swollen old white where they had once broken and healed badly.

Late spring had softened the city without cleaning it. Cart ruts held old rain and horse piss. Laundry snapped from upper windows over a butcher's blue flies. They were south enough now that the streets widened and thinned in uncertain patches, Paris fraying into Montrouge with yards, workshops, and little plots where stubborn people kept onions in dirt the color of dishwater. Elise felt the hour in her body with the same trained certainty with which she felt the knife hidden in her left boot. Vautrin's slip had browned over a candle the night before and given her the courtesy of a time: after lamps, after the district was half dark, after the runner had had room to move the goods. It was why she had agreed to come. It was why she had not warned Lucien off the errand. There had been filth enough in that choice already. She had not expected Vautrin to take even the lie back.

"If this packet gets damp," Clemence said, "you can all line your shoes with it and call it a political victory."

Ferrand grinned at her. "You've a hymn ready for everything, haven't you?"

"It's not a hymn. It's arithmetic."

Nadir did not look at either of them. "Mind the curb," he said. "The bottom bundle is already bowing."

Ferrand spread one hand. "I said I'd carry it."

"You said it three streets ago," Nadir replied. "Talking isn't lifting."

Elise kept her eyes on the lane ahead. She was Jeanne Morel here, the useful widow with careful hands and no politics worth naming. Jeanne did not hurry when there was no reason to hurry. Jeanne noticed doorways because poor women noticed doorways. Jeanne did not count the men at the far corner by their boots and the way they had made themselves look occupied with nothing. Elise saw them anyway: one with a newspaper held too still, another pretending to study harness leather in a shop window, a third whose cuffs were cleaner than his cap. She told herself she was borrowing danger from habit. If Vautrin had moved the hour up, he would have had to move more than the hour. He would have had to risk her.

Then Ferrand lifted two fingers at a cooper's boy and the boy looked away too fast.

Elise felt her stomach go small and mean inside her.

The runner's room was on the second floor of a passage house behind a shutter-maker's yard. Reine Talbot opened the door with three dressmaker's pins between her lips and a tape measure still around her neck, as if the years since she had sewn for a living had not persuaded her to stop expecting cloth worth measuring. A child's jacket lay turned inside out on her table among drying broadsides. She had chalked one cuff for shortening. The room smelled of paste, lamp oil, and boiled chicory. Reine took the pins from her mouth and squinted at Clemence first, then at the bundle, then at Ferrand with the kind of smile women used when they had long ago given up mistaking appetite for charm.

"You took your time," she said. "I've got the stove lit for glue and three neighbors who think that means I'm taking in washing. If anybody asks, you're here to collect a shirt."

"Then show me a shirt," Clemence said.

Reine snorted and moved aside. "In the chair. Don't steal it. It's the only one in the room without holes."

Nadir brought the paper in and set it down with more care than some men used laying down a child. Reine's face changed when she saw the stock. Hunger came into it, not for food but for workable things. Good paper was not abstract in rooms like this. It was time, reach, possible voices, a week's worth of circulation that would not blur into pulp at the first touch of wet fingers.

"Well," she said softly, and then with briskness again, "Lucien's getting extravagant. What's he after, canonization?"

"He's after legible type," Nadir said. "Which is harder."

He slipped the folded fox from his pocket and set it on the mantel near Reine's tin cup. "For Lucie. I forgot."

Reine picked it up with ink-thick thumb and forefinger. "Her or me?"

"Her. You've already got enough vices."

"Unkind. I've got very few. I keep them in order."

Ferrand laughed too readily, as if he had said something clever himself. He moved to the window and looked down through the slat in the curtain. Elise watched the back of his neck. Men who risked arrest with conviction in them stood a certain way when they entered a room. They spread into it. They put some part of themselves between the room and harm without looking as if they meant to. Ferrand never did. He occupied a place only until the place asked anything of him.

Clemence drew the notes from inside her dress. The packet had left a damp square on her chemise. She flattened it on the table beside the chalked jacket and began counting in the low blunt school hand of her voice, every amount spoken as if it had been scrubbed first. Reine counted with her, lips moving. Elise kept half an eye on the money and half on the door. She had hidden her own slips deeper than the notes, stitched into the inner seam of her petticoat where a casual hand would find nothing but linen. Blank to any ordinary eye. Legible with heat. Damning if understood.

Too quiet.

The yard outside should have carried a saw's whine from the shutter-maker. A woman with a basket had been quarreling in the passage when they came up. Somebody's baby had been crying through the wall. Now the house held itself in. Elise heard only the glue in Reine's pot giving a small wet burp as it simmered.

Then the first blow landed downstairs.

Not a knock. Wood gave with the flat cracking report of a shoulder or a pry bar, and at once after it came the sound Elise knew too well: men on a stair trying not to sound like men on a stair. Weight kept to the edges of the boards. Breath saved. No wasted speech until they had the room.

After lamps, Vautrin had written. The bastard.

Ferrand turned from the window before anybody else moved. For one clean second his face lost its social expression and became only calculation. He did not look surprised. He looked inconvenienced.

"Back way," he said, already stepping toward the scullery door.

Reine stared at him. "What back way? That stair jams."

"Then unjam it."

He caught the door latch, shoved it once to see if it would give, and when it did he slipped through with his own hat in hand and nothing else. Not the money. Not the paper. Not the woman whose room he had just delivered into danger. Elise caught one last breath of his cologne and then he was gone down the service stair like grease poured down a crack.

Clemence's mouth opened, closed. "That son of a bitch."

The second blow struck their own landing. Someone shouted, "Police. Open."

Reine did the stupid brave thing people did in rooms that held their whole life. She reached for the notes first. Nadir reached for the paper. Clemence reached for the stove. Elise heard herself say, too fast and too certain, "Leave the front. They'll stack there first."

Clemence's head snapped toward her.

There was no time to fix it.

The door burst inward on the next hit, splinters spitting across the room. A plainclothes sergeant came through with one sleeve half torn from the shoulder seam and a wedding band worn pale into the flesh of his hand. Two men crowded behind him so close they jammed themselves in the frame for a beat, cursing and shoving. The room was too small for authority to look elegant in. Boots slid on paste. One man's club caught the edge of the table instead of a body and sent the counted notes skidding through Reine's chalk dust.

Reine snatched the child's jacket by mistake, realized it, and lunged again. The sergeant drove forward. His shoulder took her in the chest and flung her back against the stove. The glue pot came over. Thick paste hit the floor and her skirt together with a wet slap. She screamed once, more fury than pain, then the stove door sprang open and a length of iron kissed her calf red through the wool.

Nadir dropped to one knee to save the paper bundle. Elise saw the choice come into him. The stock was on the floor by his hands. Reine was pinned against the stove with the sergeant half over her and the second man raising his club. Nadir looked at the bundle, at Reine, and then at nothing at all. He went for the body.

"Move," he said, quiet as ever, and drove his shoulder into the policeman's waist.

The three of them went down in glue and ash. The club came down anyway. It caught Reine along the temple instead of on the arm she had thrown up to shield herself. Blood opened at her hairline in a sudden dark ribbon. Elise smelled hot iron, lamp smoke, and something rank from the kicked-over chamber pot under the bed. A sheet of broadside paper slid into it and bloomed brown.

Clemence seized the fallen stove door with the hem of her apron and slammed it shut before the coals could roll. One of the detectives grabbed at her wrist. She swung the iron hook she had snatched from beside the grate and cracked his knuckles so hard he yelped like a boy. That was Clemence entire: fear translated at once into a household tool.

The third policeman came in from the landing with his hat gone and his hair pasted to his forehead. "Take the papers first," he barked.

Elise moved before she had thought of moving. She caught the chair with the holed shirt on it and hurled it at the door to jam the frame a half second more. Then she went low, under the swinging club, and got her hands under Reine's shoulders beside Nadir.

"Scullery," she said. "Now. Yard last."

"How do you know that?" Clemence shouted.

"Because I can hear them."

It was not enough of an answer, and Clemence knew it, but the floor had become a paste slick and the first sergeant was trying to rise with Nadir on top of him, so doubt had to wait its turn. Reine's blood ran warm over Elise's fingers and made the tape measure at Reine's neck slippery as eel skin. The runner was not heavy until they had to lift her over the lip where the kitchen boards swelled. Then she became all dead weight and pain.

The scullery door banged off the wall. The service stair beyond was narrower than Elise had hoped and steeper than Reine's limp leg could bear. Somebody below shouted that the yard gate was manned. Somebody above shouted to search the roof. Elise had known they would split the stair and the yard, but not yet, not this quickly. Vautrin had not only moved the hour. He had fed the room full.

Ferrand had known.

Nadir wedged himself under Reine's left arm and hauled. "On three," he said.

"There isn't a three," Clemence said.

They dragged Reine down anyway. Her boot heel struck every step. Elise heard each hit in her teeth. Halfway to the yard, a constable shouldered up from below, face flushed, truncheon lifted in both hands because there was no room to swing it properly. Elise saw where he meant to hit before he did. She let go of Reine for one terrible beat, caught the rail, and drove the heel of her boot into his knee. Bone did not break. It gave enough. He screamed and sat down hard on the step, jamming the stair with his own body.

Reine gasped, "Jesus Christ."

Clemence stared at Elise as if a seam had split open in daylight.

Elise had no breath to dress the moment. She got both hands back under Reine and dragged. The constable's cap rolled past them to the yard below where rainwater, soot, and laundry soap had made a black skin over the puddles.

The yard was smaller than it had looked from the room, a square of brick and slime with privies down one side and a broken cart against the wall. A man in shirtsleeves was already at the gate, pounding to be let through from the alley. Another policeman answered from outside. They had the mouth, then. Elise's mind laid the pattern down whether she wanted it or not. Front stair, street door, yard mouth, then neighbors, then roof if the house was worth the manpower. If they could get over the cart and through the washhouse on the left, they might still spill into the next passage before the outer ring tightened. Not because she was brave. Because Vautrin had taught her the order and because surviving men like him meant remembering what they preferred.

"There," she said, jerking her chin at the washhouse wall.

"Not the gate?" Nadir asked.

"Do you want cuffs?"

He did not waste breath arguing. Reine was slipping in his grip. Blood had run down behind one ear and soaked the collar of her blouse. The paper bundle still lay upstairs under police boots. The notes were either in evidence already or stuck in somebody's glue. The errand had gone from clandestine labor to ruin in less than a minute.

Nadir looked once up toward the room they had left, where voices were overturning drawers and furniture in the official hunger of a search. Then he hitched Reine higher and turned his back on the lost stock.

"All right," he said. "We save the woman."

It was not a grand sentence. He said it like a man choosing which pan to pull off a fire first. Elise would remember it anyway.

The broken cart came apart under them. Clemence kicked a wheel spoke loose, laid it across the trough beside the washhouse, and went first with the agility of a woman who had spent half her life moving weight through spaces built too meanly for it. Nadir shoved Reine upward from below. Elise got hold of the back of Reine's skirt and the tape measure together and nearly tore both. Lime-wet brick scraped the skin from her palms. A neighbor on the other side screamed at the sight of them and dropped a basin. Water splashed over the wall into Elise's hair.

Then they were through the washhouse, out by the tubs, and into an alley that smelled of lye, urine, and cabbage leaves turning sweet in rot.

For ten paces no one followed.

They stopped only because Reine made a sound that was less a cry than a choke and her knees buckled completely. Nadir hauled her upright again with hard economy. Blood from her temple had tracked into the corner of her mouth. She spat pink on the stones and blinked, furious and dazed.

"My jacket," she muttered. "The blue one. The cuff's not done."

Clemence touched her face once, quick and almost gentle. "To hell with the cuff."

"The client already waits like a widow," Reine said, and then winced as if her own attempt at wit had jostled the wound.

There it was, the detail of her that had nothing to do with pamphlets or raids: a woman half senseless and still annoyed about a hem she had promised to finish. Elise felt the shame of finding that moving.

Nadir shifted Reine's arm more securely over his shoulders. "There's a bone-setter on rue du Pere-Corentin who doesn't ask questions if the money's up front."

"You've got money?" Clemence said.

"Not enough. But she's bleeding now, so arithmetic can come after."

His folded fox, soaked from the splash over the wall, had worked itself loose from his pocket and hung by one softened ear. He did not notice. He started down the alley with Reine against him, walking the same side-on angle as before only now every step cost him twice. Clemence moved to go with him.

Elise touched her sleeve. "If three of us walk with blood between us, we're a procession. Let me come later."

Clemence pulled free, then looked at Reine, then back at Elise. Suspicion and necessity crossed in her face so plainly that Elise could have laid a finger between them.

"Five minutes," Clemence said. "No more."

"Go."

Nadir did not turn. "If you're not there," he said to no one in particular, "I'll tell Lucien you stopped to join the police."

"He'll know you're lying," Clemence said.

"I know. That's why it's funny."

Even with a bleeding woman on him, his wit came out dry as old type. Then he was gone around the bend, Reine's boot dragging, Clemence behind them with her shoulders already set for the next cost.

Elise waited until their footfalls had thinned. Her hands shook only then, once, not with fear exactly but with the body's delayed resentment at being used. She put two fingers into the inner seam of her petticoat and ripped the stitches she had laid there by candlelight. The slips came free in a little packet no bigger than a prayer book's leaves.

Blank paper. That was what anybody else would see.

Milk ink had its own smell when it burned, sweet first and then ugly. Elise knew that because Vautrin had trained her with household things. Invisible writing for a woman who could not officially exist in his office. Blank scraps passed hand to hand like shopping lists, then held to heat and made suddenly legible: names, hours, corners, an address that might mean a raid or a payment or the difference between her mother eating broth and not. The slips in her hand held the habits of the last weeks. Where to leave reports. How to mark urgency. Which carrier took Prefecture money and which one sold messages to whoever paid second.

If Clemence searched her later and found them, Jeanne Morel would die before morning.

If the police searched her later and found them, Elise Valette would live, perhaps, but only in Vautrin's terms.

She crouched by the alley drain. Greasy water moved there in a skin the color of pewter. Somebody had emptied soup into it earlier; cabbage leaves clung to the grate. Elise struck a match against the wall. The sulfur flared yellow and stung her nose. She cupped it deep in both hands and touched it to the first slip. The paper browned, then curled inward as the hidden writing surfaced for a second like bruises coming up under skin. One date. One initial. The square that had promised after lamps. She fed it to the drain flame before it could finish darkening.

The second one fought her. Damp from her sweat, it wanted to smolder instead of catch. She held it closer, too close, and fire nipped the side of her thumb. Pain steadied her. She used it. One by one she burned them all, pinning the blackened curls under the sole of her boot until the grease took them and carried the ash away in gray strings.

Ninety francs a month, when Vautrin was pleased. Twenty-eight due monthly at Charenton. Three hundred and twelve already hanging over her mother like a gate waiting to fall. The numbers came to her exactly because numbers always did. She saw the account office in her head with its ink bottle, its polished corner worn by generations of forearms, the clerk who never lifted his eyes higher than a woman's gloves unless payment had failed. These scraps had been the clean road back to that office. Not safety. Nothing so grand. Only routine obedience, plausible service, the practical fiction that she could still do the work and live in two ledgers at once.

Ash stuck to the wet on her thumb. She rubbed it off against the stones and left a dark smear.

"What was that?"

Clemence's voice came from the mouth of the alley, not loud. Elise had not heard her return. That frightened her more than the stair had. Clemence stood with her hands empty now, apron dark where Reine's blood had dried into it. Her hairpins had started to slip, and one lock clung damply to the side of her face. She was not breathing hard. She had had time, then, not only to walk back but to stop and watch.

Elise rose too quickly and had to hide the stiffness in her legs by brushing grime from her skirt. "Nothing useful."

"I could see that."

Clemence came closer by a step. Not close enough to smell the burned milk in the air, perhaps, but close enough to see the little black curls still lodged by the grate.

"Receipts?" she asked.

"No."

"Addresses?"

Elise could have lied more fully. She could have said sewing patterns, letters, a saint's card gone soggy in her hem. The old training pressed at her, neat and fast. But Clemence had seen too much already: the instant at the stair, the kick at the constable's knee, the way Elise had known the yard would be taken. A bad lie now would not mend anything. It would only cheapen the damage.

"Instructions," Elise said.

Clemence's face altered by a degree so small another person might not have noticed. Elise noticed because Clemence always did her feeling through countable units. A half loaf. Two bars of soap. One blanket missing. One woman who should not have known what she knew.

"Whose instructions?" Clemence asked.

The right answer would have been nobody's. The useful answer would have been mine. Elise said nothing.

Clemence looked at the smear of ash on Elise's thumb. "Widows who copy invoices don't put a man down on a stair like that."

"Widows who stay alive learn things."

"Not that fast."

There it was. No shouted accusation, no theatrical revelation. Clemence was too tired and too practical for either. Suspicion sat in her voice the same way money sat in her household books: entered once, then kept until proved otherwise.

Elise made herself meet her eyes. "If I'd wanted you taken, there were better chances than today."

"That's not much comfort."

The answer landed clean. Elise almost smiled, not from humor but from respect. Clemence did not ask questions for the pleasure of hearing herself ask them. Each one was a hook laid only where she meant to pull.

From the street beyond the alley came the thud of running feet and somebody calling for a surgeon. The raid was spreading through the neighborhood now, outward into all the ordinary lives around it. Landlords would swear they had known nothing. Children would be dragged back from windows by the scruff. By nightfall there would be three versions in circulation already: the police version, the street version, the version the quarry would tell itself to keep breathing. Elise had just burned the only route by which she could feed the first one tidily.

Clemence followed her glance toward the street. "Can you walk?" she asked.

"Yes."

"Good. Then walk."

She turned first. Elise fell into step beside her. The alley narrowed before opening to the lane, and for a few yards they had to go shoulder to shoulder. Clemence did not edge away. That almost made it worse. It was not pardon. It was proximity under revised terms.

When they reached the corner, Clemence stopped and looked both ways before crossing. The motion was ordinary. So was what she said next.

"Lucie asked if you'd be back for supper."

Elise kept her face still with effort. "What did you tell her?"

"I told her not to ask me questions I couldn't price."

It was the sort of dry line Clemence allowed herself only when the work had already gone bad enough that wit could not worsen it. Elise heard, inside it, the thing Clemence would not yet say aloud: that every person in the quarry cost something to feed, hide, trust, and bury, and Jeanne Morel had just become more expensive.

They crossed into the next street where the evening had not yet fallen and therefore exposed everything. A greengrocer was still shouting over wilted carrots. Two girls in aprons laughed outside a doorway, not having heard the noise beyond the passage house. Laundry water ran in a bright thread along the gutter. The city went on with the indecency of all cities, using the same stones for panic and commerce and appetite, never bothering to choose.

Elise felt the absence at her waist where the packet had been hidden. It was a physical emptiness, not symbolic, a strip of loosened seam against her skin. She had cut herself off with household fire and gutter water. No dead-drop note could undo that now. No tidy report could place her again in Vautrin's careful columns. If she wanted back into his good graces she would have to go in person, with all the risk of that, and offer him something larger than paper. A name. A map. A clean betrayal.

She knew, even before the thought had finished, that she did not have one clean enough left to offer.

Clemence did not speak again for the length of the street. She only kept walking with that square practical stride, red hands bare at her sides, blood drying in the cracks around her knuckles. On another day Elise might have asked how bad Reine's head wound looked, whether Nadir had enough money for the bone-setter, whether the notes had all been lost upstairs. The questions crowded her and found nowhere to go.

At the far end of the block Clemence slowed just enough to make Elise look at her.

"When we get back," she said, "you'll tell Lucien exactly how that room went bad."

Not who sent you. Not what you burned. Not why you moved before the door. Exactly how. Clemence was already setting terms, narrowing the ground on which lies would have to stand.

"All right," Elise said.

"And if you try to dress it up, I'll stop you."

"I heard you."

"I know."

They went on toward the road that would take them below again, carrying nothing from the errand except blood on their cuffs, the memory of paper left under boots, and a silence that had changed its shape. Clemence's suspicion did not blaze. It settled. Elise could feel it there between them, no wider than a ledger line and just as permanent once inked.


# Chapter 8

Clemence turned the bread sack upside down over the ration table and shook out flour dust, limestone dust, and two black beetles stiff with cold. Nothing else fell. The table was an old door laid across trestles, scrubbed so often the grain stood up like whiskers. Elise watched the empty sack keep its shape for a second in Clemence's fists, a poor body's last lie.

There were four loaves. One had gone green at the heel. There were eleven potatoes with pale shoots budding from the eyes, three onions soft at the neck, half a crock of herring clouding in its own brine, a rind of goat cheese wrapped in newspaper, and enough flour for two more suppers if nobody minded grit. Clemence lined the things in rows as if shame improved under order. Beside the food she set what had survived the night intact: one good lantern, one lantern with a cracked chimney, two cakes of soap cut down to mean slivers, a kettle leaking at the seam, six needles, a spool of black thread, and the little tin of chicory that only Lucien pretended was coffee.

What had not survived went on the other side of the table: the handcart, Nadir's composing stick, two bundles of the prison appeal broadside, a good blue basin from the laundry yard, the brass key to Charvet's print room, Marcel Bisset, Etienne Sorel, and Ferrand.

Lucie sat cross-legged on a folded blanket and counted dried beans into groups of five, lips moving with the numbers. Clemence had replaited one side of the child's hair and left the other side to spring loose; the braid stopped neat behind one ear and the rest of her head had given up. Each time Lucie reached five she clicked a bean against the lid of the tin as if it were a coin.

"Say it proper," Clemence said without looking at her. "If you're counting, count for use."

"Five, ten, fifteen, twenty-three," Lucie said.

"Twenty-five."

Lucie frowned at the beans as if they had insulted arithmetic. "Then two are hiding."

"They'll fit the room."

Nadir gave a short breath that was not quite a laugh. He had washed his face, but the blood remained under his nails and in the seam of one ear. A spoiled proof sheet had somehow become a paper fish in his fingers while he listened; he flattened it when he noticed Elise looking. "The room's full of excellent examples today."

He stood a little sideways, as he always did, his nicked cheek pale against the limestone. The sleeve of his coat had dried stiff where Colombe's blood had soaked into it. Colombe Bisset lay on two mattresses pushed together by the wall, one boot cut away, her right ankle wrapped in towels gone pink and then brown. She kept turning the silver thimble on the black ribbon at her throat with her thumb and forefinger, as if a hem still waited for her somewhere outside this chamber. Every time someone said Marcel's name, the thimble clicked lightly against her collarbone.

Abel paced behind the trestles, too young for the look of stubble he was trying to wear. His jacket sleeves still stopped short of the wrists no matter how often he tugged them down. When he wanted to interrupt and thought better of it, he rubbed the two burned fingertips of his right hand together until the skin whitened. He had been doing it since Clemence began laying out the loaves.

Lucien sat at the end of the table with a pencil and the marbled school copybook Elise had seen in the lessons room. The cover had been stitched back together with blue thread, neat as a surgeon's work. He did not use it for speeches. He wrote full names in it, spelled the way people spelled themselves when the police were not helping. Beside Marcel Bisset he had already put last seen on the landing. Beside Etienne Sorel he had written courtyard, separated in the crush. Ferrand's name he had left without a note, the pencil hovering above the paper for so long that Elise could feel Clemence's temper building from across the table.

"Cross him off," Clemence said at last. "He ran before the room even broke."

Lucien did not look up. "I can mark that he vanished. I can't mark him dead or gone for good because you want him gone."

"I want bread more than I want him gone."

"That's not the same question."

"It's exactly the same question if he comes back hungry."

Elise kept her face useful and still. The quarry smelled of wet wool, old chalk, lamp smoke, and the herring brine Clemence had uncorked. It was warmer than it had been in April, but not truly warm. The cold had only moved inward, into the blankets, into the stone bench, into the place in her skirt where the cipher slips had once ridden flat against her thigh. She felt their absence like a missing tooth. It made every silence harder to hide behind.

Lucien wrote vanished after Ferrand's name. His cough caught him on the second syllable of something he had meant to say next. He turned his head and pressed a handkerchief to his mouth before the cough could open wider. When he straightened again, the schoolmaster's gentleness had returned to his face, but thinner now, stretched over a worse arithmetic.

"Tell me again," he said to Nadir. "From the courtyard."

Nadir's expression shut another notch. "You heard it the first time."

"I know. Tell me again."

Nadir set the ruined paper fish on the table beside the cheese rind. "We had the bundle, the money, and Charvet's key. Marcel was ahead. Etienne was on the stairs with the cart. I heard boots below before I heard shouting above, which meant they had both ends. Ferrand went left so quickly I thought he'd been yanked by a wire. Colombe slipped on the second landing and the stone for the handbills came down on her foot. Marcel came back for her. That was the last clean sight I had of him."

Colombe's fingers tightened on the thimble. "He'd come back for a dropped spoon. Don't make him sound noble."

"I wasn't," Nadir said.

"Then don't make him sound dead either."

No one answered that. Water ticked somewhere in the deeper passage, slow and steady as a clock the quarry had made for itself. Overhead, faint and broad, a carriage went by in the street. Elise thought of the city moving above them with butcher's errands and laundry wagons and clerks late to offices, all of it balanced on this bad white stone.

Clemence pushed the chicory tin aside and took up the pencil herself for a moment, not to write but to point. "Nine mouths here if Ferrand keeps running. Ten if Marcel crawls back. Eleven if Etienne does. Twelve if Colombe can keep broth down. I've got enough for two days decent, maybe three if we water the soup until it tastes of the pan and not the food. Oil for two nights if Lucie doesn't read and nobody sews. Soap enough to keep the wounds from turning black if nobody wants clean shirts. After that we can eat Abel's verses."

"I'd be honored," Abel said, too quickly.

"You won't be when I start with your better adjectives."

Lucie looked up from the beans. "If Marcel's arrested, does he still count for dinner?"

The question landed more cleanly than the adult speech had. Lucie's fear always went for the practical part first, as if facts were small handles cut into a dark wall. Elise saw Lucien hear it and fail, just for an instant, to answer his pupil.

"He counts for us," he said.

"That isn't what she asked," Clemence said.

Lucie did not let either of them off. "Does he get our dinner if he's in prison?"

"No," Clemence said. "Prison gives you other slop."

Lucie nodded once, unsatisfied but informed. Then she asked, "Does Etienne still have the cart?"

"If he does," Nadir said, "he's had a very bad morning with it."

Abel stopped pacing. "We can't sit on our arses counting potatoes while they're taking people. We go back out. We find the stations. We ask at the hospitals. We ask the concierge in the next house. Somebody saw something."

"You don't ask a concierge anything with your face looking like a proclamation," Clemence said.

"My face looks normal."

"Your face looks like it wants witnesses."

Abel colored. Elise had seen him take insult with more grace when the insult came from older men. From Clemence it hit the place where hunger and boyish pride still lived together. He glanced to Lucien for rescue, and Lucien, because rescue came easier to him than refusal, gave him a version of it.

"The impulse isn't wrong," Lucien said. "The order may be."

"Then give the order," Clemence said. "Who's first?"

That was the moment Elise understood what the morning had been building toward. Until then they had still been pretending the tally itself was the work. Count the loaves, count the missing, count the coins, and some decent answer might rise from the sums. But counting was only what one did before the knife went in. After that came choice.

Lucien looked at the notebook, then at Colombe's swollen foot under the towels, then at Lucie and the loose braid down one side of her head, then at the two missing names on the page that were not Ferrand's. He had moral authority underground because he could make a room feel larger than the fear inside it. He could teach children letters on a quarry wall and make the chalk seem more solid than the police. He could tell a starving man he still belonged to history and nearly make it sound generous. But now the room wanted a butcher's order, not a teacher's mercy.

"Colombe needs a bone-setter," he said. "If the foot dies, it won't matter whether Marcel returns."

Colombe pushed herself up on one elbow with a curse that came out thin. "Marcel first. Set the foot after. My brother turns stupid when frightened. He can't hold his water when men shout at him. You know that."

Lucien flinched, not from the vulgarity but from the fact. He had known it. Elise could tell by the way his eyes lowered. He wrote something in the notebook instead of answering.

"The papers first," Nadir said. "If they take the front room or the wine shop gets watched, they'll come looking for names next. Bread matters. Names matter longer."

"Food first," Clemence said. "You can't eat a clean alias."

"No," Nadir said, "but you can rent one."

Abel struck his fist into his palm. "Ferrand first. If that bastard sold the room, he'll sell the rest."

Clemence did not even turn. "You're assuming he had anything to sell besides his own hide."

"He ran."

"So did sensible men in other centuries. That proves nothing except legs."

"Enough." Lucien's voice did not rise much, but the word carried. Then it thinned again. "Enough. Colombe's right to ask for Marcel. Nadir's right about the papers. Clemence's right about the food. Abel isn't wrong that Ferrand's a danger now whether he betrayed us or only proved himself worthless. I'm not pretending any of that is smaller than it is."

"So who's first?" Clemence asked again.

He took too long.

It was not a theatrical pause. That would have been easier to forgive. It was the real thing: a man trying to keep from cutting one life away from another and discovering that all his principles had been built for rooms with a little more bread in them. Elise knew the type of delay. She had committed it in offices with different stationery. Put a line between two necessities and the hand learned suddenly to hover.

Lucien made the mistake of answering honestly. "I don't know if there's a just first."

Clemence let out a breath through her nose. "Then we'd better pick an unjust one before the day picks it for us."

The silence after that had weight. Abel looked stricken on Lucien's behalf, which was worse than anger. Nadir looked away altogether. Colombe lay back down and turned the thimble until it clicked against bone. Elise kept her eyes on the beans in Lucie's tin because looking directly at Lucien in that minute would have felt indecent, like watching a man stripped.

He closed the notebook without slamming it. He never did anything hard unless it had been forced on him. "We'll do what we can in parallel," he said, and even he heard the weakness in it.

"With what?" Clemence asked. "Parallel with which hands?"

He had no answer that did not sound borrowed. Clemence gathered the food back into its measured rows. The beetles she swept to the floor with the side of her hand. Lucie began recounting the beans from the start.

By afternoon the inventory had spread from the ration room through the whole of the refuge. Elise helped where she could because usefulness was the only shape of innocence still available to her. She carried boiled cloths to Colombe and held the lamp while Nadir rewound the loose screws on the deeper press with a tool too small for the work. She found the second cracked lantern hanging behind the schoolroom bench and discovered that the wick was gone, cut clean, perhaps months ago, perhaps last night; scarcity had a way of erasing dates. In the sleeping gallery she turned blankets and counted what could still be called dry. Six whole, three sour with old damp, two stiff with blood from the raid. Clemence said nothing when Elise reported the number, only nodded and marked it on the back of a handbill with a bit of carpenter's chalk.

The schoolroom wall still held the previous week's sums. Lucie had written her name there in an uncertain, proud hand, and beneath it Lucien had drawn a straight line to show her where letters ought to stand if they wished to behave. Beside the slate tray lay his notebook. Elise should not have touched it. She did not. She only looked. The page he had left open held columns of names, dates, addresses gone stale, who had been taken where, who had used which alias in which lodging house, whose mother still believed him in Lyon, whose child ought not to be told until the body was certain. It was not a police file. It was the opposite of one. Vautrin's records stripped people down to leverage and category; Lucien's notebook seemed built to smuggle their full weight through a century that would prefer labels. Elise felt the old professional reflex stir in her at the sight of good information, then sour. There were forms of exactness that did not deserve the same name.

When Lucien came in behind her she stepped away from the bench at once.

"I wasn't reading," she said.

"You were looking," he said. His cough had left his voice roughened, but not unkind. "That's different."

"Only if the room forgives it."

He followed her glance to the notebook. "It's easier to lose people on paper than in a corridor. Paper gives you permission."

Elise thought of arrest sheets, cross-indexed aliases, the clean bureaucratic pleasure of reducing a body to a usable line. "Sometimes paper is all that's left of the corridor."

He looked at her then, properly, the way he had not managed in the ration room. Exhaustion had rubbed him almost plain. "Were you always this practical, Jeanne, or only when the plaster starts falling?"

Her alias fitted better when he asked nothing personal of it. "I was practical when there was rent due. I stayed practical."

"That sounds expensive."

"Everything useful is."

The corner of his mouth moved, not into a smile. "Clemence's right that I failed them this morning."

Elise could have lied and called it generosity. It would have sounded kind and he would have heard the false note anyway. "You wanted a clean answer."

"There wasn't one."

"No."

"That didn't stop the room from needing it."

He rested his knuckles on the bench, close to the notebook but not touching it. The healed scar high in his shoulder had pulled the fabric of his shirt a little crooked there; Elise noticed because the quarry taught attention through inches. "If I send for Marcel, Colombe may lose the foot. If I hide the papers first, I'm admitting we'll need to run. If I buy food before I trace Etienne, I choose the ones who are already under my eye over the ones who vanished serving us. I know all of that. Knowing isn't the same as choosing."

It was the nearest he had come, with her, to asking not for absolution but for witness. That was more dangerous than if he had reached for comfort. Comfort could be managed. Witness got into the blood.

"Then stop waiting to deserve the choice," Elise said.

His eyes came back to her, quick and sharp despite the fatigue. For an instant she thought she had spoken too close to her real training, too near the language of orders and acceptable losses. But what he heard in it seemed otherwise.

"You say that as if practice has made it easier."

"Practice makes almost everything dirtier."

Before he could answer, Clemence called from the passage for both of them. Her voice had the cut of a woman who had finished adding and disliked the total.

They gathered again in the schoolroom because the ration table had become a bandage station around Colombe. Dusk had not yet reached the surface, but underground the lamps already mattered. Clemence shut the door cloth against the draft and set a round biscuit tin on Lucien's bench. The tin had once held butter cookies for a family that could afford ornament; now the painted lid was scratched white at the edges. She kept one hand on it a second before opening it, as if the warmth of her palm might change its contents.

Inside lay the reserve in ugly little dignities: one fifty-franc note folded down and softened by too many hidings, a five-franc piece, eight single francs, and a spill of centimes wrapped in rag so they would not talk. Beneath the money sat papers in oilcloth: two residence certificates, a widow's booklet with the name not yet inked in, three baptism extracts, a blank labor carnet with a municipal stamp good enough at dusk, and the rubber seal Nadir had once said looked true only if you did not insult it by staring.

"Sixty-three francs and forty centimes," Clemence said. "All told, unless somebody's learned to mint in his sleep. This and the papers can't stay under my bedding now. If they come through here next, they lift the blankets first. Men always think women's beds are purses."

Abel leaned in despite himself. "Give them to me."

Clemence put the lid back on with one flat motion. "I'd sooner tie them to Lucie's ankle."

"I'm serious."

"So am I. You couldn't carry soup across the street without turning it into a cause."

His mouth opened on anger. Nadir cut across it before the speech could swell. "She's right about the need, if not the courier. The room at Charvet's is gone. The old flour niche is too near the front entrance. We need a new hole aboveground by tonight."

"I can take them," Abel said. "Nobody knows me."

"Plenty of people know a nineteen-year-old boy trying to look thirty," Nadir said. "It's a common species."

Lucie, curled on the bench with her chin on her knees, studied the biscuit tin instead of the adults. "If the papers get wet, do the names come off?"

"Sometimes," Elise said before anyone else could answer.

Lucie took that seriously. "Then don't let them get wet."

Clemence looked at Elise for a beat too long. Suspicion had moved beyond intuition now. It had settled into method. Elise could feel it measuring the speed with which she had dropped in the raid, the steadiness of her hands afterward, the way she listened when routes were named. Clemence did not know what Jeanne Morel was, but she knew what she was not.

"Not you," Clemence said.

Elise kept her voice level. "Because you don't trust me or because I'm wrong for the street?"

"Both would do."

Lucien lifted a hand, perhaps to soften it, perhaps to worsen it by trying. "Clemence."

"No, let her ask." Clemence's red hands stayed on the tin. Soap had eaten the skin beside her nails into small white crescents that never healed properly. "You heard me."

Elise could have taken the safer road and stepped back. There had been months of it, months spent watching, reporting, omitting, nudging, and keeping her own body out of the balance whenever possible. That had already led them here: to Colombe's foot, to Marcel's name with no station beside it, to Lucien forced into public helplessness while she stood clean enough to judge him. The thought came without nobility. She did not want to remain outside the ledger because the outside had begun to stink more than the risk.

"I'm probably the best shape for it," she said. "Widow's clothes, no child with me, no cough, no printer's ink on my cuffs. I know how to carry a bundle and look like the bundle is the whole of me."

Abel stared at her. "Since when?"

"Since before you needed shaving."

Nadir's mouth twitched. Clemence did not.

"And if you decide not to come back?" Clemence asked.

There it was, cleanly spoken. It was not accusation yet, but the square outline of it.

Elise looked at the tin, not at Lucien. "Then you've lost sixty-three francs and forty centimes, a seal, and some lies on paper. If you send Lucien, you lose the route knowledge with legs on it. If you send Nadir, you risk the best compositor you have and a face the street remembers. If you send Abel, you lose the packet to the first policeman who asks him a dull question. If you go yourself, Lucie goes with a neighbor by night because she'll never stay put while you're out. I'm expendable in the only way that's useful here."

Lucien's head came up sharply. "Don't call yourself that."

"I'm counting," Elise said.

"Arithmetic isn't neutral just because you keep your voice calm."

"No. But it still adds."

The room shifted around that under pressure rather than agreement. Lucie reached out and put one finger on the biscuit tin's painted lid, tracing the worn gold edge of a flower no one could fully see anymore. Abel looked furious that logic sounded better in Elise's mouth than in his. Nadir watched everyone except Lucien, which was how he watched hardest.

Clemence asked, "How would you carry it?"

That was the first concession. Elise answered at once, because hesitation here would read as invention. "Sewn into the inner hem or under the skirt facing, split in two packets if you want. Papers flat under the stays, money lower where it moves like weight and not like paper. Put laundry over the rest. Let me smell of soap and old water instead of fear."

"Route?"

"Not the wine shop straight through. Out by the yard. Across the Bievre where the mud ruins everybody's boots alike. After that you tell me."

Nadir gave the smallest nod. "That's not stupid."

"Nothing she's said today has been stupid," Clemence said. "That's part of the trouble."

Lucien's expression changed at that, not to surprise. He had been feeling the same thing and disliked himself for it. Elise saw the knowledge pass between them and remain unspoken. A few weeks ago she might have found satisfaction in that. Now it only made the stone seem closer to her face.

"The cache is Lemoine's coal box behind the cooper's shed," Clemence said. "Third plank up from the floor. The old one rotted through yesterday and I don't fancy losing the rest of us to damp before the police manage it. You go at first light. Not tonight."

Abel made an incredulous noise. "And that's it? We hand her the whole future because she talks like an account book?"

"No," Clemence said. "Because the future has become an account book and I don't have the luxury of pretending otherwise."

Lucien closed his eyes briefly, then opened them on Elise. "If you take it and anything turns wrong, the papers first. Hide them, burn them, throw them in the river if you must. The money matters. The names matter more."

It would have been easy to hear sentiment in that. It was not sentiment. It was strategy cut to the bone. Elise respected him more for the order than she had for any speech he had made that morning, and the respect made everything worse.

"I'll get them there," she said.

Clemence was already taking out the needle. She did not trust promises when thread would do better. "Stand still, then."

Elise stepped forward. Clemence opened the oilcloth packet and separated the contents with the speed of a laundress sorting stains. The labor carnet and the blank widow's booklet went flat against Elise's stays. The folded note and coins she wrapped in a scrap of chamois and pinned inside the lower facing of Elise's skirt, where the drag would read as bad dressmaking if anyone noticed at all. Her fingers were brisk and unceremonious, tugging cloth, biting thread, testing each stitch with the same ruthless competence she brought to ripped cuffs and burst seams.

"If you run," Clemence said around the pin held between her teeth, "you'll do it dragging my sewing."

"Fair enough."

"If you're searched?"

"I'll cry about modesty and say my dead husband taught me nothing useful."

Abel snorted.

"What?" Elise asked without turning.

"You don't sound like a widow."

"No one does until they're one."

That shut him. Lucie watched the sewing with grave attention, then said, "My name won't fit in there."

Lucien answered before Elise could. "Your name is staying with us."

Lucie considered that, then slid off the bench and came to stand by Clemence's elbow. "Make the stitches small," she told her mother. "Big ones catch."

"I know my trade."

"I know skirts."

Nadir, almost under his breath, said, "God preserve the police from competent women."

Clemence gave the hem one last hard tug and let it fall. The new weight dragged at Elise's left side, a downward insistence that changed her balance by less than an inch and more than that. Money had its own way of announcing itself to the body. So did paper when paper could decide whether a room was emptied as criminals or dispersed as widows and laborers and cousins with decent parish records.

Lucien picked up the notebook from the bench and tucked it into his coat. For a moment Elise thought he might say something private to her, something about trust or the lack of it, something that would try to clean the uglier part of what had just happened. He did not. He had learned a little in the course of the day.

"Sleep if you can," he said instead. "You leave before the carts start."

It was almost full dark by the time Elise lay down in the sleeping gallery. She did not sleep. The packet altered every turn of her body. When she shifted, the coins gave one dull, intimate knock against her thigh, like a hand from inside the skirt asking whether she understood at last what she had offered. Around her the quarry settled into its night sounds: Colombe's breath catching when the pain climbed, Lucie's brief talking in sleep, Abel still awake somewhere deeper in the passage and reciting under his breath to keep from thinking, Nadir tearing a proof sheet cleanly down the middle for tomorrow's kindling, Lucien coughing once and then smothering the rest for the sake of the others.

Elise stared into the dark until the white of the wall began to suggest itself beyond the lamp's reach. She had entered the refuge to turn lives into reportable matter. Then she had begun leaving names off pages, hiding ledgers, burning slips. Those had still been forms of observation, however compromised. This was something meaner and more binding. Beneath her hem rode bread, rent, aliases, the right to cross a checkpoint without vanishing into an office. If she failed aboveground, the loss would not be information. It would be supper. It would be the next room. It would be the thin legal fiction by which Lucie might pass for somebody the Republic had no immediate wish to lock away.

Toward dawn, when the stone sweated colder and the city above had not yet started its wheels, Elise rose and dressed. Clemence was already waiting by the passage with a bundle of washing over one arm. She handed it over without greeting. Trust had not grown in the night. It had only put on work clothes.

Elise took the damp weight of the laundry. Under it, under the skirt, under the discipline of her own careful face, the hidden packet pulled at her with every breath. For the first time since she had come down under the hill, she was carrying not their names for somebody else's ledger but one piece of their continued existence. Then she turned toward the narrow climb to the wine cellar and the morning above it.


# Chapter 9

The woman who kept the reserve cache lived over a shop that sold mourning ribbon and paper flowers, the sort that looked respectable by gaslight and shabby by morning. She took the packet from Elise with both hands, not because it was heavy but because she handled paper as if it bruised. Broken corset busks rattled in jars along her shelf. She saved every strip of black silk that crossed her counter, saying a widow could always use another piece of darkness.

She untied the string, counted the forged cards with her lips moving, then pressed the wrapped coins flat under her palm.

"Tell Clemence the green box is safer than the stove pipe," she said. "And tell her if the little one needs stockings again, she should stop buying them new. The dead leave plenty."

"I'll tell her."

The woman looked up then, quick and narrow-eyed. "And tell Lucien not to trust any man who arrives washed. Men like that have a room somewhere else to dirty first."

Elise almost smiled. "He knows that already."

"Knowing isn't the same as acting on it."

The room smelled of glue, damp card, and the dust that came off cheap velvet when it had been handled too often. Elise had the sense, brief and indecent, of how easy it would be to stay there another minute with the city muffled by cloth and walls and practical gossip. Then she stepped back into the street and pulled her veil down against the light.

She should have turned south at once and gone back toward Montrouge with the errand done. Clemence had not entrusted the money and papers to her so she could wander. The forged cards now sat under someone else's floorboards, the reserve cash under someone else's stove, and that should have been enough risk for one afternoon.

Instead she kept walking north.

The quarry cold stayed in her dress even after the day had warmed. It made the city feel doubly exposed: the horse dung already drying white at the edges, the river smell coming up through the bridges, the bright shop glass that gave back too much of a face if she let herself look. She crossed by habit more than decision, taking streets her body remembered from months of being sent where Vautrin required her. Old routes rose under her feet with insulting ease. Here was the tobacconist who watered his display ferns with last night's coffee. Here the alley where the constables came to piss between raids. Here the shuttered law-copyist's office where she had once spent three days transcribing statements so a magistrate could pretend his certainty had come from careful reading rather than the first rumor handed to him.

By the time the towers and slate roofs of the Ile de la Cite lifted ahead of her, caution had already lost the argument.

The Prefecture stood in its usual posture of stone confidence, as if no one inside had ever sweated through a shirt or lied on paper for money. Petitioners crowded the main steps, hats in hand, each with a different emergency tied in blue ribbon. Elise did not go near them. The side entrance on the quai handled copyists, charwomen, messenger boys, and men too small to deserve the front stair.

The desk sergeant there was trying to stop smoking and had taken to chewing cloves so hard the smell came off him sweet and rotten together. His left cuff had been patched in green thread by a child who had either bad eyes or a sense of joke. He looked at the sheaf of papers under Elise's arm, at the widow's veil, and then past her to a boy carrying an ink crate twice the width of his chest.

Old office work had taught her a set of bodily commandments. She did not hesitate at thresholds. She kept paper in her hands. She never turned at a name unless it belonged to her in that corridor. A woman carrying work could pass through half the state if she did not make anyone feel responsible for seeing her.

She took the nearest tied bundle from the tray by the wall, nodded as if already late for someone else's irritation, and went in.

The stone hall breathed out polish, wet wool, sealing wax, and the faint old urine smell every government building eventually admitted no matter how much soap got thrown at it. Boots rang on the tile. A clerk coughed behind a door until the cough turned theatrical and stopped. Somewhere above, a bell gave one sharp impatient note for dictation.

Elise did not head straight for Vautrin's floor. Straight lines drew questions. She climbed one flight, crossed to the copying room, came down another stair, and entered the anti-anarchist corridor from the far end where the light went mean and yellow after midday. She knew the building's useless habits as well as any prayer. Half past one was when one records clerk went out for stew and the other stole ten minutes in the yard to pick his teeth with a matchstick. Half past one was also when Vautrin preferred visitors kept waiting so they would arrive at his door already a little bent.

She had spent too much of her life learning those preferences. They rose in her now with the smoothness of a hand going to a hidden pocket.

The corridor was narrower than she remembered. Or perhaps the quarry had changed her sense of space. Here the walls pressed with varnish and framed directives. Underground, pressure came from rock and the weight of the city above. Here it came from doors shut soft over names.

Vautrin's outer room stood open. His clerk's desk was empty but for a sand-shaker, two stacked dockets, and a heel of bread wrapped in yesterday's racing sheet. The bread had been bitten twice and put down in a hurry. That meant someone nearby.

She would have gone straight to the desk if Vautrin had not spoken.

His voice came through the inner door, measured as ever, each word set down like cutlery arranged before a guest.

"No, monsieur, I am not asking the court to imagine danger. I am asking it to receive what will shortly be recoverable."

A second man answered too low for sense.

Vautrin again: "Pamphlets do not impress anymore. Every student in Paris can be found with pamphlets if one empties enough trunks. Powder is different. Powder can be weighed. Powder can be displayed. It satisfies the eye of the paper and the hand of the magistrate."

Another murmur.

"Then leave the warrant undated," Vautrin said. "Monsieur Ferrand has not yet earned the signature."

Elise had been still before. Now stillness became labor. She stood with the borrowed bundle against her ribs and felt the paper edges bite through her glove.

There was the scrape of a chair inside, then footsteps crossing away from the door. A drawer shut. Water poured into a glass. She had perhaps half a minute, perhaps less. The kind of office time that had shaped her work was always measured in other people's inconveniences.

She set the borrowed bundle on the clerk's chair and moved.

Vautrin kept his outer room with the false disorder of men who wanted subordinates to think genius had no time for neatness. Elise knew better. The active files always lay left of the blotter, the completed ones to the right, the sensitive ones under whatever object would make another hand seem presumptuous. Today the object was a paperweight in the form of a bronze hound, one ear rubbed bright from years of use.

Under the hound sat a thin docket tied with blue ribbon.

She slipped it free and opened it on the clerk's desk, turning the pages with two fingers so the paper would not crackle.

The first sheet was a reimbursement ledger. Special disbursements, Section III. Three entries in the same dry hand.

`12 April 1895. J. Ferrand. Advance for cultivation expenses: 35 francs.`

`2 May 1895. J. Ferrand. Supplementary indemnity upon successful introduction to Delatour circle: 40 francs.`

`14 May 1895. J. Ferrand. Final payment upon delivery of material proof sufficient to support search and detention: 60 francs.`

Sixty francs for the last step alone.

The number sat on the page with the obscene calm of official ink. Sixty francs was more than two months at Charenton. Sixty francs was enough bread to keep Clemence from watering soup into gray dishwater for weeks. A hundred and thirty-five in total, if Ferrand did as instructed. The state would pay him more to dirty one room properly than it paid Elise to live inside a lie for a month.

There was a receipt pinned behind the ledger entry, Ferrand's name scratched in an ugly slant as if the pen had offended him. Beside the signature was a thumbprint of diluted violet ink. Elise could almost smell his cheap cologne through the paper, though that was memory and contempt doing each other's work.

The next sheets made the first look almost honest.

One was a draft deposition written in legal French so clean it mocked the man meant to speak it. Ferrand was to testify that Lucien Delatour had requested explosive compounds and instructed him to store them near the press room until a suitable public target was chosen. The date line stood blank. So did the exact quantity of gelignite. Ferrand had not yet placed the lie inside a room, but the sentence waiting for him already existed.

Behind it sat the warrant application.

Preventive search and detention under the widened statutes. Subjects: Lucien Delatour, Nadir Belkacem, Abel Saint-Clair, and unnamed associates occupying a concealed site off rue de la Tombe-Issoire. Grounds: clandestine printing apparatus, unlawful association, possession or instruction in the handling of explosive materials to be recovered on site. The list of objects to be seized had already been drawn up in a tidy vertical hand: hand press, type cases, correspondence, school copybooks, address ledgers, chemical materials, detonators.

School copybooks.

Lucie's clumsy alphabets could go into a police sack beside powder and come back out for a newspaper illustrator as evidence of breeding.

In the margin, in Vautrin's blue pencil, a note:

`If substances are not yet visible in situ, intermediary to complete arrangement before execution. Delay no longer than one week.`

The phrasing was mild, almost courteous. It landed harder for that reason. Complete arrangement. As if furnishing a rented room. As if one merely shifted a chair and laid out clean linen before guests arrived.

Footsteps sounded at the corridor end.

Elise pulled the deposition free first, then the ledger sheet beneath it. Thin copies, not originals. Vautrin kept duplicates because he trusted paper more than memory and himself more than everyone else. She folded both once, twice, and slid them inside the cuff of her sleeve. The edge of the warrant snagged her skin and opened a hot bright line across the pad of her thumb.

She had just retied the blue ribbon when the outer clerk came back in with his lunch pail swinging.

He was a narrow man with soup on his waistcoat and a tiny varnished seashell fixed to his watch chain, the sort of thing a child might have pressed into his hand and ordered him to keep forever. He stopped when he saw her at the desk.

"Yes?"

Elise did not turn too fast. Fast looked guilty. Offended looked legitimate.

"This was left in the wrong order," she said, lifting the docket half an inch. "The Ferrand disbursement is sitting on top of the Montrouge seizure requests. If Monsieur Vautrin wants the magistrate papers before four, he'll want them untangled first."

The clerk frowned, more at the possibility of late work than at her. "Who asked you?"

"No one yet. That's how I knew to do it."

He made a face that admitted the logic while resenting it. "Leave it there."

"As you like."

She set the file down and picked up the borrowed bundle from the chair. The clerk had already turned to his desk, prying open the lunch pail. Steam and onion came up between them. He lifted the lid and glanced once at her sleeve, perhaps because of the blood beginning to spot the glove thumb, perhaps because instinct occasionally troubled even small men.

"You're from which office?"

"Copying."

He snorted. "Aren't we all."

Then he sat and began to eat.

Elise walked out without haste. Her back knew exactly how much speed the corridor would forgive.

At the stair landing she heard Vautrin's inner door open. She did not look. Looking was a confession. She kept down the steps with the bundle still under her arm, one hand closed hard over the papers in her sleeve. A draught crossed the stairwell carrying his soap and his clean-cuff smell. It touched her cheek and was gone. Whether he had stepped into the outer room in time to see the hem of her skirt vanish, she could not tell.

The desk sergeant at the side entrance was arguing with the ink boy about a receipt ticket. His cloves and bad temper had both sharpened. Elise let the argument cover her exit and did not breathe properly until the quai wind hit her full in the face.

Only then did she realize how badly her hand hurt.

Blood had dried tacky inside the glove. She took the first side street, found a public lavatory whose door would not quite latch, and stood in the sour dark with one boot braced against the wall while she worked the papers out of her sleeve. They were damp now, not ruined but close enough to make her careful. Her thumb left a red crescent on Ferrand's final payment line.

She used the pin from her collar to lift the stitches at the inside hem of her dress. The Surete marker lay where Vautrin had first told her to keep it, a brass certainty no bigger than two stacked coins, smooth from her own nervous touching over the weeks. She slid the folded copies in beside it and stitched the seam back with fingers that wanted to shake and did not.

The metal and paper rested together against her calf when she stood. Approval and evidence in one seam.

In the alley outside, two delivery boys were pissing against the wall and discussing a cyclist who had broken his jaw near the market. A woman farther down shook a rug over a window ledge and sent dust into the light. Paris had room for every appetite it could price. Behind her, inside the Prefecture, Vautrin had left a warrant undated while Ferrand earned the right to fill it.

Elise turned south at last.

The long walk back felt different with the papers hidden on her body. She no longer thought in terms of whether Ferrand was dangerous. That question had gone stale. The question now was whether she meant to let the state build its case in the shape of Lucien's rooms and children's slates and Nadir's hands blackened by honest work. She could still do nothing. Doing nothing was often what service required. She could go below, say the money and papers were safe, keep quiet, and wait for Ferrand to finish proving what Vautrin had already paid for.

The possibility sickened her less by morality than by texture. She had seen the list. She knew which objects would be taken first, what order boots would use on the stair, how long a hand press took to catalog when a clerk was lazy. Silence was no longer abstract. Silence had itemized itself.

By the time she reached the wine shop on rue de la Tombe-Issoire, evening had flattened the windows to a dirty shine. Wine smell above, chalk damp below. She gave the cellar knock and waited through the scrape of the bolt.

The descent always chilled her more after a day aboveground. The quarry held cold the way offices held secrets: patiently, with no interest in whether human bodies found it convenient.

In the ration room Clemence was kneeling over a crate of turnips with her sleeves rolled and a seam ripped open under one arm where the day had pulled too hard. She was trimming rot from the vegetables with the same blunt knife she used on soap blocks, wasting nothing she could bully back into use.

"The drop?" Clemence asked.

"Done. The green box, not the stove pipe."

"Good." Clemence flicked a thumb-sized black spot from a turnip onto the floor. "You were longer than you should've been."

Elise set the excuse in her mouth and found it too thin even before it spoke. "The streets were thick by the bridge."

Clemence looked at her once, quick and flat. She had the sort of face that read damage faster than comfort. "Lucien's in the schoolroom."

"I need him now."

"Then need him quietly. Lucie's finally asleep."

Elise nodded and went.

The schoolroom smelled of chalk, lamp oil, and the damp mineral breath that came off the walls after a warm day above. Lucien sat at the narrow table with a stack of copy sheets near his elbow and one hand pressed to his mouth while he coughed into the crook of his arm. He had taken off his coat. His shirt sleeves were rolled unevenly, one forearm whiter where the cuff usually kept it from the quarry dust. On the bench behind him Lucie slept curled around a slate as if it were something soft, one scraped knee bare where her stocking had worked down.

Lucien lifted his head at Elise's step. His voice came back low from the cough, plain rather than rough.

"Did the errand hold?"

"Yes."

She did not move closer at once. The papers in her hem seemed to have changed the way cloth sat on her body. Lucien watched her the way he listened to children reading aloud, hearing the place where the line would fail before the child knew it.

"There's more," he said.

"Yes."

He glanced at Lucie, then rose and crossed to the bench. He eased the slate from the girl's arms, tucked a folded shawl under her head, and brushed chalk dust off her cheek with the back of two fingers. The gesture was practiced enough to belong to him more than politics ever did. When he straightened, his shoulder caught the lamplight and threw the old scar high near the collar into brief relief before the shirt settled back over it.

"Come into the print chamber," he said.

"No. Here."

That made him still.

"Jeanne?"

She almost hated him for using the name gently.

Elise stepped to the table, lifted her skirt just enough to reach the inner hem, and worked the folded papers loose. The motion was indecent in a room built for lessons and sleep, which was part of why she did it quickly and without apology. She laid the copies between the copybooks and the lamp.

"Read."

Lucien took the first sheet. His eyes moved once down the page, then back to the top with a harder focus.

"What is this?"

"Ferrand's wages."

He picked up the second page before answering. The silence went long enough for the drip in the far wall to seem to mark it out.

"No," he said finally. "Not wages. A schedule."

"Yes."

He read the draft deposition, then the warrant application. When he reached the margin note in blue pencil, his mouth changed shape as if the limestone air had turned bitter.

"The blanks are for where and when," he said. "Not whether."

"I know."

He set the papers down with care that was not calm. "Where did you get them?"

"From a drawer the police shouldn't have left to me."

"That's not an answer."

"It's the one I've got."

Lucien looked up. Tiredness sharpened him. Under strain his voice lost the patient braid of clauses and went simpler.

"Most widowed copyists don't walk into the Prefecture and out again carrying this."

"Most widowed copyists don't live in a quarry with a provocateur on the stair."

"Don't turn the question."

"Then don't waste time on the wrong part of it."

That landed. She saw it land. He drew breath to press harder, thought better of it, and touched Ferrand's name with one finger instead.

"You're certain?"

"I heard Vautrin say the warrant was to stay undated until Ferrand earned it. Then I read the rest."

"You heard Vautrin."

"Yes."

He leaned back a fraction, not enough to step away from her, enough to admit distance had just entered the room.

"Why tell me?"

Because she had seen Lucie's copybooks listed beside powder. Because sixty francs had been put against the act of fouling the press room. Because if she stayed quiet now the quarry would become a case file with children in the appendix. Because Vautrin's blue pencil had made something in her feel skinned.

What she said was, "Because Ferrand isn't trying to watch you. He's trying to furnish the room for a search."

Lucien looked at the warrant again. "Powder, detonators, chemical materials. He means to bring the thing himself if he has to."

"Yes."

"And name me as the man who asked."

"Yes."

He gave a short breath that was almost a laugh and had no amusement in it. "He'll have trouble getting me to ask politely."

"He doesn't need your manners. He needs your cellar and one foolish witness."

"Abel."

"Abel, if he can. Anyone younger and hungry enough to mistake being used for being chosen."

Lucien's hand went to the stack of copy sheets beside the lamp, not to protect them exactly but as if his body already meant to count what could be moved. "Why not bring this to Clemence first?"

"Because you'll decide quicker. Clemence will ask better questions, and I don't have answers I can live with tonight."

That was too true. It stood between them like a third person.

He folded the payment sheet once, then unfolded it again. "If I ask how you know that corridor, will you lie?"

Elise looked at the lamp chimney, where soot had made a gray crescent above the flame. "Probably."

He nodded, a single exhausted movement. "Then I'll ask something else. Is there any part of this you're shaping to drive us where somebody else wants us?"

"No." She heard how fast the answer came and made herself add the rest cleanly. "No. If anything, I'm too late."

Lucien studied her face with the same listening look he gave a room when everyone in it was pretending not to be afraid. She let him. A lie could survive scrutiny better than eagerness. At last he asked, "What do you think Ferrand expects next?"

"Access. He wants the press room, the stores, the places where paper and metal make you look organized. If he can't find explosive material there, he'll put it there. If he can't charm Abel into handling it, he'll make the boy feel cowardly for refusing."

"And the warrant?"

"One week, maybe less. Vautrin wrote that in the margin."

"So he already knows where we're hidden."

"He knows enough." Elise kept her hands flat on the table because they wanted to close. "Enough for a search if Ferrand gives him something that shines."

Lucien's gaze dropped to the listed objects again. Hand press. Type cases. Correspondence. School copybooks. He touched the last words with his thumb, very lightly, as if the paper itself were dirty.

"He wants the children in it," he said.

"He wants whatever makes a clean picture in a man's skull before he writes it down."

"And you came straight back."

"Yes."

"Not to save yourself."

That was close enough to the bone that she felt contempt rise in her before shame could. "If I were saving myself, I'd have kept walking."

The answer struck harder than she intended. Lucien took it without flinching, but she saw the hurt register as plainly as if she had laid a ruler across his knuckles. He had a way of absorbing injury that made it feel meaner after the fact.

"All right," he said.

He stood and crossed to the print chamber door, listening into the passage a moment before shutting it firmly. When he came back, the room had changed. Not softened. Tightened toward use.

"We move the press," he said. "Tonight if Nadir is back before full dark. Before dawn if not."

Elise let out breath she had not known she was holding. Relief felt less like air than like one strap loosening around her ribs.
