"Split the type cases," she said. "Don't send them all through one passage. And move the address scraps with the school papers. If they find one without the other, they'll write whatever link they need."

His eyes flicked to her. "You sound very sure of what they'll write."

"I know what they'll write."

"Yes," he said quietly. "That's part of what troubles me."

There it was again, not knowledge but the edge of it.

He picked up the pages and held them toward her. "These don't stay in the print room."

She took them back. "They won't."

"Can you keep them hidden?"

"Yes."

"Then do it well."

She folded the copies smaller, feeling their thinness fight under her fingers. "Ferrand can't know you know."

"No."

"If he feels the door closing, he'll rush."

"So will Abel, if he smells a secret."

"Then keep them apart."

Lucien gave her a look almost like weary amusement. "You're fond of the easier half of other people's tasks."

"Moving iron through those passages isn't the easier half."

"No," he said. "It isn't."

For one moment neither of them moved. Lucie slept on the bench with her mouth slightly open, one hand curled under her chin. Water ticked in the wall. Far off, someone laughed in the sleeping gallery and got hushed. The whole refuge seemed to hang there on the thin fact of not yet being searched.

Then Lucien opened the door and called softly down the passage, "Clemence."

She answered at once, as if she had been listening from just beyond sight. When she came in, she had the knife still in one hand and a strip of turnip skin caught to her wrist. Her eyes went from Lucien to Elise to the papers in Elise's hand.

"What now?"

"Wake Nadir when he comes in," Lucien said. "Find sacks, extra lamp oil, and whatever cloth we've got left for wrapping the type. We move the press before morning."

"On whose bright idea?"

"Mine."

Clemence did not believe that for a second. Her face narrowed another notch.

"That takes four backs we don't have."

"Then we borrow from sleep."

"And Ferrand?"

Lucien's mouth thinned. "Ferrand stays out of the deep rooms tonight."

Clemence shifted her knife to the other hand. "You're finally listening to the washed bastard?"

"I'm listening to necessity."

Her gaze landed on Elise again. Not accusation yet. Not forgiveness. Something meaner than either because it still had work to do. "If necessity starts giving orders in a clean skirt, I hope it can carry iron."

"I can," Elise said.

Clemence gave one curt nod, as if strength at least was a currency she recognized, then disappeared into the passage already calling for sacking and hands.

Lucien listened to her voice recede through the stone bends. When he turned back, his own had gone quieter.

"This buys us time," he said. "Nothing more."

"I know."

"And Jeanne." He stopped there long enough that she felt the missing question gather weight anyway. "When I ask the rest, don't insult me with a small lie."

Elise looked at him, at the chalk dust on his sleeve and the fatigue hollowing the skin under his eyes, at the hand that had just moved a sleeping child before it moved a press. The truth pushed once against the back of her teeth and stayed there.

"Then don't ask tonight," she said.

His expression altered so slightly another person might not have seen it. She did. Hurt again, and a decision not to spend it yet.

"No," he said. "Not tonight."

He crossed to the wall and began clearing the children's slates from the shelf where they would be in the way once the type cases came through. Practical motion. No rhetoric. His cough caught him halfway through the second armful and bent him double for a moment before he mastered it.

Elise tucked the folded copies back into her hem. The brass marker met them with a small hard knock against her calf.

In the passages outside, the quarry had begun to wake in a new rhythm: Clemence's brisk voice, Nadir being sent for, someone cursing softly over sacks, the first scrape of a crate dragged where it did not usually stand. The state still touched Elise's leg through brass and paper while, all around her, the refuge moved because she had chosen not to keep quiet.


# Chapter 10

By the time they got the screw free, Elise's palms were striped raw where the rope had burned through the chalk dust. The press had always looked smaller when it stood in its corner with the ink balls hanging beside it and a proof sheet pinned crooked to the wall, only another object the quarry had learned to keep alive. Once Lucien and Nadir had lifted off the frisket and unpinned the tympan, once the chase was set aside and the little drawer of furniture emptied into a flour sack, it turned ugly. The body of it had weight in the wrong places. The copper cheeks were cold with seep and old grease. It did not want to be carried.

Clemence stood over the ledgers on an upturned crate, apron tied tight over her skirt as if this were still laundry and not flight. She wet her thumb before every page turn although the lye had cracked the skin there into white seams. Her fast face moved over names, subscriptions, the book of bread tallies, the wrapper of forged papers, the list of front addresses. She made two piles, what could be replaced and what would hang somebody if the police got their hands on it.

"That one," she said, tapping a narrow account book with a red thread down the spine. "Not because of the figures. Because Berthe writes to us in the margins when she has no other paper."

Nadir took it from her and slid it into an oilcloth bundle. He always stood a little side-on, as if rooms had taught him not to give them his whole chest. Tonight there was a folded scrap of blue handbill tucked into his sleeve, something he had started turning into one of the small paper animals he made for Lucie when the child had to sit quiet. It had gone soft with sweat.

Abel had shed his jacket but not his theater. His shirt clung dark between the shoulder blades, and one suspender had been mended with thread too bright a blue for the rest of him. Printer's burns freckled two fingertips. He kept opening and shutting his hands as though he wanted the night to notice he was suffering.

"Ferrand said rollers," he muttered, looking at the stripped frame. "If we'd got the rollers under it earlier, we wouldn't be standing here all night like draymen."

Nadir did not look up. "Then Ferrand can come back and be a roller."

"He's finding oil."

"He's finding his own skin," Clemence said. "Same as he always does when there's weight."

Lucien coughed into his wrist, waited the cough down, then bent again over the press bed. He had tied a rag around one palm where the metal had pinched him earlier. Damp had darkened the hair at his temples and made the silver there show harder. "Ferrand isn't moving this machine," he said. "We're the ones moving it. Abel, get the second pole under. Nadir, take the records through first with Clemence. Jeanne, stay here a moment."

The false name still made her turn a fraction late when he used it. Elise crossed the room anyway. The floor was slick with old ink and limestone paste. In the lamp's weak circle the press looked less like an instrument than like part of the quarry itself, something dug out of the wall and taught to make words.

Lucien put his shoulder against one cheek of the frame and tested the lift. The effort brought a tightness around his mouth that had nothing to do with temper. "We'll take the body to the survey pocket behind the schoolroom," he said, low enough that the room had to lean toward him. "The type goes after. The records last. We don't use the main turn by the wine stair. If Ferrand has talked to anybody aboveground, that's the first place they'd think to watch."

Abel straightened. "If Ferrand has talked, we're already fucked."

Clemence's head snapped up. "Watch your mouth."

He colored like a child and pushed on because shame always made him louder. "I'm saying what everybody's thinking. He knows where the chamber is. He knows what sits in it. We ought to be asking how Mlle Morel suddenly came back from town full of urgent wisdom about moving presses and hiding names."

The room went still in the way quarry rooms did, not empty but listening. Water kept dripping somewhere beyond the wall. Elise had known this would come. She had heard it in the changed texture of Abel's politeness all evening, in the way Clemence had handed her a lamp without letting their fingers touch.

Lucien did not look at her first. He looked at Abel. "We can ask that question after the machine is out."

"And if the answer matters before then?"

"Then it still matters after, only with the press intact."

Abel gave a short, disbelieving laugh. "That's convenient for her."

Clemence's voice cut in, brisk and exact. "Convenient or not, I don't like guessing in the dark. She carried the money once. That bought her another week. It didn't buy her the keys to every room we have."

Elise could have said that she did not want the keys. She could have said that if she had wanted them, the state would already have them. Both answers were true and useless. She kept her mouth shut because the first rule of surviving a room like this was to make other people spend themselves before you did.

Lucien set both hands flat on the copper cheek. "Jeanne warned us before Ferrand could put his hand on the records. She's here, and he isn't. Tonight that's the arithmetic. She takes the front lift with me."

Abel stared. "You'd put her on the blind side?"

"Yes."

"You don't even know where she got it."

Lucien's eyes came to Elise then, not warm, not cold, simply level. "No. I don't. I know she came back when walking away would have been easier. I know she has worked every ugly job we've put in front of her. I know the front is the hardest place, which is why she gets it if she wants it."

The choice was public, and because it was public it cost him more than any private kindness could have. Elise felt the cost of it like another weight set into her hands.

"I'll take it," she said.

Clemence blew out a breath through her nose. "Then don't be brave in a foolish way. Be useful."

"She's had practice," Nadir said, and if there was a hook in it, it was a light one. He took the first bundle of records from Clemence and slung it under his arm. "Come on, Abel. Unless you want to stand here and be offended till dawn."

They worked the poles through the frame and wrapped them with sacking where the wood would bite. The first lift nearly failed before it began. The press stuck to the floor with old grime. Lucien counted, they heaved, and the whole body rose no higher than a hand's width before dropping back with a clang that made lamp flames jump.

Clemence swore, not prettily. "Again. Before the whole quarter hears."

The second lift came up true. Copper ground against rope. Elise took the left front, Lucien the right. The blind side meant going backward up the first six cut steps out of the print chamber, heels feeling for stone worn hollow by years of wet boots. Behind them, Nadir and Abel carried the rear poles, taking the heavier share on the slope. The machine moved an inch, then another. Her shoulders screamed at once. Weight ran from the pole through her collarbone into her teeth.

"Left foot," Lucien said, his voice pitched for her and nobody else. "There's a broken edge on the next one."

She found it with her heel, felt the stone crumble, corrected. Chalk grit slid under her sole. The press shifted and all four of them made the same involuntary sound, a short grunt dragged out of the body before pride could stop it.

The passage tightened after the stairs. That was the bad part, not the load itself but the turning. The quarry had not been cut for machines. It had been cut for stone and then for bones and then for people hiding from the law. The ceiling lowered. A buttress of untouched chalk bulged from the wall where the route kinked toward the schoolroom. They had measured it twice with a length of string and still the press looked too broad.

"Tilt on my count," Lucien said.

Abel answered too quickly, already breathless. "I've got it."

Nadir said, "That's not the same as having it."

They tilted. The right cheek cleared. The left caught with a scrape that went through Elise's spine like a knife. Copper shrieked on stone. The frame lurched back toward the rear.

"Hold," Lucien said.

Abel lost his grip for half a second. Half a second was enough. The rear pole dropped against his shoulder, and the weight shot forward into Elise and Lucien. Elise's heel slid. She slammed her free hand against the wall, skinning it open, and felt the whole machine coming down into the narrow place where there was nowhere for a body to get out of its way.

Lucien braced and took it on the bad shoulder with a sound he tried to swallow. Elise saw, in one bright stupid flash, the newspaper paragraph Vautrin wanted: illegal press discovered, radical schoolmaster arrested, documents seized, case complete. Then she stopped seeing paragraphs and looked at stone.

"Higher on the back," she snapped. "Not down. Higher. Nadir, shove his side. Abel, if you drop this again I'll break your fingers myself."

Abel made a hurt, furious noise, but fear took the boast out of him. Nadir set his shoulder under the rear pole and drove upward. Lucien obeyed before pride could argue. The press rose enough for Elise to wrench her body sideways and drag the left cheek past the bulge of chalk. Copper grated, then slid free.

For three breaths nobody moved except to keep the thing from killing them.

Nadir let out air. "Well. She means it."

Abel panted behind them. "I slipped."

"Yes," Clemence said from down the passage with the lamp. "We all saw."

Lucien's face had gone pale under the quarry dust, but his voice stayed even. "We're through the worst turn. Again."

That was how the night went. Nothing cleanly finished, only one bad angle after another. The survey pocket had once held tools or stolen wine or bodies; now it was the last chamber on a mean little branch behind the schoolroom wall, dry enough for paper and low enough that tall men had to stoop. They could not carry the press there whole. They had to set it down twice on the route, each landing a controlled collapse with knees buckling and rope yanking skin away in strips. Elise's arms trembled so hard by the second rest that she had to lean her forehead against the copper and pretend she was only catching breath.

Past the schoolroom, they went by the alcove where the children slept behind hanging blankets. One shifted at the noise. A small face appeared in the gap, Lucie with one braid undone and chalk still ghosting one knee above her stocking.

"Mama?" she whispered. "Are they taking the wall away?"

Clemence did not turn soft. She never did while there was work in front of her. But she crossed the distance in two steps and tucked the blanket higher around the child with those soap-rough hands. "No. Go back under. It's only grown people being idiots with a machine."

Lucie looked past her at Elise and Lucien bent under the load. Fear made her fix on the practical thing, as it always did. "Will there still be lessons in the morning?"

Lucien, carrying the front with the pole jammed across his chest, managed to answer. "If this doesn't kill me, yes."

That got a breath that might have been a laugh out of her. She disappeared. Clemence stayed one extra second with her hand flat to the blanket, then lifted the lamp again and said, "Move."

They got the body into the survey pocket at last and lowered it onto folded sacks. Elise's knees nearly failed when the weight left her. Her arms kept holding a moment longer, stupid with effort, before they remembered they were empty.

There was no triumph in the room, only more work. Nadir and Clemence started the records across in bundles. Abel went back for the type cases with a look on him halfway between penance and resentment. Lucien rolled his left shoulder once and stopped when pain touched it.

Elise saw the stop. "You should have let me take more of it."

"You were already taking your share."

"That isn't an answer."

He wiped his wrist over his mouth. "No. It isn't."

The type cases were worse in a different way. The press body had been brute weight, honest and hateful. The type demanded finesse when nobody had any left. Each case had to stay level enough that the sorts did not slither out of their compartments and turn an alphabet into scrap. Nadir took the first two with the reverence of a priest carrying somebody else's god. Abel, angry at being watched, tried to prove he could be careful by moving too fast.

Elise saw the slip before he did. The third case struck the door stone, pitched toward his body, and the little lead letters lifted in their trays like grain hit by wind.

She dropped the ledger bundle in her hands and drove her forearm under the falling edge. The case slammed into bone hard enough to light her vision white. She rammed her hip against the wall and held while Nadir caught the other side.

A rain of sorts jumped anyway, peppering the floor. Tiny e's and a's bounced into the chalk dust. One upper-case R spun to a stop by her shoe.

"Slowly," Nadir said, though his own breath had gone sharp. "Slowly, for the love of print."

Abel's mouth opened and shut. "I had it."

Elise looked at the scattered lead, at the compartments still mostly intact, at the blood beginning to run from a split on her forearm into the grime. "No," she said. "You didn't."

It came out flatter than anger, which made it land harder. Shame shut him up at last.

They knelt in the dirt to gather the spilled letters. The work forced them close. Lead chilled her fingertips. Nadir sorted without seeming to look, flicking pieces back to their boxes with infuriating grace.

"Here," he said, dropping a bent comma into her palm. "That one would have wandered all night."

"Thank you."

He glanced at her arm. "You catch type the way other people catch knives."

"Type cuts cleaner."

The corner of his mouth moved. "That's a printer's answer."

"It's a practical one."

"Same thing on a good day."

When they carried the last case in, Abel kept both hands under it and his eyes fixed on the level. Clemence followed with the final bundle of records, including the little red-spined account book and the packet of forged papers Elise herself had once risked her neck to cache. Lucien came last with an oilcloth parcel tucked under his arm, smaller than the others and held too close to be communal property. His notebook, Elise thought. Names the police had mislaid on purpose. Names that could still bleed if a clerk wrote them badly enough.

They made the new chamber disappear by degrees. Type cases under a blanket. The press body behind stacked sacks of chalk and two broken surveyor's crates. Records inside a shallow recess curtained with old canvas. Clemence moved through it all with the authority of a woman who knew exactly what rooms did for the people in them. She set the ledgers down, stepped back, changed the angle of a crate by an inch, and the whole arrangement stopped looking deliberate.

Abel hovered, wanting absolution and knowing better than to ask for it. Finally he said, "It's not much of a hiding place."

"No hiding place is much of one once somebody talks," Clemence said.

He flushed again. "That's not what I meant."

"Then mean better."

Lucien should have been tired enough to let the rebuke stand on its own, but he looked at Abel with weary pity instead of impatience. "It buys us time. Time isn't a little thing."

Abel's answer came too fast. "Only if you use it."

There it was again, the young men's quarrel with any plan that did not end in flame. Ferrand's work, crawling under the skin where speeches could not reach. Elise felt it like grit between teeth.

Nadir gathered up the empty sacks. "I'm going to sleep before our little saint discovers another philosophy in the dark."

"I don't need mothering."

"No," Clemence said. "You need feeding and a keeper, but we have had this conversation already."

That got the room's only honest laugh. Even Abel gave in enough to show his teeth. The laugh helped because it made them briefly human again, not suspects and burdens and hands on a machine.

Clemence picked up the lamp. Her face, in that light, was all work and no softness until she looked at Lucien's shoulder. "You're not carrying another damned thing tonight."

"I know."

"Do you?"

"Yes."

She turned to Elise then, measuring without liking what she measured. "You're bleeding on my floor."

Elise looked down. The cut on her forearm had made a dark paste with the chalk. "I'll wash it."

"Do that. Then sleep, if you can. We start again in six hours whether the Republic deserves it or not."

One by one they left. Nadir ducked out with his sacks and the ruined blue paper still in his sleeve. Abel followed, shoulders set hard with the effort of swallowing humiliation. Clemence paused at the entrance curtain.

"Lucien."

"Yes?"

"Don't make a chapel of this machine. It's a tool."

He gave her a tired half smile. "That's why we moved it."

"Good. Remember."

Then she was gone too, her steps brisk on the chalk. The light she took with her narrowed the room to the single lamp left on an overturned ink crate. The press, blanketed and hidden, gave off the faint sour smell of metal that had known too many hands.

For a while neither of them spoke. The quarry settled around them. Water dripped. Somewhere in the sleeping gallery a man rolled over and coughed into his blanket. Above all of it, too high and far to matter until it suddenly did, carriage thunder moved over the city streets.

Elise sat on one of the folded sacks because her legs would not hold another minute of standing. The delayed shaking had started in her hands. Now that there was no weight to answer, her body seemed to want to go on lifting anyway.

Lucien noticed without looking directly at it. He set the lamp closer, crouched, and held out a strip of clean rag. "Give me your arm."

"You carried the worst of it."

"I asked for your arm, not a debate."

That schoolmasterly tone would have irritated her in any other hour. Now it eased something. She held her arm out. He poured a little water from the jug over the cut first. The wash stung so hard she hissed. Pink ran down into the dust at their feet.

"There's your practical courage," he said quietly. "Very impressive until it meets cold water."

"I didn't say it was noble."

"No. You rarely do."

His fingers were careful and rough at once. Chalk had settled into the hair on the back of his hand. When he turned her wrist to wrap the rag, his thumb passed over the pale burn scar inside it. He did not ask what had made it. The fact that he might have, and chose not to, disturbed her more than the question would have.

"You should sit down before you fall down," she said.

"I'm sitting down."

He had not noticed he had eased onto one knee to save the shoulder. When he shifted, pain crossed his face so quickly another person might have missed it. Elise did not.

"Show me," she said.

"There's nothing to show."

"Then that makes us both liars."

He looked at her properly then, tired enough that pretense had thinned out of him. "You say that as if it were intimate."

"Tonight it may be."

For a moment she thought he would refuse. Then he sat back against the chalk wall and began unbuttoning his shirt with one hand. His left shoulder moved more stiffly than the right. When the cloth slid down, she saw the older scar high above the shoulder blade, white and puckered where the bullet had gone in, and beneath it the bruise the pole had already started to print into him in darkening bands.

She reached before she had decided to. His skin was cool with drying sweat and powdery with chalk. When her fingers touched the bruise, his breath caught.

"That bad?" she asked.

"Only when a woman with no mercy puts her hand on it."

"Poor man."

"Unspeakably."

The dry wit in it made her want to laugh and do something less harmless than laugh. She fetched the water again, wet a corner of cloth, and wiped the chalk away from the bruise with small strokes. He stayed still for her. The room had narrowed further to lamp hiss, water drip, and the scrape of cloth on skin. There was nothing else.

"You didn't have to defend me in front of them," she said at last.

"I know."

"That sounded expensive."

"Most things worth doing do."

She looked at the bruise instead of his face. "You still don't trust me."

He took his time answering. That was one of the things about him. He never made a show of patience; he merely had more of it than other people. "I trust some things about you," he said. "Not all of them. I suspect the missing parts would matter if I knew them. I also think Abel is young enough to mistake suspicion for intelligence, and I won't let Ferrand's filth decide the shape of our nights."

The phrase "our nights" settled low in her body in a way it should not have.

"You make that sound almost domestic," she said.

"God forbid."

"You're tired."

"Very."

"And still pompous."

"A little."

She smiled before she could stop it. He saw the smile and did not answer it with triumph, only with the smallest softening at the corners of his mouth. The softness made him more dangerous than beauty would have.

She finished wiping the bruise and left her hand there. Under the skin she could feel the delayed hammer of his pulse. His shirt had fallen open across his chest. The hollow at the base of his throat was gray with chalk. She knew the smell of printer's ink on him now, the damp wool, the labor-salt. She knew too much and not enough.

"How did you learn to carry like that?" he asked.

The question was gentle. That made it sharper.

"The same way everybody learns," she said. "By being given heavy things and no choice."

"That's an answer. It's not the whole answer."

"No."

"Will you tell me the whole of anything tonight?"

She could have lied with more care than that. She was too tired for care. "Probably not."

He nodded once, accepting the wound instead of pretending it was not one. "Then I'll settle for what you can manage."

The pity in that would have driven her from the room. The absence of pity kept her there. He was not absolving her. He was making space she had not earned.

Elise set the cloth down. "If this is still comradeship," she said, hearing how private and sharp her own voice had gone, "it's a filthy form of it."

"No," he said. "It isn't that anymore."

He did not move first with his hands. He moved with the look, with the decision not to look away. She crossed the distance because if she let him come to her with that patience she would lose what remained of her nerve. Her mouth hit his harder than she meant it to. His back struck the wall. The breath he let out was almost a laugh and then not a laugh at all because she kissed him again and his good hand came up into her hair.

There was limestone grit between their mouths. She tasted it, and the salt under it, and the fact that he had been carrying half a machine an hour ago. None of it felt separate from the work. His hand slid down the back of her neck, not gentle, not rough, simply certain. When he kissed her jaw she felt the delayed tremor run through herself again, but lower now, lower and meaner.

"Jeanne," he said once, against her cheek, as if asking whether the name would hold.

It did what it always did. It answered for her. She put her hand over his mouth for a second, not to silence him but because the false name hurt more when it was tender, then moved the hand down and began unfastening the rest of his shirt.

He watched her do it. That nearly undid her. Men watched for vanity, for permission, for advantage. He watched as if seeing what she chose were its own fact. When the shirt opened she bent and put her mouth on the old bullet scar first. His whole body tightened. It was not recoil. It was memory.

"You don't have to be kind to it," he said, voice low and unsteady.

"I wasn't intending kindness."

That got an honest sound out of him, half breath, half broken laugh. She bit lightly at the skin beside the scar. He took the back of her head in his hand, not pushing, not restraining, only holding because his body had decided to need something.

When his fingers found the buttons at her bodice, they fumbled from fatigue. She pushed his hand away and did them herself, faster, annoyed by the cloth, by the hooks, by the whole expensive architecture of female respectability that still sat between her skin and what she wanted. He helped with the last of it, sliding the loosened bodice and chemise from one shoulder, then the other. The cooler air in the chamber made her nipples tighten at once. His eyes dropped there and darkened in a way that was almost anger.

"That look," she said.

"What look?"

"As if you'd like to blame me for something."

"I would like," he said, the schoolmaster stripped out of his voice now, "to stop talking for one minute."

She kissed him again and let him have the minute. His hand came over her breast with a roughness that made sense after the whole night. His thumb dragged across the nipple. The pull of it shot straight through her, sudden enough that she made a sound and hated herself a little for how quickly it came.

He heard the hate in it before she could hide it. "None of that," he murmured.

"None of what?"

"That punishment you keep for yourself."

"You don't know anything about that."

"Enough."

She should have answered. Instead she unfastened his trousers. The effort of bending over him sent pain through her shoulders, and the pain only sharpened the want. He hissed when she got her hand inside, then closed his eyes with a discipline that looked almost pious until she moved again and took the piety out of him.

There was no clean place to do it. The floor was stone. The wall was worse. He shrugged out of his coat one-armed and spread it over the folded printer's blankets, swearing softly when the bad shoulder protested. She pushed him back onto it and climbed over his lap before he could waste another word on courtesy.

Under her skirts the night still clung cold to her legs. He got both hands to her thighs and stopped when he felt the tremor there.

"Are you cold?"

"No."

"Afraid?"

She could have lied. "Yes."

He nodded as if fear were no insult. "So am I."

That, more than tenderness would have, made it possible. She leaned down and put her forehead to his. "Then don't be careful with me like glass."

"You're not glass."

"No."

"But I'm trying not to hurt you."

"Lucien." She caught his wrist and pulled his hand higher, between the split panels of her drawers. "Then do something useful."

The first touch there nearly folded her in half. She had been tight with strain for hours. Want had been sitting under the strain, mean and bright, waiting for the room to empty. His fingers found how wet she already was and paused on a breath that changed him. She felt the change under her own hand too, felt him harden fully against her palm.

"You came here angry," he said.

"I came here carrying a press."

"That too."

His thumb worked slow once, then again, learning the pressure that made her thighs jerk around him. The wall scraped the back of her shoulder through the loosened chemise. His mouth was at her breast, then lower on her ribs, then back to her mouth because the space did not let him go far and neither of them wanted far. She kissed him hard enough to split the inside of her lip on his tooth and tasted blood with the chalk.

When he pushed the drawers aside and touched her directly she bit his shoulder to keep from making noise. He sucked air through his teeth, whether from pain or pleasure she could not tell. It was probably both. Her body had gone from trembling to a terrible, focused ache. Every small movement of his hand made the ache narrow and sharpen.

"Look at me," he said.

She did. That was worse. He was not taking refuge in the dark. His gaze stayed on her face while his fingers moved between her legs with slow, exact patience that had nothing clean or instructional in it. It was hunger disciplined into usefulness. She rocked against his hand because the alternative was to stop breathing. When the pressure finally broke and went through her, it went through violently, not sweet at all. She buried her face in his neck and came with her teeth in the cloth of his shirt where it had fallen under him, knees shaking, the rough taste of old soap and sweat in her mouth.

He held her through it, hand still hard at her hip. When she lifted her head, shocked by how exposed her own face must look, he kissed the corner of her mouth where she had bitten it and tasted the blood there without comment.

"Still afraid?" he asked.

"Yes," she said, because at this point dishonesty would have been idiotic.

"Good."

"Good?"

"It means we know where we're standing."

She laughed once, breathless and offended, then took him properly in her hand again to punish him for sounding wiser than any man had a right to sound half-undressed on a quarry floor. The punishment worked on both of them. He swore, low and rare enough to matter.

When he entered her, it was awkward because there was no angle in the room that suited bodies and bad shoulders and too many skirts. They managed it anyway. She lowered herself onto him slowly at first, teeth clenched against the first stretch, then faster when the discomfort turned and the need outran it. His eyes shut this time. His hand spread over her waist, then slid to grip her hip with more force than he had used anywhere else.

The printer's blankets shifted under them. Somewhere beneath the sacking an edge of type case dug through and bruised her knee. She welcomed the bruise. It made the whole thing part of the same night.

He tried once to lift under her and the shoulder betrayed him. The movement broke into a gasp and a short cough that tore out of his chest. She put her palm flat to the wall behind him, braced herself, and took the rhythm over.

"There," she said, rougher than she had meant to. "Leave it."

He looked up at her, breath unsteady. "You sound pleased with command."

"I'm pleased with efficiency."

"Liar."

"Yes."

She rode him harder after that because talking had become impossible. The stone wall answered each movement with a dull scrape against her hand. The lamp flame shook and made his face lurch in and out of brightness beneath her. She could feel how carefully he was trying not to let the shoulder fail and how completely his body had gone beyond care in every other respect. When he came close, the restraint left his face so abruptly it was almost frightening. He put his mouth against her breast, not to be gentle, to keep the noise inside himself, and the sensation of that, the heat and pressure of it, sent her over again with less warning than the first time.

She came clenching around him. The shock of it pulled him with her. His fingers bit into her hip. He said her false name once, broken apart by breath, and then he was coming too, body going rigid under her, spend filling the space between her thighs while the quarry dripped and the lamp hissed and nothing in the world offered absolution.

For a while there was only the labor of getting air back. Elise stayed over him because moving seemed too ambitious. Her loosened hair had come all the way down. It clung damp to her neck. Lucien's hand, open now instead of gripping, moved once along her spine and stopped at the middle as if even tenderness had to rest.

Then the cough took him for real. It was not the warning cough from before but the deep one from the damp in his lungs. She shifted off him at once and reached for the water jug. By the time she got it into his hand he had mastered it back down, but the effort had left his eyes wet and furious.

"That's indecent timing," she said.

"I've had the same complaint myself."

He drank, handed her the jug, and pulled his shirt together without bothering to button it. She sat beside him on the blankets with her skirts still askew, chemise hanging open, feeling his wetness cooling under the split of her drawers and hating how intimate that felt. He leaned his head back against the wall. The side of his throat pulsed once, slow now.

"If you ask me anything tonight," she said, "I'll either lie or tell you something useless."

"Then I won't ask."

"That's not sensible."

"No." He turned his head toward her. "But sense had its chance before we put a press through a hole in the earth."

She should have laughed. Instead the words went into the place where guilt had been lodged all night and made it ache differently. She looked at his shoulder, at the bruise forming under quarry dust, at the body she had just ridden to pleasure under a name that was not hers.

"You make poor bargains," she said.

"Often."

"And defend the wrong people."

"Also often."

"You don't even know whether I deserve any of this."

His gaze held her there. Tired, open, no innocence in it at all. "No," he said. "I know I wanted it anyway."

That was the cruelest answer available because it refused both absolution and protection. It left the choice where it belonged.

She bent and kissed him once more, less from desire now than from the inability to bear the space his honesty made. He kissed her back just as briefly. They were both too exhausted for anything prettier.

When at last they pulled their clothes into order, it was clumsy and unspeaking. Hooks missed. Buttons went into the wrong holes and had to be redone. He shook out her bodice for her because chalk had gotten inside the lining. She tied her hair with fingers that still would not entirely steady. The room began returning to itself around them, press hidden, ledgers curtained, lamp low.

"You should sleep here," he said. "There's no sense waking the whole gallery with more footsteps."

"Clemence will love that."

"Clemence loves almost nothing at this hour."

"At any hour."

"That's not true. She loves clean linen, exact sums, and her daughter. We just fail to qualify."

This time she did laugh, a short tired sound. He eased down onto the blankets beside the wall and made space for her without ceremony. She lay because she had no good reason not to. The stone kept its cold even through the layers. Their shoulders touched. That little contact felt more dangerous than the sex had.

He fell toward sleep fast, as men who have spent the night carrying machinery often do. He did not fall all the way asleep. His breathing changed. Once, almost gone, he said, "Morning lessons are still your fault if I die."

"Then don't die before breakfast," she murmured.

"Despot."

After that he drifted.

Elise did not. Her body was too satisfied and too alert at once. Every place they had put weight into each other kept speaking. The bite of rope across her palms. The bruise rising on her knee. The soreness between her legs, not damage, just fact. Lucien beside her under the blanket that smelled of old ink and lime. Above them Paris still existed, with its files and fees and inspectors and men who would turn a room like this into evidence if they could just get their hands on it.

In the hem of the skirt twisted under her hip, the hidden brass marker pressed against her thigh through the cloth, small and hard as a coin. She could feel it while the last wet trace of him cooled between her legs. When he shifted in his sleep and his hand came to rest, heavy and trusting, over her waist, she closed her eyes against the dark and knew that whatever happened next would have to pass through flesh before it reached principle.


# Chapter 11

The spoiled proof had lost the same accent mark three times, and Nadir refused to waste decent paper on bad setting. He stood side-on to the packing table, long fingers blackened at the cuticles, turning the ruined sheet into a white bird for Lucie with the quick patience he never spent on adults.

"If she asks for a horse again," he said, folding the neck down with his thumbnail, "I'm leaving her the square and the difficulty. Birds are honest. Horses always look like ministers."

Elise cinched the twine around the parcel of primers and translated notices. "Tell her it's a goat and she'll forgive you."

"She knows the difference, which is more than I can say for the Republic."

Ferrand laughed from the doorway before the joke had finished landing. He had shaved badly that morning; a little crescent of soap still clung under one ear, fighting with the cheap cologne he poured over himself as if scent could pass for cleanliness. The black satchel rode under his arm, worn smooth at the corners from being handled more than trusted.

"If the schoolroom's done," he said, "some of us have a city to cross."

Lucien looked up from the account sheet spread beside the lamp. Chalk dust had settled in the silver at his temple. He had buttoned his shirt wrong in the hurry of the morning, one hole off at the throat, and Elise wanted with obscene force to reach across the table and set him straight. "You'll cross it no faster for sneering at the hands carrying your paper," he said. "Two stops only. The bookseller first, then the cooper's wife. Back before supper."

His voice stayed ordinary. That was the mercy and the difficulty. A few hours earlier, in the cold dark beyond the moved press, she had had her mouth against the scar under his shoulder and his hand braced at the back of her neck hard enough to leave an ache she could still feel when she swallowed. Now he spoke to her like any other pair of hands in the room. Clemence was at the flour bins. Abel was sorting type in the next chamber. Nobody could be allowed to read her face.

Nadir set the finished bird on top of the bundle for a moment, looking almost pleased with himself. "If she crushes this one too, I'll take it personally."

"She keeps them under her pillow," Lucien said without looking up. "That isn't crushing. That's curation."

Ferrand made a soft, impatient click with his tongue. "Christ, you lot could turn a hanging into a lesson."

Lucien's eyes lifted then, warm no longer. "Then try not to bring one home with you."

Ferrand grinned as if he'd been complimented. He always grinned hardest when the room soured against him. Elise had seen enough men like that in prefecture corridors, half tout and half pet dog, living off the second before someone decided to kick. He shifted the satchel higher under his arm, protective without seeming to notice he'd done it.

They went up by the wine-shop stairs into early June brightness. Warmth lay over the street, but the quarry cold stayed caught in Elise's sleeves and under her breasts. The wine-shop keeper was rinsing sour red from the floorboards above the trap; the smell of yeast and vinegar followed them out. Beyond the door the city had already begun rubbing itself awake in earnest: barrows clattering, a knife-grinder's wheel whining, horse piss drying sharp in the sun, women calling the price of cherries as if summer could be bullied into arriving faster.

The parcel weighed more than it looked. Primers, mutual-aid circulars, translated notices for foundry men who read Arabic better than French, and a wrapped packet of subscription coins sewn into the middle where a quick thief would not find them. Elise carried it against her hip. Nadir carried the second bundle and walked the way he always did aboveground, shoulders angled, making himself narrow in the crowd without ever seeming to yield. Ferrand drifted half a pace behind them, jaunty, idle, his satchel bumping his thigh.

"You don't need to come for both stops," Nadir said without turning. "Unless you've suddenly developed an interest in schoolbooks."

"I've got business near the barrier," Ferrand said. "A man who owes me for fuse wire and gratitude."

"One of those debts is imaginary," Nadir said.

Ferrand laughed again. "You're in a mood."

"I'm in Paris."

They crossed a lane where wet laundry hung from a second-story line and dripped onto the hats below. Elise kept count by habit: three uniforms at the end of the square, too far to matter; a postman; a woman with a basket of eels; two men in dark jackets leaning where men leaned when they wanted not to be noticed. One had his hat low and his boots too good for a carter. The other had the blank face of a clerk waiting to become a hand.

She saw them the moment Nadir saw them. His stride altered by less than an inch. He did not look at her. He said, very mildly, "Don't stop if the woman with the red apron asks for change. She bites."

Then the man with the clerk's face stepped off the curb and said, "Belkacem."

The name struck the air like a slap. Nadir had time to turn his head. No more than that. The second man seized his elbow, not rough at first, almost courteous, and the courtesy was what made it practiced. A uniform came in from the lane mouth. Another from behind the eel woman. The space closed like a purse.

Elise shifted the parcel to free her hand. Ferrand had already stepped back. Not running. Not startled. Simply making room, as if he'd been waiting for furniture to be moved.

"Papers," the clerk-faced man said. "And keep your hands where I can see them."

"You've got my hands," Nadir said.

The answer earned him the blow. Not a fist. A truncheon jabbed low and fast into the back of his knee. He pitched sideways into a vegetable cart, cabbages thudding against wood, his bundle splitting when it hit the wheel. Primers burst into the gutter. A sheet of the Arabic notices sailed once in the air like laundry and came down in horse muck.

The woman at the cart swore and dragged her produce back with both hands. Someone laughed. A boy shouted for his brother to look.

Elise moved before she'd decided to. She put her shoulder into the uniform nearest her and caught only cloth and bone; he turned on her with insulted ease and drove the heel of his hand into her chest. She went back into the cart edge hard enough to bark the breath out of herself. The parcel stayed in her arms because she clutched it on instinct, ridiculous and telling, primers protected while a man hit the stones.

"Leave it," Nadir said. His cheek was against the wheel rim now, one officer folding his arm higher, higher, until Elise could hear the catch in his breath. "Jeanne. Don't."

He used the alias for her and gave nothing else away. Even then.

The clerk-faced man turned Nadir's pockets out with the same expression he might have used counting receipts. A carpenter's pencil. A folded handkerchief. Two sous. The paper bird dropped last, landing in the wet strip beside the curb. The officer stooped, opened it with thick fingers, and smirked.

"Scholar and entertainer," he said. "How useful."

He crushed it back into a white wad and let it fall.

The uniform at Nadir's shoulders yanked him upright only to shove him face-first again against the cart. The humiliation mattered as much as the pain; Elise knew that rhythm too. Make the body clumsy in public, make the crowd complicit, make the arrest itself do part of the work of punishment. One woman looked away with her mouth pinched tight. Another watched openly, choosing her pears while Nadir's arm was twisted until his fingers spread uselessly against the wheel spoke. Ink marked the wood where his hand slid.

"You've mistaken him," Elise said, because she had to say something, because silence from her would have rung louder than failure. "He's carrying school papers."

The clerk-faced man spared her half a glance. "Then the schools are under poor management, madame."

Madame. Not widow, not girl. A little courtesy laid over force like varnish over rot. Elise knew that voice too.

Ferrand reached her then, not to help Nadir but to catch Elise by the sleeve before she could lunge again. His grip was quick and dry. "Enough," he murmured. "You want the whole bloody block taken?"

She jerked free. "Were they waiting?"

"They're police. That's what they do."

He didn't sound winded. He didn't sound surprised. He sounded annoyed at a timetable shift.

The officers moved fast once the crowd had formed. They half dragged, half marched Nadir toward the covered police carriage waiting beyond the lane mouth. One of them kept a hand on the back of his neck as if displaying an unruly schoolboy. His cap had gone; Elise saw the nick in his left cheek dark with sweat and dirt, and saw, more clearly than either, the one place his gaze landed before they pushed his head down into the carriage.

Not on her.

On Ferrand.

It was only a flick of the eyes. It might have meant nothing to anyone who had not spent weeks watching both men dislike each other with increasing exactness. To Elise it landed with the force of a verdict. Nadir had had one instant to spend before the carriage swallowed him, and he had spent it there.

The door slammed. The horse tossed once in the shafts. Then the carriage was away, wheels cutting through the trampled notices and the cabbage leaves and the little crowd already beginning to relax back into trade.

Elise stood in the smell of split vegetables and horse sweat and paper pulp, the shove still hot in her chest. The crushed bird lay near her boot. She bent and picked it up before the gutter took it, smoothing it once with her thumb though it was ruined beyond rescue.

Ferrand stooped for a surviving packet of primers. "No point standing here," he said. "If they've taken him, they've taken him."

She stared at him. His satchel was tucked close under his arm, dry, untouched. He had kept it clear of the whole mess with the care of a man saving his own teeth in a tavern brawl.

"You moved before they touched him," she said.

He gave her an incredulous look, as if she had accused him of breathing. "Of course I moved. I'm not an idiot."

"No," she said. "You're not."

He heard something in her voice then, because the grin went from his face. Only for a second. Then it returned, looser, easier, all appetite and shrug. "Come on, widow. Unless you've got a key to the carriage, all we're doing is furnishing the street with grief."

She wanted to put the parcel down and break his nose with both hands. Instead she gathered the salvageable papers, their edges slimed with gutter water, and walked back toward the wine-shop stairs with Ferrand beside her and Nadir already vanishing into the machine she knew too well: the clerk, the ledger, the waiting room that stank of piss and chloride, the first questions asked before the blood had dried, the specific pleasure some policemen took in crippling printer's hands because print traveled farther than fists.

She kept the crushed bird in her pocket all the way down.

* * *

By the time they got back below, street dirt had dried into a gray tide-mark on Elise's skirt. The quarry air took the heat off her skin at once. Lamp smoke, wet wool, soap, and the mineral chill of limestone closed around her again, and for one stupid second she had the animal hope that the last half hour might stop at the stair mouth and fail to follow them in.

Clemence was cutting bread in the ration room with the long knife she used for everything from turnips to string. Her sleeves were rolled to the elbow. Red, lye-bitten knuckles flashed as she brought the blade down. Lucien sat at the trestle with the account book open and a stump of pencil tucked behind one ear. Abel was at the far wall, sorting battered sorts back into the case, lips moving over a line of verse under his breath as he worked, not for display but because he liked the feel of language in his mouth when his hands were busy.

Lucie looked up from the floor where she had been drawing letters in chalk dust. One stocking had slumped down into her shoe. "Did Nadir bring the bird?"

Nobody answered fast enough.

Lucien rose first. He read it before Elise spoke; she saw the knowledge enter his face by the way his shoulders set, not by any show. "Where?" he asked.

"Near the market road," Elise said. "Two plainclothes, three uniforms, a carriage waiting."

Ferrand tossed the salvaged primers onto the table. "They had his name before they had his wrists. Clean lift. Fast work."

Clemence's knife stopped on the board. "Was he carrying anything that names us?"

"No," Elise said. "Primers. The foundry notices. Coins sewn in the wrapping."

"How much coin?"

"Not enough to make this the expensive part."

Clemence gave one short nod, the practical arithmetic already moving behind her face. Nadir's wages gone. His compositing gone. The Arabic notices delayed. One more place in the city's cracked economy where the quarry would have to pretend it still existed.

Lucien looked from Elise to Ferrand. "Tell it clean."

Elise did. The lane. The men closing in. The carriage. The papers in the gutter. She did not say that one of the officers had the precise bored tone of the anti-anarchist floor at the prefecture, or that the shove she took had been textbook restraint rather than panic. Those were pieces of knowledge with sharp edges; in a room already full of fear, she could feel them slicing toward her if she handled them wrong.

Ferrand filled the spaces she left with energy instead of fact. "What matters is they took him in daylight because they can. In front of fruit sellers and children. That's the lesson, isn't it? Not the bloody carriage model."

Lucie had gone still on the floor. Clemence wiped her hands on her apron and said, "Lucie, schoolroom. Now."

"I want the bird."

"Now."

The girl looked from one adult face to another, saw enough to understand she had been shut out on purpose, and got to her feet with the resentful care of a child trying not to cry. Elise nearly reached into her pocket then. Not yet. The paper inside had become a damp knot from her palm.

When Lucie was gone, Abel said, "Taken where?"

He had set down the composing stick without realizing it; it lay on the floor near his boot. His cuffs were too short again, wrists all bone, one printer-burned fingertip worrying at the seam of his sleeve. He looked very young and was trying not to.

"Local post first, maybe the island after," Lucien said. "If they named him in the street, they'll want the paperwork to follow the theater."

"Theater?" Ferrand barked a laugh. "They had his face in the dirt, Lucien. That's not theater to him."

"No," Lucien said. "It's theater to the people who paid for the carriage."

Ferrand spread his hands. "Fine. Call it what you like. The point stays. We keep printing primers and ration slips and they keep loading us like sacks. How many more times do you want the lesson before you let it penetrate that noble skull?"

Clemence slapped the bread heel onto a plate. "Don't start."

"Why not? Because the child can hear? The child's already hearing boots on stairs, Clemence. We all are."

"Because you've got a mouth full of fuse smoke and nowhere decent to spit it."

That drew a crooked smile from Abel despite himself. Ferrand saw it and fed it at once.

"Look at him," Ferrand said, nodding toward the young man. "At least the boy knows shame when he sees it. Nadir gets hauled off in public and what have we got? Lessons. Tallies. Another careful speech about timing."

Lucien took the pencil from behind his ear and set it down beside the open account book as if he didn't trust himself to keep holding it. "We have an arrest less than an hour old," he said. "We don't yet know where he's booked, who saw, or what else moved with it. Anyone who calls for an explosion before those facts arrives wanting something other than Nadir."

"And anyone who waits for facts while police work a man over wants clean hands more than comrades."

The room seemed to tighten by a foot in every direction. Water dripped somewhere behind the wall. Elise could hear it between the words.

Abel said, too fast, "They'll break his hands."

Nobody answered him at once. Ferrand did not look away. Elise hated him for that accuracy. Printer's hands were always a target. She had once seen a compositor on the prefecture bench after questioning, nail beds black and swelling, fingertips split so he could not feel type for weeks. Ferrand didn't need the memory to know it. He only needed to understand what frightened a room.

"Yes," he said quietly. "If they're serious, they'll start there."

Clemence swore under her breath. Not ornament, only labor and dread. "Enough."

Abel's face went white under the chalk dust. "So what do we do? Set another page? We can print a complaint in three sizes of type if that helps."

Lucien turned to him, and his voice changed. Less room in it now. Plainer. Hurt made him simpler, not softer. "We find where he is. We move what can still be moved. We don't hand the police a blast they'll use to bury every name they have not managed to steal yet."

"That's what you always say," Abel shot back. The words came running over each other. "Wait. Count. Endure. As if patience were a machine you can feed bodies into and get justice out the other side. It isn't. It just makes graves neater."

Ferrand's smile widened almost tenderly. Elise could have killed him for the tenderness.

Lucien coughed once into his fist, stopped it, and went on. "Patience isn't holy. It's expensive. That's why I spend it carefully. If we answer this with a crowd and broken glass, all we've done is let them choose the stage and charge us for the tickets."

"Jesus Christ," Ferrand said. "A phrase for the poster. Meanwhile Nadir's getting his fingers opened like oysters."

Elise said, before she could decide how much she meant to reveal, "The carriage was waiting before we turned the lane. That wasn't a random patrol finding a man with bad luck. It was arranged. A public lift. They wanted witnesses."

Ferrand swung toward her. "Exactly."

The one word was triumph, not agreement. She could have bitten through it.

"Exactly means they wanted a story," Lucien said.

"Then give them one worth fearing."

"That's not fear," Lucien said. "That's administration with better copy."

Ferrand snorted. "You've grown fond of copy. Of school slates. Of keeping a tidy little parish under the hill while they come up and down at will. Maybe the old Commune froze whatever nerve was left in you."

Abel flinched as if the insult had hit him, not Lucien. He loved Lucien. Elise had known that long before she knew he also resented him. Both truths sat naked now.

Lucien took a step closer to Ferrand. He was lean enough that anger never made him large, only exact. "Mind yourself."

"Or what? You'll lecture me to death?"

"No. I'll put you outside until you remember this room isn't your stage."

Ferrand lifted his brows, amused, and let the satchel slide off his shoulder onto the bench with a heavy thud. "There. The wicked bag's down. You can all stop staring at it and listen. This is what they're counting on, Lucien. That you'll turn every seizure into another discussion about decency. Men like Belkacem disappear because the state knows half of us are too afraid and the other half are too polite."

"I'm not afraid," Abel said.

He said it too quickly, which made it true in the wrong direction. Elise watched shame hit him the instant after the words left his mouth. He straightened anyway, chin up, as if posture could age him.

Lucien looked at him, not unkindly, which was perhaps the worst thing he could have done. "I know you're frightened," he said. "So am I. That's why I won't spend you to soothe it."

Abel went red. Ferrand smelled the blood in the water.

"Spend him?" Ferrand said. "Listen to yourself. The boy isn't flour in Clemence's cupboard. He's a man."

"Then stop shopping for him," Clemence snapped.

For one clean second no one spoke. Ferrand's grin twitched. He hated direct insult if he couldn't turn it into a joke at once.

Elise saw Abel looking not at Lucien anymore but at the satchel slumped against the bench leg, cracked leather dark with handling. His fingers had gone still on the seam of his cuff. It was the look of a hungry boy at a bakery window, yes, but hungrier than food. He wanted something the room was not giving him: permission, scale, a shape large enough to hold fear without calling it fear.

She should have said his name then. She should have crossed the room and kicked the bag into Lucien's keeping and told the whole truth about Ferrand in whatever clumsy ruin it made.

Instead she kept calculating what could be proved, what would sound like panic, what would expose the source of her certainty. She had been trained too long to wait for evidence clean enough to survive speech. Nadir was in a carriage because of one kind of waiting. Abel was staring at gelignite because of another.

Lucien drew a breath that hit the cough and failed. He pressed his fist to his mouth, mastered it, and said, "This ends here for now. Clemence, move the children deeper in. Abel, with me after supper. Elise, I want the route written down while it's fresh. Ferrand, keep that bag shut or take it aboveground. I won't have bomb talk in the schoolroom."

Ferrand bowed from the waist, mocking and almost graceful. "As the schoolmaster says."

But when Lucien bent over the account book again and Clemence began dividing bread with furious accuracy, Ferrand did not pick the satchel up at once. He went to the basin alcove to wash street dust from his hands, humming under his breath, and the bag stayed where it was.

Abel's eyes followed it.

* * *

The quarry settled badly that night. Not to peace. To an imitation of it. Voices dropped because walls were thin and children listened, not because anyone had calmed. Somewhere in the deeper chamber a baby started, wailed once, then quieted at the breast. The lamps hissed. Limestone gave back the day's heat grudgingly, so that the air felt colder the longer you stood still.

Elise was at the basin in the side alcove, rubbing the brown smear of cart filth from her skirt with soap that smelled faintly of tallow and lye. The stain had set in the weave. So had the officer's handprint in her chest; every breath still found the bruise. She wrung out the cloth and watched gray water stripe the stone.

Lucien came in without warning her. That new knowledge between them made warning impossible in some ways and necessary in others. He stopped at the threshold rather than crowding the alcove. His restraint had always been more dangerous than another man's hunger.

"Did they name him before they touched him?" he asked.

"Yes."

"You said local post first. That sounded less like a guess than I'd like."

She kept scrubbing the same place on the skirt. "A waiting carriage means paperwork already moving. They like the nearest room first. Less chance of losing a body in the street."

"A body."

She shut her eyes for half a second. "That's what the system calls a man until the clerk sits down."

Lucien said nothing. Water dripped from the cloth onto her knuckles. She wished, stupidly, that he would touch her. She was equally glad he did not.

"And Ferrand?" he said.

"He moved before the hands were on Nadir."

"I saw that much in your face when you came in."

"Then maybe see the rest."

The words were sharper than she'd meant. She heard her own stress in them at once: flat, over-clean, every syllable scrubbed. Lucien heard it too. He leaned his shoulder against the stone, not easy, only steady.

"I am seeing it," he said softly, and the full form landed because he wanted it to. "I just need the piece that can be used before I throw him out and lose whatever else he knows."

Used. There it was again, the necessary ugliness she loved him for and hated because she knew its limits. She could have told him then. Ferrand was not merely poison. Ferrand was paid poison. Vautrin's. Mine too, if you follow the line far enough. The confession rose as far as her teeth and stopped there, trapped by the old terror that once said aloud it would become irreversible, no longer something she might yet manage with craft and timing.

Before either of them could speak again, Clemence's voice cut down the passage.

"Lucien."

It was not loud. It did not need to be.

They found the ration room half assembled in panic. Ferrand was on his knees by the bench, swearing into the stone as he yanked at blankets and looked under the table as if the satchel might have tucked itself there out of malice. Clemence stood with both hands braced on the breadboard. Lucie, in her night shift and one sock, had clearly been dragged back out of bed. Chalk dust still marked one knee.

"What's happened?" Lucien said.

Ferrand came up fast, face stripped of charm at last. "The little bastard took it."

Lucien's voice stayed level. "Say what he took."

"My bag."

"Say what was in it."

Ferrand hesitated just long enough to damn himself. "Tools."

Elise said, "Gelignite. And the pistol."

Ferrand wheeled on her. "All right, yes. Enough for a job, not enough for a war. Happy?"

"No," Clemence said. "Who's missing?"

Abel's blanket lay collapsed in the sleeping gallery doorway. His jacket was gone from the peg. So was the better cap he wore when he wanted to look older. On the floor near his mattress sat an opened booklet of Hugo with a torn paper marker halfway through, as if he had meant to come back and finish the stanza after he saved the century.

Lucien crossed to the bunk, touched the cold blanket once, and turned. The cough had left his face. Anger had not. "When did you last see him?"

Ferrand spread both hands. "How the fuck should I know? I'm not his nurse."

"No," Clemence said. "You're the disease."

Lucie spoke into the gap because adults had left it open. "He wrapped the buckle in his scarf."

Every head turned toward her.

Clemence swore. "I told you to stay in bed."

"I was in bed."

"Lucie."

The child lifted her chin. Fear made her more literal, not less. "I was. Then Abel came past. He had Jules's bag and his own scarf around the metal so it wouldn't knock on the wall. I said his laces were loose and he'd fall on the north stairs and he said not to wake you because you'd make a household out of it."

For one impossible second Clemence nearly laughed. It came out as something uglier.

Lucien crouched so he was level with the girl. "When?"

"After the shouting. Before the last lamp turn. He smelled like ink and that bad cologne because he'd been near Jules's coat." She frowned, trying to be exact. "He had the pistol in the back of his trousers. I saw the wood."

Ferrand's face changed again, this time into naked fury. "Christ."

Lucie looked from him to Lucien, then to Elise. "Are they breaking Nadir's fingers now?"

The room took the question like a blow to the mouth.

Clemence moved first, catching the girl against her apron with a roughness that was almost tenderness. "Enough. You don't need that in your head."

"It's already in my head."

There was no answer to that worth giving in front of a child. Elise felt the crushed paper bird in her pocket like a second, smaller accusation.

Lucien rose. "North stairs means he wanted the courier exit."

"Or he wanted you to think that," Ferrand snapped. "He has heard enough from me to know three better places than that. Barrier office. Magistrate's door. Half the fucking boulevard if he wants a crowd."

"He heard it from you," Elise said. "That's the point."

"Don't start with me."

"I haven't yet started."

Ferrand took a step toward her. Lucien got between them so quickly the movement looked effortless until the cough caught him at the end of it. He swallowed it down and put one hand flat against Ferrand's chest.

"You stay here," he said.

Ferrand stared. "Are you insane? It's my bag."

"And your mouth helped send him out with it."

"Then let me fix it."

"No."

The word dropped like a stone into water. Simple. Final. Ferrand's nostrils flared. Elise could almost see the calculations moving behind his eyes: whether to strike, whether to laugh, whether the room had turned too far against him for either to work.

"You think he'll listen to you?" he said. "To more patience? He wants a history, Lucien. Not another errand."

"He wants not to feel helpless," Lucien said. "You sold him spectacle as if that were manhood."

Ferrand sneered. "And you sold him waiting as if that were wisdom."

Clemence shifted Lucie behind her. "Argue later. Move now."

No one trusted the same facts. Ferrand thought Abel wanted a crowd big enough to make news. Lucien thought he wanted an act that could be aimed at the men who had taken Nadir. Clemence thought grief itself would choose badly if given shoes and dark. Elise thought Ferrand had been whispering too many possible scripts into the boy's ear for any of them to know which one had stuck. A barracks gate. A magistrate's carriage. A police clerk on a narrow stair. A cafe full of the wrong people. That was the true danger: not a plan, but a young man full of bad theater and enough explosives to make any impulse fatal.

Lucien turned to Elise. "You come with me."

Ferrand barked a laugh. "Her?"

Lucien did not look at him. "She was there. She sees streets cleanly when she's frightened. Better than either of us."

It was praise. It was also a wound. Elise took it anyway. "I'll go."

Clemence said, "If you find him, don't let him start talking in public. Get hold of his hands first."

Only she could have made it domestic and tactical at once. Elise nodded.

Lucie wriggled around her mother's hip. "Give him the bird."

Elise had forgotten the thing for one stunned instant. She took the crushed paper from her pocket. It was soft now, ruined past folding. Lucie looked at it and knew.

"They stepped on it," the child said.

"Yes."

Lucie took it anyway, flattening it on her palm with grave care. "He'll make another."

No one corrected her. Not because anyone believed it. Because the lie was too small and too merciful to waste breath on.

Lucien had already snatched his coat from the peg. He shoved his arms through the sleeves and winced as the cloth caught at the old scar high in his shoulder. Elise saw it because she had seen the skin itself there the night before; there were costs now even in looking. He checked his pockets with quick schoolmaster precision: coins, notebook stub, no weapon worth naming. He never liked pistols. He liked them less in confined places.

Ferrand moved again toward the stair. Lucien stopped him with the same flat hand, harder this time. "If I find you ahead of me," he said, "I'll assume you aren't trying to save him."

Ferrand's grin came back in pieces. "Then you'd better be quick, old man."

Lucien's hand tightened once in the front of Ferrand's shirt, bunching the cloth at the collarbone. Not enough to make a scene of it. Enough that Ferrand felt the strength still left in him. "Stay under the hill," Lucien said.

Then he let go and turned away before Ferrand could choose a response that would keep his dignity intact.

Elise followed Lucien up the passage at a run. Behind them the ration room was already changing shape around the absence: Clemence dragging Lucie back toward bed, Ferrand swearing low and venomous, the lamp hiss, the chalky scrape of someone's boot on stone. Ahead lay the wine-shop stairs and after them the city, indecently alive in the dark.

When they came out aboveground, night had not made Paris quieter. It had only changed the register. Crockery rang from a cafe. A hand-organ wheezed somewhere near the barrier. Laughter spilled from a door and was cut off when it shut. The air smelled of dust, beer, hot horse, and the last cherries turning sweet in the market baskets. Abel was out in it with gelignite warm against his leg and a pistol in his belt and nineteen years' worth of shame, vanity, hunger, and love all looking for one place to detonate.

Lucien coughed once on the pavement, bent, mastered it, and pointed toward the split of the road without speaking. Elise took the other side by instinct. Neither of them touched the other. They moved fast enough that anyone watching might have thought they were late for something ordinary, and that was the worst part. The city kept its evening while somewhere inside it a boy with burned fingertips went looking for a blast he could mistake for courage.


# Chapter 12

By nine the stalls on rue Daguerre had narrowed the street to a damp throat of cabbages, rabbit fur, wet straw, and women's skirts, and Elise hated the market for continuing. A boy of nineteen had gone into Paris with a satchel of gelignite and a pistol, and still the fishwives slapped silver bodies onto boards as if the day had been promised to them in writing. A man in shirtsleeves stood arguing over the price of onions. Two little girls in matching straw hats dragged a hoop between the puddles. Somewhere behind the awnings a butcher laughed so hard he had to lean his forehead against a hook.

She and Lucien had been aboveground since first light. They had already walked the length of one lodging street, crossed two printer lanes, and doubled back from a dead rumor near the boulevard where a cooper swore he had seen Abel's coat and then admitted, with mild annoyance, that every hungry boy in the quarter owned the same coat. The city kept putting its shoulder in front of them. Barrows jammed the crossings. An omnibus took half an age to lurch clear. A flower seller had set her buckets right across the mouth of an alley and would not move them until she had finished trimming the stems. Elise found herself resenting each ordinary delay with the same vulgar ferocity she might once have reserved for a police clerk.

Lucien moved beside her in his dark work jacket, leaner than ever in the growing warmth, quarry pale under the skin. His cough had not broken loose all morning, but she could hear it waiting in him. He had the look he got when he was counting several calamities at once and refusing all of them the dignity of panic. That refusal steadied other people. In Elise, by midday, it had started to feel like a reproach.

At the hatch, before dawn had properly taken hold above the street, Clemence had stood in her unlaced boots with her apron thrown over yesterday's skirt, one red hand cupped around a lamp chimney because the draft worried her more than the missing explosives for exactly three seconds at a time. She kept picking at the split skin beside her thumbnail, a winter crack that never fully closed even in June. "He took the satchel, the pistol, and his better scarf," she had said. "Left the blanket. Idiot." Lucie, hair only half braided and one stocking slouched around her ankle, had asked whether Abel had taken bread. That was the question a child asked when the adults in the room were too proud to say the real one aloud. Lucien had told them both to stay below and had taken the little leather purse from the ration shelf with the look of a man borrowing from hunger.

The first house they tried belonged to a widow on rue d'Enfer who kept violets in a chipped mustard jar because she believed flowers made men pay on time. She listened to Lucien, pinched her lower lip between two nicotine-yellowed fingers, and said Abel had not slept there in two weeks, though he still owed for one blanket and a spoon. "He said he'd settle when the printing paid," she said. "I told him printers pay in arguments." She glanced past Elise toward the street, where carts were rattling over the stones. "If he's in trouble, tell him not to bleed on the mattress. The stain never leaves."

After that they went north to a paper warehouse where Abel sometimes collected spoiled offcuts for kindling and wrapping. The clerk there was a narrow-shouldered boy with flour on one cuff from helping his sister before work and a private horror of dust; he kept blowing at the ledger rather than touching it. He had seen no sign of Abel, but he remembered Ferrand, though not by name. "The moustached one," he said. "Smells like a brothel trying to pass for a pharmacy." Lucien's mouth tightened. Elise heard in the clerk's disgust what she had already known too late: Ferrand never belonged anywhere he entered. He only managed, for a little while, to make his vulgarity look like confidence.

They crossed back toward the market because every path in that quarter crossed the market sooner or later. The heat had started to rise off the stones. Strawberries shone in shallow baskets like varnished wounds; the cherries were darker, almost black in places, their skins beginning to split. Lucien stopped so abruptly that Elise nearly walked into him.

"What are you doing?"

He was already reaching into the purse. He turned the coins over in his palm, counted once, then again, as if arithmetic might become kinder under pressure. "Buying fruit."

"Now?"

"For Lucie."

The woman behind the stall had her left thumb wrapped in linen and kept nudging rolling cherries back toward the scale with the heel of her hand because she refused to lose the use of the rest of it. A canary feather was tucked into the brim of her hat, absurdly bright against her mourning ribbon. She looked from Lucien's face to the purse and understood the whole transaction at once. "The bruised ones are cheaper," she said, not unkindly.

"Then give me the bruised ones."

She weighed out a paper cone no larger than Elise's two hands together. "For a child?"

"Yes."

"Keep them in the shade, then. By evening they'll drink through the paper."

"She'll eat them before that if we get home."

If we get home. He said it as if the clause did not deserve emphasis. Elise watched him fold the top of the paper down with more care than she had seen him give his own bandages. For an instant she wanted to tell him he could not afford this, and the thought disgusted her before it had fully formed. The purse had looked almost flat in his hand. He still spent from it as if a ten-year-old girl's pleasure at sucking cherry flesh from the stone belonged in the same ledger as explosives and police.

He tucked the fruit into the inside pocket of his jacket. A dark stem protruded through the fold like stitching. Then he set off again, and she followed.

They asked after Abel in places that had nothing theatrical about them: at a cobbler's bench where soles hung like tongues from nails; at a cafe that smelled of chicory, drains, and yesterday's smoke; at the side door of a bindery where three girls were beating dust from covers and laughing over a serialized romance one of them had hidden inside the glue invoices. No one had seen him, or else they had seen someone young, hurried, and male, which was almost the same as having seen no one at all. The city was full of boys trying to look older than their supper and men trying to look less tired than their wives.

Around noon the noise changed. It thickened, not with danger but with leisure. Shutters went up on the wine shops. A barrel organ started somewhere near the boulevard, jerking out a tune that arrived in fragments between cartwheels. A man was setting painted horses onto the pole of a small carousel, cursing softly each time a strap slipped. Elise hated that too: not the tune, not the children who would ride by evening, but the fact that the day had enough room in it for amusement. Abel had taken a roomful of death out under his arm and Paris had made space for spun sugar and brass.

They turned into narrower streets where Abel had sometimes carried wrapped sheets from the press to safer hands. Lucien knew the routes better than she did, though he coughed twice before admitting he needed to slow. He leaned one hand against a wall veined with old damp while she watched a tram of schoolboys push past, ink on their cuffs, summer energy making them rude.

"He won't go to the river," she said. "Ferrand likes edges, not open water. Too many exits for a man who wants an audience."

Lucien glanced at her. "You say that as if you've known his kind for years."
