She kept her face level. "I've known enough vain men."

"Abel isn't vain in the same way."

"He still wants to be seen."

"Yes," Lucien said, after a moment. "That's the trouble. He wants witness, and Ferrand taught him to mistake witness for scale."

He pushed off the wall and kept walking. The sentence stayed with her because it was true and because it did not spare Abel the insult of accuracy. Lucien loved the boy. He would not pretend that love had made him less foolish.

The bookseller kept a shop on rue Monsieur-le-Prince so narrow Elise had to turn sideways past the front table. Books swelled on the shelves from damp and were arranged according to an order visible only to the proprietor, who wore steel spectacles on a black ribbon and kept rubbing a spot of sealing wax between finger and thumb as if the habit had replaced prayer. His name was Borel. He had once been a schoolmaster in Poitiers, Lucien whispered before they went in, and had never forgiven any living person for it.

"If this is about payment, I told your young idiot last month that I'm not a bank," Borel said before either of them had spoken three words. His voice was cracked but not weak. "If it's about the Hugo volume, I still say he ought to have kept it. A boy should own at least one book too fine for his room."

"It's about Abel," Lucien said. "He's missing."

Borel's fingers stopped moving over the wax. "Missing from where?"

"From the place he ought to be."

"That covers most boys of nineteen."

Even now, with the afternoon slipping, the old man's dryness made Lucien almost smile. Elise wanted to shake him. Instead she stood in the smell of paper mold and dust and watched him decide whether they were worth plain speech.

"I haven't seen him today," he said at last. "Not for some time. But when he still ran packets for me, he liked the old route by Porte d'Orleans. Said the agents watched the proper road because agents adore the proper road." Borel sniffed and turned to reach under a pile of gazettes. He brought out a twine-wrapped parcel and set it on the counter as if demonstrating size. "He took bundles this shape to a cooper in Montrouge. Cut behind the barrier, past the disused octroi house. There's a cellar under it. Boys used it for cards and cigarettes before the clerks abandoned the place."

Lucien went still.

"Why are you telling me this now?" he asked.

Borel gave him a look of genuine impatience. "Because until now you asked whether I had seen him. You didn't ask whether I remembered him." He pushed the parcel aside. "And because he came in last week wanting to know whether the back door of that little tollhouse still shut. I said I hadn't made a hobby of inspecting municipal ruins."

Elise felt the day alter around her, not become faster, but narrower. The tollhouse rose in her mind with bureaucratic clarity: a gate, a cellar, a road, a state mark on a half-ruined wall. Ferrand would like the symbolism because it could be printed by morning. Abel would like it because it sounded like history. Between those two tastes a great many strangers could die.

Outside the shop the light had begun to lower, though the heat of the stones was still coming up through Elise's soles. They went south quickly now, but quickness did not mean emptiness. Clerks were out from their counters. Apprentices were shoving one another toward evening freedom. Women with market baskets bargained for the last cheap vegetables. A pair of soldiers had bought paper rosettes from a girl no older than Lucie and pinned them to each other's caps in a show of foolish tenderness. Elise kept thinking that if she stopped any one of them and told them what was moving through the quarter under one boy's arm, they would stare at her as if she were mad, not because she was wrong but because catastrophe that had not yet happened always looked indecent in daylight.

By the time they reached the roads feeding toward Porte d'Orleans, the amusements had begun to announce themselves. Lanterns were going up outside the wine gardens. The shooting gallery tested its rifles with small flat cracks that made Elise's shoulders twitch before her mind sorted them into carnival and not gunfire. Someone in a striped vest was bawling for customers at a hoop game. Accordion music leaked over a wall and tangled with the clatter of carts still coming in late from outside the city. It would have been vulgar on any other evening. Now it felt obscene and useful at once. Ferrand would hear crowd in it. Abel would hear occasion.

Lucien stopped near a low stretch of wall where the road widened before the gate. On the other side lay a strip of scrub and, beyond it, the darker block of the old tollhouse, half hidden by nettles and the angle of the lane. Not close enough yet to see whether a lamp burned. Close enough to know Borel had not invented it.

"Wait," Lucien said.

"We don't have time."

"We have exactly enough time to avoid blundering into a cellar full of powder like idiots. Sit down for one minute."

He was already coughing, the sound dragged up from deep in the chest and cut short because he would not give it the dignity of a fit. Elise hated that she obeyed before deciding to. They sat on the wall in the lengthening shadow while the shooting gallery cracked again and a child somewhere nearby screamed with delighted terror at the rise of a swing.

Lucien took the fruit from his pocket and looked at the paper. Cherry juice had already soaked through one fold in a dark bloom.

"Ruined," Elise said.

"Not to Lucie."

He opened the paper anyway. The cherries were bruised, a few burst, their flesh sticking to the white. He picked one up, wiped it once on the least stained corner of the wrapper, and handed it to her. She stared, then took it. The skin broke between her teeth. It was warm from his body.

"Before we go farther," he said, looking not at her but at the tollhouse, "I'm going to say one thing, and then I'm not saying another word about it tonight unless you force me."

She swallowed the fruit. Her mouth tasted of sugar and iron from the split skin. "All right."

"I know you've lied."

The fairground noise seemed, for one stupid instant, to recede. Elise kept the stone between her fingers until the juice slicked her thumb.

Lucien's voice stayed even. That made it worse. "Not about everything. I don't know enough for everything. But Jeanne Morel doesn't cover the whole of you, and she never did."

She said nothing.

"A copyist's widow doesn't move toward a window when the police hit a room. She doesn't count exits every time she enters a cafe. She doesn't touch the hem of her dress before she undresses, every time, as if something that matters is sewn into it. And she doesn't know men like Ferrand as quickly as you knew him."

The cherry stone clicked from Elise's teeth into her palm. She felt, absurdly, the weight of the brass disk hidden in the lining she was not even wearing now, as if suspicion could call metal through cloth and distance alike.

"You should ask the rest," she said.

"No."

The bluntness of it made her turn to him. He had not raised his voice. He looked tired enough to be transparent in the dusk.

"Why not?"

"Because if I ask now, you'll either lie because you're frightened or tell me the truth because you're tired, and I'd mistrust both by morning." He rubbed his thumb over a cherry stem, breaking it cleanly. "And because Abel is twenty minutes from doing something irreparable, perhaps less. I don't mean to spend those minutes pulling confession out of you to satisfy my injury."

"Your injury."

"Don't pretend it isn't that."

She almost laughed. The sound would have come out ugly. Injury was too neat a word for the thing sitting between them. Injury sounded like a bruise that could yellow and leave. What she had done, and not yet fully done, was a structure, not a blow.

"Then call it what it is," she said.

Lucien's mouth shifted, not quite a smile. "If I knew exactly what it was, we'd be having a different evening." He folded the paper over the cherries again, careful even now. "For tonight I know enough. Enough to see the lie. Enough to see that you still came out with me this morning. Enough to know I need that fact before I need the rest."

Need. Not trust. The distinction landed with humiliating precision.

Elise looked past him toward the lane. A cart rolled by with two boys perched on the back boards, singing something obscene in the bright careless voices of the almost grown. The song made the whole road sound younger than it was. She thought of Nadir dragged into custody, of Abel with Ferrand's satchel, of the Prefecture's appetite for a blast large enough to print into policy. She could tell Lucien now. She could split the thing open here on the wall, under the accordion and the shooting gallery and the smell of hot wine. She could say Surete. She could say Vautrin. She could say I was sent to measure the rooms you sleep in and the people you love. Then what? He would hear the rest of the night through that knowledge. He would hear her footsteps differently. He would go into the tollhouse alone or not at all.

Mercy made no room in her. It only let guilt expand.

"You shouldn't need me," she said quietly.

"That would be cleaner, yes."

"Cleaner for whom?"

"For my pride. Perhaps for your conscience, if you've still got ambitions in that direction." He said it without cruelty, which made it sting more cleanly than cruelty would have. Then he added, after a pause, "But I do need you. And I haven't stopped wanting you because I grew suspicious. I wish I had. It would've made me look wiser."

The words did not sound like absolution. They sounded worse. They sounded like a fact he had argued with and lost.

Elise turned to him fully then. The dusk had softened his face only to expose the wear in it: the silver at his temple, the hollows under the eyes, the mouth drawn thinner by the day's failures. She could have borne anger. Anger would have given her edges to push against. This quiet, with desire still alive in it, took those edges away.

"Lucien," she said, and stopped because there was no version of the next sentence that did not begin the collapse.

"Not now," he said again, softer this time.

He put one hand on the back of her neck, not tentative, not forgiving. His palm was dry and warm from the walk. Then he kissed her. It lasted only a moment, but it was not a brotherly mercy or a token laid on the forehead of the condemned. His mouth was tired, hard, wanting. The rasp of his stubble caught her upper lip. Elise tasted cherry and dust and the bitterness of the day on him. When he drew back he rested his brow against hers once, a weary pressure, as if that were the nearest he could come to taking anything back.

The shooting gallery cracked again. Somebody laughed. The world resumed its vulgar continuity around them.

Lucien straightened first. "Now. Abel."

The name brought the road back into focus. Elise looked toward the tollhouse and forced her mind onto shape, access, crowd. "If Ferrand is with him, they'll wait for dark enough to make witnesses useful. Not full dark. He'll want faces in the road, lamps, people close enough to count."

"Abel won't want an empty building."

"No. He'll want a sign."

Lucien nodded. "A gatehouse. A tax room. The city taking its bite before bread reaches the table. He'd think that meant something."

"It does mean something," Elise said. "Just not what Ferrand will let it mean."

That made him look at her sharply, hearing more in the sentence than she intended to give. She went on before he could choose whether to press. "A cellar gives them cover to set the charge. The road gives them people. The lane behind it gives Ferrand an exit if he thinks he still wants one."

"And Abel knows the place."

"From the packets."

"From feeling useful," Lucien said. "God help me."

The confession was almost soundless. She had never heard him ask God for anything.

Lantern light had begun to collect along the road in trembling pools. Beyond the scrub the tollhouse remained dark, but darkness no longer meant emptiness. It meant walls thick enough to hide behind. It meant a boy with printer's burns on two fingers, trying to look older than terror. It meant Ferrand's grin in a cellar, explaining to Abel how history liked a louder hand.

Lucien wrapped the cherries again and returned them to his pocket as carefully as if the paper still held a future untouched by the next hour. Then he stood, one hand briefly braced on the wall until the cough passed behind his teeth.

"It's there," he said.

Elise rose with him. The music from the wine garden floated over the road; a woman shrieked at a joke; a rifle cracked and won somebody a tin prize. The city had made room for pleasure, for hunger, for lies, for children waiting belowground with dirty knees and empty hands. It had made room, too, for the little ruined tollhouse near Porte d'Orleans.

"Yes," she said. "It's there."

They went toward it through the crowd before the cherries could finish soaking through the paper.


# Chapter 13

By the time they reached the disused tollhouse near Porte d'Orleans, the last of the day had gone the color of old brass. The road was still thick. Carts coming in from the southern villages stood wheel to wheel at the barrier, and people on foot kept sliding through the gaps as if impatience could thin stone or timber. A woman with a rabbit trussed by the hind legs had her basket hooked into the crook of one elbow and a sleeping child on the other hip. Two apprentices in flour-dusted sleeves were sharing a heel of bread. A carter sat on his shaft and scratched the neck of his horse with the tenderness of a priest touching a relic. The tollhouse stood just off that press of life with one shutter hanging loose and the old octroi plaque gone black with soot, as if the building had already been struck once and left to wait for the second blow.

Lucien stopped in the lee of a wall to get his breath without announcing that he needed to. Sweat had darkened the collar of his shirt. The newspaper parcel in his coat pocket had bled through; she could smell apricot before she saw the stain. He had carried the fruit all day and had still not thrown it away.

"It's him," Elise said.

She had seen the light first. Not from the broken upper room. From the low cellar slit at road level, where a blade of yellow moved across the dust whenever someone passed in front of a lamp below. The tollhouse ought to have been dark. Nobody used it now except boys taking pisses in bad weather and men who wanted a place where no one respectable would follow.

Lucien put one hand on the wall. His cough stayed where it lived these days, just behind the breastbone, and did not come out. "If Ferrand is with him, he won't have come to talk sense."

"He didn't come for sense in the first place."

Another sound reached them through the road noise. Not speech yet. A hollow thud from underfoot. Wood striking stone. Someone moving a crate or a box and trying not to let it sound like work.

Elise looked toward the barrier again. More bodies had filled the approach in the last minute alone. A seamstress with her hat pinned crooked was arguing over a fare with a cabman. A girl of perhaps twelve had a bundle of cress tucked under her arm and kept licking salt from her thumb. A municipal clerk in sleeve garters came out from a wine shop and paused to piss in the weeds behind a post. Ferrand had chosen the place as carefully as any accountant balancing columns. There was enough traffic for dead strangers, enough official stone for symbolism, and enough police within shouting distance to certify the lesson afterward.

A whistle sounded far off to the north. Short. Professional. Not alarm yet. Placement.

Lucien heard it too. His face changed, not into fear exactly but into a plainer version of itself, stripped of anything a room might once have mistaken for patience. "They're closing."

Elise felt the old arithmetic fall into place with a speed that made her sick. The whistles were too early for chance and too well spaced for some ordinary street quarrel. Ferrand had not brought Abel here merely to let him perform a fit of grief in private. He had chosen the building, chosen the hour, chosen the road, and someone on the police side had chosen how long to wait before tightening the ring. Vautrin was not hurrying to stop a bomb. He was hurrying to inherit one.

"Back door," she said.

They went through weeds and broken crockery behind the tollhouse, keeping low where the wall had collapsed into a nettled ditch. The rear entrance had lost its door years ago. A strip of warped plank leaned across the opening and scraped softly when Lucien eased it aside. Inside, the old office still held the ghost of official life: a nailed counter, a shelf stained by spilled ink, rust marks where a stove had once stood, tax numbers chalked on a wall and half rubbed out by weather and boys' shoulders. Dust lay over everything except the fresh tracks leading inward, boot soles marked in pale crescents on the floorboards.

The cellar stair yawned open at the back.

Elise heard Ferrand before she saw him.

"I'm telling you the timing matters," he said, voice low and coaxing. "If you plant it too soon, you've only frightened a clerk. Too late and the road thins. You want the carts stalled. You want the whole gate bunged up like a bottle."

Abel answered from deeper down. "Stop saying you. I know what I want."

"Do you? Good. Then stop trembling and prove it."

There was a silence thick enough for Elise to hear the road again through the cellar slit: harness chains, a horse blowing through its nose, somebody laughing because they had not yet been taught the shape of the next five minutes.

Lucien stepped forward and she caught his sleeve. His shoulder under her hand was hard with the effort of holding himself still.

"If he sees you first," she whispered, "he'll hear a father before he hears anything else."

"I'm not his father."

"He thinks you're his father when it suits him."

The words stung him. They were meant to. She would not have said them in any other room. He looked at her once in the bad light, understanding both the cruelty and the use of it, then nodded.

They went down together.

The cellar was not much larger than a laundry closet. Barrel racks lined one wall, most of them empty, and the beams were so low Lucien had to bend. The road-level slit let in a ribbon of dust-gray evening and the passing shadows of wheels and skirts. Ferrand had set a lamp on an overturned crate. Beside it lay the satchel Abel had stolen, its mouth open just enough to show waxed paper, fuse cord, and the dull metal glint of the pistol butt. The chemical smell rose out of it, sweet and wrong.

Abel stood by the stair with the pistol already in his hand. He had changed his coat since morning, or perhaps stolen another one in haste. This one was too broad in the shoulder and too long in the sleeve; when he lifted the gun, the cuff slid down over the base of his thumb. His face had narrowed in the last two days. Under the lamp he looked younger than nineteen and more used up than some men of forty.

Ferrand crouched by the crate with a length of fuse between his fingers as if he were cutting sausage. He turned at the sound of their steps and smiled at once, easy as a host whose guests had come sooner than expected.

"So you've come after all," he said. "I was wondering whether the schoolmaster would bring his widow or send her to cry somewhere safer."

Lucien did not look at him. "Abel."

The boy's mouth moved before any sound came out. Elise saw the struggle with hope and pride pass over his face so quickly it was almost indecent to witness. He had wanted Lucien to come. He had also wanted to refuse him. Those wants stood in him side by side like men with knives.

"Don't," Abel said. "Don't talk to me as if this is another lesson."

"Then don't make me watch you die for Ferrand's amusement."

Ferrand gave a short laugh. "You always did know how to start with the grave."

Elise kept her eyes on the satchel. Three steps from Ferrand. Two from Abel. The cellar slit showed a cart wheel crawling past at the height of her shoulder. If the charge went off in the road, that wheel would become spokes in somebody's face.

"He didn't bring it here for you," she said to Abel.

Ferrand clicked his tongue. "No? Who did I bring it for, then?"

"For the papers. For the police report. For every man who wants a dead quarter to hold up in the Chamber."

Abel's eyes jerked to her. "What are you saying?"

She heard Lucien take one careful breath. He knew enough now about the holes in her life to hear danger in the confidence of her voice, but this was not the moment for him to spend that knowledge.

"I'm saying he picked this place because he doesn't care who goes into the account," Elise said. "You, the clerk upstairs, the woman with the rabbit, the child asleep in the cart. It all counts the same once it's printed."

Abel flicked a glance toward the slit window before he could stop himself. Through it the rabbit woman's skirt passed in a blur of dark cloth. A child coughed. The sound was so ordinary it seemed to belong to another world.

Ferrand rose slowly, fuse still in hand. His grin did not leave, but it tightened at the corners. "Listen to her. She sees a crowd and suddenly discovers a conscience."

"I had one before you did."

"Then you've wasted it."

Lucien moved down one more step. "Abel, look at me."

"I'm looking."

"No. You're looking at the part of this you'll remember later, and you've chosen the wrong bit. Look at him." Lucien tipped his chin toward Ferrand. "He was hungry for a bomb before Nadir was in irons."

At Nadir's name the boy's grip on the pistol changed. Not tighter. Worse. The barrel dipped a fraction, then came up again with a tremor running through it.

"Don't say his name as if you've done right by him."

"I didn't say I had."

"You told us to wait."

"I told you not to do their work for them."

Abel's laugh broke in the middle. "Their work? They took him in daylight. They put hands on him in front of half the street and you wanted patience, and bread counted, and a meeting, and one more speech about not giving them what they wanted." He took a step down from the stair, toward Lucien, then checked himself when Ferrand shifted behind him. "Do you know what he said to me that morning? He said the line was crooked on the broadside because I was composing too fast. That was it. That was the last thing. And now he's in some room with them breaking him apart and you still want me to behave."

Lucien's face emptied further. "I want you not to kill a laundress and an apprentice baker for the Prefecture's convenience."

"You don't know who'll die."

"He does," Elise said.

Ferrand stopped smiling.

That, more than anything, frightened her. The charm had not dropped in panic. It had simply become unnecessary. What showed underneath was not theatrical rage but impatience, the dull contempt of a man forced to wait while other people had feelings in the way of his work.

"Careful," he said. "You'll say more than you mean."

"I've been saying less than I mean for months."

Lucien shot her one sharp look at that, but Abel was faster.

"What does that mean?" he demanded. He had gone pale in a way the lamp made meaner, green at the mouth. "How does she know what he knows?"

Ferrand spread one hand. "Ask her. I'm all curiosity myself."

Elise felt the room narrow another inch. Not from the walls. From time. The whistle sounded again outside, nearer now, answered from somewhere off to the side road. Ferrand heard it and turned his head the smallest amount, like a dog pricking at a familiar call. He knew exactly how much company was on its way.

Abel heard it too. Some part of him understood then, or nearly did. "Who did you tell?"

"No one you need fret over," Ferrand said.

That answer did more than any accusation could have done. Abel stared at him. Hurt crossed his face nakedly, too quick for performance. It was the face of a boy who had given away the last money in his pocket because an older man laughed with him and called him comrade.

Elise stepped into it before Ferrand could plaster over the crack.

"He told the police enough to get them here," she said. "He wanted a road full of witnesses and uniforms close enough to say what happened before anyone else could."

"You lying bitch," Ferrand said, almost mildly.

That word, in his mouth, was less violent than the calm around it.

Abel swung the pistol toward Elise so fast Lucien swore and reached out. The muzzle stopped level with her chest. Up close the gun looked absurd in Abel's narrow hand, not because it was small but because his wrist still had the unfinished look of youth. The printer's burns on his fingertips showed black against the steel.

"How do you know that?" he said.

She did not move. Behind him Ferrand's right hand had already dropped toward the crate, toward the matches and the cap and the prepared length of fuse.

"Because men like him are always easier to read when bodies are involved," she said. "Because he has never once talked about escape. Because every time he says cause he means audience."

Ferrand gave up the pretense all at once. "Audience is the cause, you little fool. What did you think you were doing here? Writing a sonnet? A bomb in an empty ditch is nothing. A bomb at a gate is a city. That's how you get them to answer."

"By blowing open washerwomen?" Lucien said.

"By blowing open France, if you're lucky."

"That's not France." Elise flicked her eyes toward the slit. "That's supper."

Abel did look then. The cart wheel had moved on. In its place passed the hem of a striped skirt, a pair of muddy clogs, a man's hand dangling a string of onions. Not symbols. Purchases. Hunger. He saw them and his face changed again, not toward surrender but toward confusion so violent it almost counted as pain.

Ferrand saw it too, and contempt rose cleanly through his voice. "Don't start getting tender now. Tenderness is what they beat into you before they ask for names."

"Shut up," Abel said, still looking toward the road.

"No. You're too near the thing now for one of Lucien's sermons. You light it, you put it under the sill, and you let the quarter hear what was done to your friend. If you die after, so much the better. Boys don't get remembered for hesitating."

There it was. Not martyrdom as belief, but martyrdom as copy. Ferrand wanted noise and a young face to pin beside it. Abel's grief was merely the handier fuel.

Lucien came down the final step. "Abel, put the pistol down."

"Don't come closer."

"I'm close. That's already happened." Lucien's voice had shortened with the rest of him. No schoolroom braid to it now, only stripped wood. "Listen to me. Nadir would have mocked this room before he crossed it. He'd have called the beam rotten and the plan lazy, and he'd have been right. He knew the price of paper. He knew the price of names. He didn't teach you the cases so you could blow a horse to pieces and call it fidelity."

Abel's eyes filled so suddenly Elise thought for one absurd second that he might sob. He did not. He bared his teeth instead.

"You don't know what he'd call anything now."

"No," Lucien said. "That's why you don't get to speak for him with powder."

Ferrand had stopped pretending not to move. His fingers were on the fuse. Elise saw the scratch of the matchbox against the crate as clearly as if he had struck it already.

"Abel," she said.

His gaze snapped back to her, and because he was frightened he came at her with anger.

"What?"

"If he lights it, he'll leave you here with it."

"He won't."

Ferrand's mouth twitched. That tiny thing told her more than any confession.

"He already has," she said.

The match rasped.

Lucien lunged for Ferrand at the same moment Abel jerked the pistol toward Elise's face. She moved not away but in, catching Abel's gun wrist with both hands as the shot went off. The report slammed around the cellar like a kicked door. Splinters burst from the beam above her head. Abel cried out in shock more than pain, and the pistol skidded half free in his grip.

Ferrand cursed as Lucien hit him shoulder first, driving him into the crate. The lamp slewed sideways. Flame licked high, then steadied. The satchel tipped and spilled one wrapped stick against Elise's shoe.

Abel was lighter than she had expected and harder to hold. Panic had made him all elbow and knee. He twisted like something caught in a trap and not yet aware it was dying. She smelled sweat, ink, and the cheap wool of the stolen coat. His sleeve slid over her hand and for an instant she had skin, then cloth, then skin again. He tried to wrench his arm free and she drove the heel of her palm into his wrist until the gun fell and rang against stone.

"Let go," he shouted, voice breaking. "Let go of me."

"No."

He struck at her with his free hand. His printer-burned fingers scraped across her cheek and then, in blind desperation, found the inside of her left wrist and bit down through cuff and skin. Pain shot bright and useless through her arm. She slammed her forehead into his mouth. His head hit the wall. Blood started out of his lip at once.

To her left Ferrand had got one arm clear. Lucien was hauling at him, coughing now, one bad breath after another, but Ferrand still had the fuse in his hand and the stump of a lit match between his fingers. He brought the flame down toward the cap with a care that would have suited a clerk totaling figures.

Elise saw the movement and knew all at once that there was no world left in which she could keep everyone alive. The only choice still open was arithmetic.

The satchel lay half under the crate, half free, strap looped toward the stair. If Ferrand got it up into the room above or under the road-side sill, the people outside would take the force at chest height. If it went here, below the floor, the stone might eat enough of it to spare most of them.

She hooked the strap with her foot and kicked.

The satchel slid across the damp floor toward the space beneath the stair, bumping against Abel's shin. He gave a strangled sound and dropped toward it at once, instinct stronger than reason now, whether from zeal or terror she could not tell. She went with him, still locked on his arm. They hit the packed earth under the steps hard enough to jar her teeth. The bag jammed against a post, heavy and soft with packed cylinders.

Ferrand saw where it had gone and lost the last of his composure.

"Move," he snarled at Lucien.

Lucien caught his coat, but Ferrand tore free, leaving a seam in his sleeve. One of the apricots flew from Lucien's pocket, split on the stone, and filled the cellar with a sudden ripe smell so wrong in that place it might have been obscenity. Ferrand's boot came down in the fruit and skidded. He slammed into the stair rail, recovered, and dropped toward the satchel with the smoking fuse clenched between finger and thumb.

"Abel," Lucien shouted.

The boy looked up.

That was the terrible thing. He looked up. For one second the cellar ceased to be a stage in his head and became exactly what it was: rotten beam, split fruit, damp earth, the man he loved bent over and gray with exertion, the man who had flattered him reaching over him with contempt naked on his face, the woman he half wanted to shoot gripping him so hard she shook. He saw it. Elise knew he saw it because shame went through him like a knife.

"I didn't," he said, not finishing whatever sentence had come for him.

Ferrand drove an elbow into Lucien's chest and came on.

Elise threw herself over Abel and the satchel both.

She did not do it out of tenderness. She did it because his body was between the charge and the stair and because there was no time left for gentler arrangements. Abel gave one furious animal cry and tried to buck her off. The bag shifted under her hip. Ferrand's hand closed on the strap. Lucien caught Ferrand around the neck from behind. The fuse spat sparks onto canvas. Somebody's heel hammered against wood. Dust came down from the underside of the steps in little white patters, delicate as flour.

Then the world did not become light. It became pressure.

The blast punched the air out of her chest and drove stone into her mouth. The stair above her cracked like wet timber under an axe. Something hard struck the side of her head. Something hot went through her thigh and stayed there. For an instant she knew nothing except the weight of Abel's ribs under her forearm and the astonishing softness of the satchel giving way beneath them. Then the beam above broke and the cellar filled with chalk, splinters, lamp oil, dirt, and a silence so complete she thought she had gone deaf forever.

She had not. Sound came back as a thin iron whine, and under it a horse screaming outside.

Elise tried to breathe and could not tell whether the obstruction was inside her chest or lying over her. The air was white with dust. It tasted of old lime and burst wood and blood. She coughed mud onto her own hand and saw that the hand was hers only because of the burn scar under the muck.

"Lucien?"

She could not hear her own voice. It came to her as a shape in her skull.

Something moved to her right. A figure dragged itself through the fallen stair, bent nearly double. Lucien. His hair and shoulders were white with dust, making him look suddenly ancient, quarried from the same stone as the room. Blood ran from a cut over one eyebrow, thin as ink. He was coughing with his whole body but still moving.

He dropped to his knees beside her and put both hands under her arms. His mouth formed her name. The first time she did not hear it. The second time the ringing let him through.

"Elise. Elise. Can you move?"

She tried. Pain took hold of her left thigh with a steadiness more frightening than any stab. Not a flare. A lodged fact. She looked down and saw the torn skirt, the dark wetness, the jag of rusted iron or broken hoop sunk through cloth and flesh just above the knee. Blood had already glued chalk dust into a pink paste around it.

Abel was still partly under her.

She rolled enough to see him and wished, uselessly and too late, that she had not.

He had not become anything grand. One side of his head was laid open above the ear where the stair timber had come down. His eyes were open but not seeing. The coat sleeve still covered half his hand. At the cuff, two fingertips showed with the old printer's burns intact, black marks on a hand that had gone the color of wax. His mouth was full of white grit and blood. He might have been a boy dragged out of a chalk pit after trying to hide there. Nothing in him resembled a legend.

Lucien saw where she was looking and closed Abel's eyes with two fingers that left tracks in the dust.

He did it fast, almost angrily, because the police whistles were shrilling now from the road and grief had no room large enough to stand upright in.

Ferrand lay nearer the stair. At first Elise thought the blast had thrown a bundle of rags there. Then the bundle moved. He had lost his moustache to fire on one side and most of his cheek with it. One hand, blackened to the wrist, still clutched the burned nub of fuse as if paperwork might yet be completed. He tried to pull himself forward and got only as far as smearing blood and apricot across the dirt.

Lucien rose to his feet and swayed. Ferrand looked up at him with one working eye. Whatever he meant to say came out wet and shapeless. Lucien did not answer. He kicked the pistol away from reach and bent back to Elise.

From the road came screams, but not the long tearing chorus that would have followed a full blast under the crowd. Individual voices. A horse in blind pain. A woman crying out for her son. Men shouting for water, for room, for a doctor, each in his own throat. The floor had buckled under the slit window and showered the road with timber, glass, and dust. It had not ripped the line of carts open. The stone had taken the worst of it. The tollhouse had kept its dead mostly to itself.

Ferrand made a sound then, a low gargle full of hatred. He was trying to drag his ruined body toward the daylight.

Elise found enough breath to speak. "Don't let him out."

Lucien looked at Ferrand. Then at the way the cellar wall had split, showing the road through a new crack as wide as a hand. Then at the blood on Elise's skirt.

"He isn't going anywhere."

Ferrand's one eye moved between them, still calculating. Even now. Even with his jaw hanging and the skin of his hand gone. Elise understood with cold disgust that if he had possessed one more breath clean enough for words, he would have spent it on the story rather than on pain. He had wanted a useful atrocity. The blast had killed him in the middle of his own accounting.

His burned hand opened. The stub of fuse slipped free. After that he did not move again.

Boots thundered above.

"Here," a man shouted. "Behind. Behind the house."

Another whistle answered almost at once, so close now it seemed to come from inside the broken wall.

Lucien dropped beside her again. "Listen to me."

She turned her head. Dust cracked at the corner of her mouth.

"I need you up."

"Abel?"

The question was stupid and he treated it as if it were not.

"Gone."

She looked past him at the boy's hand where the sleeve had fallen back. At nineteen the bones were still too fine. He had spent half the book trying to widen himself with rhetoric and bad company. Death had stripped him back down to apprenticeship.

"The road?" she asked.

Lucien listened. A wagon wheel groaned outside. Somebody sobbed and kept sobbing. No general panic. No collapsing wall of bodies. Not what Ferrand had wanted.

"Still standing," he said. "Come on."

He snapped the iron out of her thigh before she could brace. The pain was so large it erased the room. When it shrank again she was already biting the heel of her hand to keep from crying out. Lucien tore a strip from the torn inner lining of his coat and rammed it hard against the wound. His fingers shook once and then steadied. The apricot smell was stronger now, sticky and almost rotten under the chalk.

"You bastard," she said, not sure whether she meant him for the iron or Ferrand for everything.

"Save it," he said, and the dryness of it was so nearly his old voice that she could have wept if there had been any moisture left in her.

He got one arm around her back and hauled her upright. Her bad leg folded at once. He swore then, the word harsh from him because it came so rarely, and turned so that she leaned almost full against him. The bullet scar in his left shoulder must have been screaming. He did not mention it. The broken seam of his coat gaped, and half of the newspaper parcel still clung inside the pocket with the second apricot mashed flat.

Above them the rear room filled with running feet.

"Back," Elise said. "Through the ditch."

He nodded and half dragged, half carried her toward the collapsed wall at the rear where the blast had opened a new way out. Something settled in the wreckage behind them. She did not look back again.

The night outside hit her like bad water, warm and full of noise. The back lot was choked with nettles and broken tiles. Lucien got her over the fallen stones one step at a time while the front of the tollhouse erupted in authority. Lantern light swung wild across the road. Men shouted for the area to be cleared, then immediately crowded it themselves. Through the weeds Elise saw the rabbit woman crouched in the gutter with her child under her body and both of them alive. The rabbit had torn loose and was lying in the road with its legs still tied. The carter's horse was down on one knee, flank scored by glass, rolling its eye white. The flour-sleeved apprentices were on their faces beside a wheel, one of them moving, one only stunned. Blood, yes. Cuts. Panic. Not the butcher's table Ferrand had budgeted for.

Lucien got her into the ditch and down among the reeds.

A sergeant's voice carried over the rest, practiced and already arranging the scene. "Bomb attack at the gate. Anarchists. Seal the road. Nobody leaves."

Nobody had yet gone into the cellar to count who was dead.

Elise almost laughed. The sound that came out was blood and spit.

Lucien put one hand over her mouth, not roughly, just enough. His palm smelled of dust, sweat, and fruit.

"Can you be quiet?"

She nodded.

They moved bent double along the ditch behind the wall while the road filled with uniforms. More whistles. More boots. Somebody shouted for a stretcher. Somebody else shouted for names. A witness began speaking too fast and was cut off by another voice, official and smoother, instructing him what he must have seen. Elise knew that tone better than she knew some prayers. It did not gather facts. It allocated them.

At the corner of the lot Lucien had to stop and cough. He turned away from her to do it and blood spotted the weeds. Not much. Enough.

"Lucien."

"Walk."

"You're bleeding."

"So are you."

Even now the answer carried the old schoolmaster's annoyance at wasted observation. It steadied her more than comfort would have done. She put her arm harder around his shoulders and forced the bad leg to take a fraction of her weight. Fire ran up from the wound. The world tilted, corrected, tilted again.

Behind them the tollhouse had started to burn in earnest. The cellar fire found old timber and lamp oil and climbed. Orange light licked up through the broken floor. In that light she could see the road crowd breaking into pieces under police hands, witnesses turned into file material before the smoke had even thinned.

They made the far end of the ditch and the shadow of the wall beyond it. Lucien lowered her there for one breath. Her skirt stuck wetly to her thigh. The hem felt heavier on one side, but pain flattened every smaller sensation into irrelevance.

"Can you manage the lane?" he asked.

"If I can't, you'll drag me."

"That was always the plan."

She looked at him. Chalk had settled in his eyelashes. Blood from his brow cut a clean line down the dust on his face. He was alive. That fact landed in her with a force almost as destabilizing as the blast.

"I should've told you everything," she said.

The words came out before prudence could stop them. Pain had burned through the careful ledgers in which she usually kept herself. For a moment she hated that loss of discipline more than the wound.

Lucien closed his eyes once, briefly, as if taking a measurement.

"Not here," he said.

He got her back up.

They took the lane south, away from the first cordon. Twice they had to flatten themselves against walls while police ran past toward the glow. Once a woman with a torn sleeve and soot on her cheek stared straight at Elise's bloody dress and then, out of some exhausted instinct for class solidarity or simple refusal, looked away. No one wanted more of the night than the night had already handed out.

By the time the whistles thinned behind them, Elise could feel the blood cooling on her leg and turning tacky. Every step pulled at the wound and at something deeper, some binding she had kept tight for months that had now come loose and would not retie to its old shape. Abel dead in the cellar. Ferrand dead with the fuse in his hand. The crowd spared the worst and still marked. The police already calling it what they needed it to be.

From far back at the gate came the first clear word hurled through a speaking trumpet of authority: conspiracy.

No one had looked long enough to know.

She leaned harder into Lucien and kept moving while the road behind them filled with the story Vautrin had wanted all along.


# Chapter 14

By morning the blast had sorted itself into separate claims on Elise's body. Her left side hurt where stone and splintered wood had come through wool and skin together. Her scalp stung under a strip of linen that smelled of boiled soap. Fine white grit kept turning up between her teeth. Each time she swallowed, she found another grain of the tollhouse cellar and worked it loose with her tongue.

The quarry had gone back to its ordinary sounds around her. Water dripped somewhere deeper in the tunnels. A man coughed in the print chamber and spat into a pail. Carriages rolled overhead in a dull wooden thunder that felt farther away than the sky. The enclosed air held lamp oil, wet wool, printer's ink, old soap, and a faint sweet rotten edge from the singed timber that still clung to her hair.

Clemence had done the night's work already. She had cut Elise out of the worst of the dress in the dark, packed the torn place at her ribs with folded lint, and bound it tight enough that breathing required attention. The bandage had dried a little to the skin. When Elise shifted on the pallet, the linen tugged at her side and brought the whole cellar back in a white hot instant: Abel's shoulder under her hands, Ferrand shouting above them, the kick of the satchel as it went off, the wall arriving where the air should have been.

"Don't move like that," Clemence said from the brazier. "If you tear it open again, I'm not wasting another strip this morning."

Her voice was brisk with lost sleep. She stood in shirtsleeves with her apron tied on over her skirt and a dented cocoa tin open beside her knee, full of saved buttons, clean pins, a needle stuck through a scrap of felt, and short lengths of thread wound around cardboard. Nothing in the tin had been bought new. Clemence kept such things the way another woman might keep relics.

Lucie sat on an upturned crate near the lamp, one stocking rolled at the ankle and the other properly pulled, as if she had begun to dress and then forgotten the second half of the task. A paper fox, softened from pocket sweat and handling, kept appearing and disappearing in her fist. Nadir had folded it for her weeks ago from a spoiled handbill. She had flattened one ear by worrying it with her thumb. Now she leaned over a newspaper almost as broad as her chest and mouthed the letters under her breath.

"Con... spi..." she whispered. "Conspi..."

"You can wait for bread before you eat the ink," Clemence said.

"I wasn't eating it."

"You were licking your finger."

Lucie tucked the finger into her palm, chastened for exactly one breath, then bent over the page again. Elise watched her mouth shape the syllables and felt the peculiar shame of surviving into daylight. Abel was dead. Ferrand was dead. Nadir, perhaps. The child still had a paper fox in her hand and a headline under it.

Elise pushed herself up on one elbow and had to stop halfway. The bandage bit. Black dots moved through her vision and thinned slowly.

"Water," she said.

Clemence crossed without hurry and held the cup while Elise drank. The tin taste of boiled water gave way to limestone. Elise could not tell whether her hands were shaking or Clemence's were simply more solid.

"Where's Lucien?"

"Up," Clemence said. "He went for the morning sheets and to see what else the city is selling with them."

At that, Lucie looked up. "He said he'd bring the one with pictures if there was one."

"He said he'd bring himself back in one piece," Clemence said. "The pictures are extra."

The irony of that nearly made Elise laugh, and laughter would have split something. She swallowed it down. Her mouth still tasted of burned wool.

Lucien came in a few minutes later with street dust on his cuffs and daylight on his shoulders, carrying three folded newspapers under one arm and a heel of bread wrapped in yesterday's sheet under the other. When he bent to set them on the crate, something bruised and orange rolled from his coat pocket and landed against Lucie's shoe.

An apricot. It had split down one side and stained the lining of his pocket.

Lucie stared at it, then at him.

He looked at the fruit as if he had found a message from another life. "I bought those yesterday," he said. His voice was hoarse, plain with fatigue. "I forgot one."

Lucie picked it up carefully. "It's still good."

"The clean half's good." He rubbed a thumb over the newsprint before unfolding it, a schoolmaster's old habit, flattening the creases as if tidier paper might produce tidier facts. "Eat it before your mother sees you waste the sun."

"I already see it," Clemence said.

"Then I stand corrected."

Lucie scraped the burst side off against the edge of the crate and sucked the rest down in two intent bites, apricot juice on her wrist, fox tucked under her elbow. The room watched her eat. It was not hunger alone that made them quiet. The ordinary act had become indecently dear.

Lucien laid the newspapers across Elise's blanket. The black ink had already come off on his fingertips.

"What are they saying?" she asked.

"Enough," he said. "More than enough."

He opened the first paper. The headline took half the front page.

ANARCHIST PLOT AT PORTE D'ORLEANS  
TWO FANATICS DESTROYED BY THEIR OWN MACHINE

The second had chosen a different arrangement of the same appetite. SECRET CELLAR OF NIHILISTS. The third preferred civic triumph: THE POLICE TRACE A VAST CONSPIRACY.

Lucie sounded out the last word, slower this time. "Con... spir... a... cy."

"Yes," Lucien said. "That one."

He read standing up, because there was nowhere in the chamber that did not already belong to someone's pain or labor. Ferrand had become a "known extremist courier." Abel was a "young disciple corrupted by foreign doctrines and criminal propaganda." A schoolmaster of notorious sympathies was said to have directed the circle but remained at large. The tollhouse explosion, which Elise remembered as a cellar, a body in her grip, and one terrified second of useless prayer to a God she did not believe in, had become in print an attempted massacre narrowly averted by the vigilance of the authorities.

The piece was careful with Ferrand. Too careful. It placed him among the dead without a smear of doubt, as though he had spent his last breath in service to the cause instead of trying to feed it bodies. Vautrin would have arranged that. A dead provocateur was more useful as evidence than as payroll.

"Read the rest," Clemence said.

Lucien did not answer at once. He turned the page with the side of his hand. Elise saw his cough gathering before it came. He looked away, coughed into his fist, then went on.

"There's a note lower down," he said. "On Belkacem."

Lucie straightened. The sucked apricot pit sat bright in her palm like a small wet bone.

Lucien read the paragraph once to himself before he read it aloud. "'The named associate Belkacem, arrested earlier this week in connection with the same anti-social milieu, died yesterday while attempting escape during transfer after a violent struggle with his escort. In view of ongoing inquiries, the body has not yet been returned to relations.'"

No one in the room moved.

The sentence had the finished polish Elise knew too well. Not returned. Ongoing inquiries. Violent struggle. Language built to make a corpse do clerical work after the heart had stopped. She could hear the office in it: a pen scraped clean, a blotter pressed down, a man saying that this wording would do because nobody worth counting would ask what Belkacem's face looked like afterward.

"They killed him," Lucie said.

Clemence drew in a breath through her nose. "Eat the rest of your bread when we have it."

"I know they killed him." Lucie looked from one adult to the other with furious child patience. "I didn't ask that part."

She set the apricot pit on the newspaper, right on the word transfer. "How do you run if they took your belt and your laces? They always take those."

Lucien lowered the page. For a moment his face was older than the silver in his hair. "You do not," he said. Then, correcting himself for her rather than from conviction: "You don't, most likely."

Elise put her fingers on the column and drew the sheet closer. The paper crackled under her hand. There it was in smaller print, buried as if burial inside the page mattered: attempted escape. She had heard policemen say it at tavern doors over men who could barely stand, over women dragged by the wrists, over bodies still warm enough to steam in winter. An escape attempt could mean a boot, a truncheon, a staircase, a bucket of water thrown too late. It could mean they had wanted words from him and had taken too much flesh while getting them.

"They didn't return the body because they don't want it counted," she said.

The room took that in.

Clemence's gaze shifted to her with a quick sharpness that felt more surgical than surprise. "You know their phrases well."

Elise kept looking at the print. "I've heard enough of them."

"That's not what I said."

Lucien cut across before the line could harden. "The paper says what it was written to say. That's the part we know."

"We know Ferrand's in there clean as baptism," Clemence said. "That bastard never looked that decent alive."

Lucie's mouth tightened around the child effort not to cry. "Will Nadir have a grave?"

No one answered quickly enough.

Lucien folded the top of the page back with deliberate care. "Not yet," he said. "But a man doesn't stop belonging to the earth because the Prefecture mislays him."

Lucie nodded as if filing the sentence for later argument with God, teachers, or officials. She put the apricot pit in her pocket beside the paper fox.

Elise read the article again while Lucien moved through the other pages. The details disagreed from column to column. One paper said two bombs. Another said a cellar arsenal. A third hinted at foreign funds and secret maps. The lies did not need to match. They only needed to accumulate. Ferrand's name, Abel's name, Belkacem's name, the word conspiracy laid over them like a net. By afternoon the city would know the shape it had been instructed to see.

And somewhere in the same city Vautrin would be drinking coffee under clean windows, turning dead men into proof.

Lucien laid the second paper on top of the first. "My name isn't printed," he said.

"Yet," Clemence answered.

"Yet."

"Then he thinks he still has use for the hunt."

"Or for the witness," Elise said before she could stop herself.

Lucien looked at her. The silence that followed was not Clemence's practical silence or Lucie's baffled one. It held recognition, old and not yet fully spoken.

"Yes," he said at last. "Possibly that too."

His eyes dropped to the bandage under her blouse, to the line where dried blood had browned the linen. He came and crouched beside the pallet. The closeness brought street air and tobacco that had not been lit, only carried. His hand hovered once over her shoulder as if asking permission from flesh instead of from her mouth, then settled lightly on the blanket near her knee.

"How bad?" he asked.

"Bad enough to make Clemence insulting."

"That means you'll live," Clemence said.

"You saved a room full of strangers who will spend the day reading that you meant to blow them apart," Lucien said.

"A poor use of labor."

It should have been dry. Instead it came out ragged. The laugh that followed scraped her side and turned into a hiss.

Lucien's hand tightened on the blanket. "Don't do that again."

"What, save them?"

"Pretend you're made of hinges and string. We can quarrel when you can stand."

The word quarrel hung between them like a future both near and expensive. Elise looked at his hand and knew exactly how little she had earned the right to want it there.

From deeper in the quarry someone called Lucien's name. He stood, coughed again into his fist, and gathered two of the papers.

"I need to show the others before rumor improves them further," he said.

"Take the bread," Clemence said.

"Cut it first."

"I can do both."

"I know."

He bent and touched Lucie's hair with the backs of his fingers as he passed. She leaned into it for the span of a breath and then pretended she had not. He went out with the papers under his arm, shoulders narrowed against a ceiling too low for any useful kind of pride.

When he was gone, the chamber seemed smaller. The headline stayed on Elise's blanket. The ink had rubbed off where Lucie's apricot pit had sat and made a dark crescent.

Clemence took up the empty cup and basin. "Now," she said. "Before the cloth sticks harder."

Elise knew what she meant before she reached for the torn dress at the foot of the pallet. In the night's confusion Clemence had pulled most of it off her, but the skirt and one side seam remained tangled under her hip, stiff with blood and quarry dust. Elise had hidden the brass marker in that hem on her first week below ground, three flat stitches inside the fold where no search had found it because no searcher had thought like a woman mending clothes she could not afford to replace.

She had not thought of it in weeks. Pain had driven every smaller secret out of the room.

Now it came back entire.

"Burn that," she said too quickly.

Clemence looked at the dress, then at her. "Burn good wool because you bled on it?"

"It's torn."

"So are you. I'm not feeding cloth to the stove for sentiment."

Lucie slid off the crate with the newspaper under her arm. "Should I go?"

"You should sit in the schoolroom doorway where I can see you," Clemence said. "Take your paper. Read me the big words if you trip over them. It will keep your mouth busy."

"That's not what mouths are for."

"Today that's what they're for."

Lucie went, muttering the headline to herself with offended dignity, fox and apricot pit making little shapes in her pocket. From the doorway she settled cross-legged just beyond the lamp spill, close enough to watch, far enough to pretend she was not.

Clemence set the basin on a crate and poured in fresh hot water from the kettle. Steam rose, carrying soap and metal and the wet mineral smell of the quarry. She put the cocoa tin by her knee, drew out the needle and thread, and then reached for the cloth at Elise's side.

"Hold the blanket between your teeth if you need to," she said. "I need both my hands."

"Your manner improves every day."

"And still you stay."

The first peel of linen from skin made the chamber tilt. Elise took the blanket after all. The cloth had glued itself to the torn place under her ribs. Clemence worked slowly, wetting, lifting, wetting again, until the bandage came away pink and then red. Air touched the wound and turned into pain so exact it had corners.

It was not large enough to look theatrical. That almost offended her. A long ragged tear above the hip. Stone grit still in two places. Skin charred brown at one edge where heat had kissed through wool. The body was vulgar in its economies. Ferrand and Abel had died for a blast that had not even given her a handsome scar.

Clemence did not waste pity on it. She wiped the blood away, frowned, and leaned closer. Her knuckles were rough from soap and cold water and labor. She smelled of lye, wool, and the onion she must have cut before dawn.

"You're lucky," she said.

"I had noticed."

"No. You hadn't." Clemence used the clean cloth to press at the deeper side of the tear. "An inch higher and this turns into prayers and piss."

From the doorway Lucie said, "I can hear you, Maman."

"Then hear useful things. Sound out the line under conspiracy."

Lucie obeyed with poor grace. "'The... author... ities...'"

Elise shut her eyes. The words came from two places at once: the child in the doorway and the column on the blanket, both of them laboring under the same language.

Clemence cleaned the wound again. Grit clicked into the basin. Once she had to use the tip of the needle to tease out a splinter buried shallow in the skin, and Elise swore around the blanket before she could stop herself.

Clemence glanced up. "There. Now you sound like someone with blood in her."

Elise spat the blanket out. "Fuck you."

"Better."

Lucie looked over from the doorway, startled enough to forget the newspaper for a second. "You said she ought to swear if it helped."

"I didn't say she ought to teach you new words."

"I already knew that one."

"Of course you did."

Clemence threaded the needle and took two small stitches where the skin would not lie shut by itself. The pull of thread through flesh felt unnaturally domestic, as if sewing and injury had decided to become one profession. Elise watched Clemence's face rather than the work. Fear made Clemence more exact, never softer. Each movement was measured. Each pause had an account behind it.

"How long have you known papers lie like that?" Clemence asked.

The question came as neatly as the stitch.

Elise looked at the ceiling. White dust furred the beams. "Since before this morning."

"That's not an answer either."

"It's the one I've got."

Clemence cut the thread with her teeth. "You always have one."

In the doorway Lucie began again. "'The authorities continue their vigilance against anti-social elements...'"

Her small voice flattened on vigilance and then recovered. Elise felt each syllable like another stitch. There had been nights, before the quarry, when she had copied police dictation till her wrist cramped, taking down the soft phrases men used when they wanted clubs to sound administrative. The Republic loved a clean euphemism. It could push a face into the floorboards and call the gesture vigilance before the blood dried.

Clemence reached for the dress at Elise's hip. "Lift."

Elise did not move quickly enough.

Clemence's hand stilled on the cloth. "Lift," she said again.

Elise pushed herself a little to one side, breath held, and Clemence dragged the remaining skirt free. The hem came with a tearing sound where blast and blood had joined it to the wound. Elise had to bite the inside of her cheek hard enough to taste iron.

The dress looked less like clothing than evidence. One panel was scorched through. The side seam had burst. White quarry dust had caked into the folds. And along the lower hem, where Elise herself had once worked tiny hidden stitches by lamplight, the thread had loosened under the strain.

Clemence saw it.

Not the marker yet. The seam first. A laundress's eye went there before a spy's fear did.

"This was resewn by someone who didn't expect another pair of hands in it," Clemence said.

"I mend my own things."

"You mend like a clerk."

"There are worse crimes."

"There are also plainer answers."

She slid a fingernail under the half-torn fold to free the cloth from a clot. Elise knew the marker was no bigger than a two-sous piece, brass gone dull from months against wool. She had hidden it because Vautrin had told her never to let it leave her person. Proof of approval, proof of access, proof that if another officer took her up she could be claimed instead of jailed. She had carried it against her body the way a poorer woman might carry a saint's medal.

Now it was only weight in the wrong seam.

"Leave that," Elise said.

Clemence did not even look at her. "Don't tell me how to save cloth."

"It's filthy."

"So I'll wash it. We're not rich enough for your disgust."

She tugged the hem open another inch. A few grains of grit pattered into the basin. Then the brass marker came loose and dropped with a clear, mean sound against the enamel.

Lucie looked up at once. "What was that?"

No one answered her.

The disc rocked once in the bottom of the basin, wet now, brighter than it had any right to be. On one side the stamped shield caught the lamp. On the other, under the smear of blood and soap, the word showed plainly enough for anyone who could read slowly and accurately.

SURETE.

Clemence did not gasp. She did not curse. Her face, which usually moved faster than most people's hands, went still for one second and no longer. Then she put two fingers into the basin, lifted the disc out, and turned it in her palm.

From the doorway Lucie said, quieter, "Maman?"

Clemence closed her hand. "A bit of brass," she said. "Mind your line."

Lucie looked unconvinced, but the newspaper still owned part of her concentration. She bent back over it. "'Against... anti-social...'"

The room did not feel the same after that. Elise saw the change first in Clemence's hands. Not rougher. Worse than rougher. Deliberate. The woman set the marker on the folded edge of her apron for the briefest instant, wiped the basin water from her fingers on her skirt, and tucked the disc into the deep pocket at her hip as if pocketing a coin owed.

Elise could have spoken then. She could have lied, or confessed enough to spoil the lie, or said Vautrin's name out loud and shattered the hour. Pain left her too slow and pride left her too late. More than either of those, Lucie was in the doorway with Nadir's paper fox in her pocket and the newspaper in her lap, working her mouth carefully around the word authorities. There was no way to drag the truth into the room that would not land on the child first.

Clemence dipped a clean strip of linen in the water and wrung it out. "Roll a little," she said.

Elise obeyed.

The bandage went on with infuriating competence. Around them the quarry kept up its indifferent life: dripping water, the dull labor of the press farther in, a murmur of men taking the news from Lucien chamber by chamber. Aboveground the city was already widening the blast into a story large enough to trample any body that contradicted it. Belowground another story had narrowed to the size of a brass disc warming in Clemence's pocket.

"Does anti-social mean thief?" Lucie asked from the doorway.

"Sometimes," Clemence said.

"Does it mean murderer?"

Clemence tied the knot under Elise's ribs, small and exact. "It means they want everyone frightened in the same direction."

It was the closest thing to tenderness she had offered all morning, and it was not meant for Elise.

She gathered the ruined dress into her lap, not to return it, not yet. Her thumb rested once over the place in the hem where the marker had hidden. When she rose, the apron pocket gave a small weighted pull against her skirt.

Elise looked up at her. Clemence looked back. Nothing was said. That silence had more shape than speech. It had timing in it. It had labor. It had the clean patience of a woman who had found the exact stain and chosen not to wash it in front of the child.

From the doorway Lucie caught on the same word again and again. "Es... cape. Escape. Escape."

Clemence did not correct her.


# Chapter 15

By noon the quarry had turned close. Early summer aboveground meant the air downstairs stopped moving; lamp heat and human breath sat under the limestone and would not lift. Elise knew the brass marker was gone before Clemence said a word. The hem of her dress felt wrong against her shin, stitched edge rough where somebody with steadier hands than hers had opened it and sewn it shut again. Each step tugged the bandage tight around her left ribs. The blast had left that whole side raw and yellowing, and the pain kept drawing her palm back to the empty place in the cloth, as if metal might return through wanting.

She found Clemence in the ration room with the door half shut against the gallery noise. Bread, onions, two wedges of hard cheese, and the week's lamp-oil ledger were spread over the table in the order Clemence liked: food first, then numbers, then the things that made the food visible after dark. A child's stocking with the heel worn thin hung from her apron string, waiting for evening and a needle. She was cutting the noon loaf into shares so exact they looked accusatory.

"You came on your own," Clemence said without lifting her head. "Good. Shut it."

Elise closed the door. The latch sounded louder than it should have.

Clemence finished the slice in her hand, set the knife down, and drew the marker from her apron pocket. Brass on wood. Small enough to hide in a hem, heavy enough to settle a life. She placed it between them beside the bread crumbs.

"Name."

Elise's mouth dried at once. "Clemence, let me tell it properly."

"You can tell it any filthy way you like after. Name first."

The room smelled of onion skins, old soap, and the damp wool dress Clemence had hung too close to the stove. Elise watched her own reflection bend in the coin-bright brass.

"Elise Valette."

"Again."

"Elise Valette."

Clemence nodded once, as if confirming a figure she had already added twice. "And what are you, Elise Valette?"

Elise put both hands on the table because she did not trust them at her sides. The burn scar inside her left wrist caught against a crumb. "A confidential operative with the Surete."

"A paid liar, then."

"Yes."

Clemence breathed out through her nose. Not shock. Not fury yet. Something narrower and harder, the kind of breath a woman used when a wash tub split and the water went over the floor after three hours' work.

"How long?"

"From before I came in."

"I know that. Don't waste my time pretending I'm stupid." Clemence tapped the marker with one nail. Her knuckles were rough and red from lye. "What were you sent for?"

"The refuge. The press. Names. Routes. Whoever Vautrin could make into a case."

"Lucien?"

Elise felt the answer before she said it, a fresh pull under the bandage. "Yes."

Clemence looked at her then. Her face did not open into outrage. That would have been simpler. It stayed flat with work, the way it did when a pot boiled over and there were still three more things on the fire.

"He was the point."

"Yes."

"Not one name among many."

"No."

Clemence slid the marker back toward herself and closed her hand over it. "My girl slept with her head against your arm after the raid on the yard."

Elise said nothing.

"I boiled your bloody shift in my own pot yesterday. I cut the cloth off your side because you were shaking too hard to hold still. I thought I was tending a woman who had been stupid in one direction and brave in another." Clemence's voice stayed brisk, almost dry. That was worse than if she had shouted. "Turns out I was rinsing state brass out of my own wash water."

"I never put Lucie on paper," Elise said. "Not once. Not you either. I left you both out from the beginning."

"Don't spend that like a virtue."

"I know it isn't."

"You walked in here to price us. You don't get credit for failing to sell every piece."

The words landed clean because they were clean. Elise had no counter to them that was not another arrangement of guilt. She could feel the old training trying to rise anyway, the one that took damage and sorted it into order before it became panic. Facts. Sequence. Leverage. She hated herself most in the moments when that part still worked.

"Ferrand was police too," she said. "Not like me. Worse. Paid to make something bloody enough to print. I learned it after the raid. I warned Lucien what I could without saying what I was."

"And Abel is still dead."

"Yes."

"And Nadir is still dead."

"Yes."

Clemence picked up the knife again, not to threaten, only because her hand wanted work. She shaved a strip from the cheese rind and set it aside for soup. "Did you know what they were doing to Nadir?"

"No." Elise heard how fast that came and forced herself not to say more until the truth found its own shape. "Not while he was alive. I knew Vautrin wanted a case. I did not know how far they had gone with him until the papers lied."

"The papers always lie."

"Yes."

The quarry held its sounds the way a chest held a cough. Somewhere deeper in the passage somebody dropped a pan. The metal rang once and died. Elise could hear Lucie reciting letters in the schoolroom beyond the wall, too loud on the ones she liked, softer on the ones that still felt foreign in her mouth.

Clemence said, "How close are they now?"

"I don't know exactly."

"Then what use are you?"

That stung because it was practical. Clemence did not care whether Elise suffered. She cared whether suffering could buy anything.

"Useful enough to learn," Elise said. "He'll want something after the tollhouse. He does not have the map. Not the current one. Not the entrances as Lucien keeps them now. If I can hear what he thinks he knows, I can bring it back before he moves."

"If."

"If."

Clemence looked at the bread, the ledger, the stocking, the woman across from her. Elise could almost watch the arithmetic happen. Not moral arithmetic. Worse. Household arithmetic. How many mouths, how many hours, how much danger a person brought compared with what she might still carry back in her two hands.

"You tell Lucien before evening bread," Clemence said. "In your own name. In your own mouth. I'm not doing that labor for you."

"I will."

"You tell him everything that concerns this place. If I hear you left out one useful fact because it made you look less foul, I will finish the work the blast started."

Elise believed her.

"Before that," Clemence went on, "you go see your inspector and you find out what sort of ruin he is buying. Engineers, smoke, a cordon, a plain grab in the street, I don't care. Bring back something I can weigh."

"Why give me the hours at all?"

Clemence's face changed then, not softer, just more tired. "Because if he's already moving on us, I would rather know by noon than by the sound of boots." She stripped off the child's stocking from her apron string, folded it once, and shoved it into her pocket. "And because Lucien has buried enough boys this week. I'm letting you hand him one lie less than I had to."

Elise swallowed. The room tasted of limestone dust and onion.

"Take the brown shawl," Clemence said. "Your sleeve is burned through. You look like trouble from half a street away."

She threw it across without looking. Elise caught it one-handed against her sore side.

"Clemence."

"Don't say thank you to me. Go."

Elise wrapped the shawl around the scorched shoulder of her dress and left with the brass marker still in Clemence's fist.

***

Aboveground the heat came at her all at once, not clean warmth but the rank Paris mix of horse sweat, drains, cabbage water, and carriage grease cooking in the sun. She had spent enough weeks in the quarry that the light felt excessive now. Every surface reflected. Window glass, shop signs, a knife on a butcher's board, the bare forearm of a woman leaning from a wash line to shake crumbs into the gutter. The city was full of people buying fruit and arguing over fish as if the papers had not turned two dead men into a public warning and a third into an erased body by breakfast.

Elise took the long route north because habit still made her check reflections and crossings even with her side on fire. She passed a newspaper board with Ferrand's name big as theater type and Abel's smaller beneath it, the two of them flattened into one useful lesson for respectable readers. Delatour's name had not made the headline yet. That omission felt temporary. The state liked sequence. First the dead boys, then the plot, then the schoolmaster at the center explaining it all with the wrong face in the dock.

The stationer's on rue Saint-Jacques kept copybooks in the window, black mourning borders on the top shelf and school primers below, as if grief and instruction were neighboring trades. Elise had used the back room twice before for exchanges Vautrin did not want entered at the Prefecture desk. The owner never asked questions. He only rang the bell once when she stepped in, then busied himself with sealing wax as though women came in bleeding through their stays every day.

Vautrin was in the rear office by the window, which he had opened half an inch and no more. Dust still managed to get in. He had laid his gloves on the desk with the fingers matched precisely, as if even empty leather ought to keep discipline. His cuffs were cleaner than the room deserved.

He rose when she entered, not out of courtesy so much as inspection.

"Mlle Valette," he said. "You look as though the Republic has been making waste of you."

"That was always part of the arrangement."

"Sit down."

She stayed standing. The chair would have forced her ribs against the wood.

Vautrin noticed, filed it away, and sat alone. A narrow envelope lay beside his blotter. Another paper, folded twice and sealed with office wax, rested on top of it. Charenton, Elise thought at once, before she could stop herself.

"I expected you sooner after the tollhouse," he said. "But you are here, which suggests you still understand the difference between injury and folly."

"Say what you want."

"I should think that has become obvious." He touched the envelope with two fingers. "Your stipend for the month. Ninety francs. An additional twenty because the incident produced visible damage. I can also regularize the asylum account before close of business. Your mother need not feel your recent instability."

The words did what they were meant to do. Not in the heart. Lower. In the stomach, where hunger and money and fear of letters from institutions lived in the same wet place. Ninety francs. Twenty more. The monthly twenty-eight to keep Charenton from writing again in that ugly blue hand. Enough to buy linen that did not smell of damp strangers. Enough to keep a clerk from moving her mother to a dirtier ward out of spite or routine or because the right signature had not come in time.

She said, "And in return?"

Vautrin's heavy lids lowered slightly, amused by the necessity of asking. "The quarry map on linen backing. The current entrances. The names of whoever remains. Most importantly, a signed statement that Lucien Delatour housed Ferrand, retained Saint-Clair after the theft, and directed the conspiracy that culminated at the tollhouse."

"Directed." She could not keep the contempt out of it.

"Do not be childish. I am not asking you to invent a dragon. Only to place the existing bodies in their proper administrative order." He steepled his fingers. "Ferrand lodged among them. Saint-Clair acted from within their circle. Delatour maintained the refuge and exercised authority there. The court does not require lyric truth. It requires a chain."

"A clean statement," Elise said.

"Exactly."

"A lie, arranged properly."

"A useful arrangement of facts. You have always understood that distinction."

She had. That was what made the room dangerous. Not that he sounded monstrous. He sounded reasonable in the old office language, the one that made starvation into arrears and beatings into necessary pressure and men like Nadir disappear into corrective procedure. She had spoken it herself for years. More than once she had survived by speaking it better than the men in the hall.

Vautrin watched her face and went on in the same patient tone. "The alternative is not noble ruin, Elise. Let us leave novels aside. The alternative is that you become a deniable woman with a damaged body and no sponsor, attached to a circle that now knows what you are. That is not tragedy. It is merely bad administration. I am offering you re-entry while you can still take it."

"Re-entry to what?"

"To salary. To use. To being protected by the office that made you worth paying in the first place." He slid the sealed paper half an inch toward her. "There could be clerical work later if you have finally lost your taste for sentiment. There could even be less field exposure. I do reward correction when it arrives in time."

Elise looked at the wax seal and thought of her mother combing the same strip of hair fifty times because the attendants only noticed quiet patients. She thought of the last time she had visited Charenton, the iron smell under the wash water, the way one old woman had gnawed the edge of her own blanket into strings because nobody had given her enough to do with her hands. Twenty-eight francs a month bought the difference between a cleaner corner and a bucket set closer to the bed. It bought a nurse remembering a name. It bought extra soap.
