It bought the right not to have a woman left in her own filth until afternoon.

Vautrin knew exactly where the arithmetic cut. He always had.

"You have something for me," he said.

Elise had, in fact, brought something. Since the raid, when the milk-ink slips went into the drain, one habit from the old life had survived all the same. When fear grew too large to carry whole, she turned it into headings. She had written by lamplight at dawn because pain and dread had pushed her back into order: survivors, injuries, newspaper lies, the wine-stair entrance, how long Lucien's cough had lasted in the dark before he mastered it. She had folded the page and tucked it behind her stays before climbing aboveground, because some foul little chamber inside her still believed a woman ought to arrive at a bargain carrying merchandise.

Now she reached into her bodice and drew out the paper.

Vautrin's gaze sharpened. "There. You see? We are not dealing with melodrama after all."

The sheet was damp with sweat from her skin. Her own hand stared back at her in small, disciplined lines, the ugly efficient script he had paid for and improved. Abel dead. Ferrand dead. Belkacem officially dead. Delatour: coughing, diminished, still central. One entrance possibly compromised after the blast. One child omitted again. It was all there, almost tender in its accuracy. That was the obscenity of it. The state liked exactness more than truth, and she had been exact for a living.

The office lamp stood ready on the desk because Vautrin disliked shadows on documents. Elise held the corner of the sheet to the chimney.

For the first second he did not understand what she was doing. Then the edge browned, curled, and caught. Milk-pale fibers darkened. Her neat hand began to blister away.

"Elise."

She held the page until the flame bit her fingers. Lucien's name blackened first, then split. The line about the entrance went next, then the count of blankets in the sleeping gallery, then all of it collapsed into a twisted strip that dropped ash across Vautrin's blotter.

He did not lunge. That was not his style. He only pulled the blotter back before the ash could mark the desk.

"That was the last thing I had for you," she said.

Vautrin's face went stiller than anger. "No. The last thing you have is the map."

"You'll have to find it without me."

"And Delatour's statement."

"Without me."

He looked at her burned fingertips, at the singed paper on his desk, at the woman who had just made herself expensive in the wrong direction.

"This is a sentimental choice," he said.

"No." Elise shook her stinging hand once. "It's an expensive one. That's different."

"Expense is not virtue."

"I know."

For the first time something almost like fatigue touched his mouth. Not pity. Annoyance at waste. "You have been among them too long if you think refusal cleans you."

"I don't think it cleans anything."

"Good. Then we remain in the vicinity of reality." He folded his gloves one over the other and set them in his hat. "Listen carefully. The papers can be made to wait a little while longer for Delatour if I wish it. Engineers cannot be made to ignore stone forever. Old surveys exist. Street reports exist. Blind searches also exist, and blind searches break more than a guided one. You are declining the guided version."

The warning landed because it was almost generous in form.

"How long?" she asked.

"Long enough that you should use the afternoon well. Not so long that you should mistake it for mercy." He stood. "As for the asylum account, when the office writes next, do not imagine feeling will answer the letter."

She believed that too.

At the door he added, "If you change your mind, you know where to find me. If you do not, someone else will eventually find the quarry. You are not indispensable, Mlle Valette. You are only early."

She stepped back into the stationer's shop with the smell of burned report on her fingers and Vautrin's arithmetic still moving under her skin.

Outside, the newspaper board had not changed. Ferrand still grinned in print. Abel was still reduced to a cautionary youth. Nadir was still dead in a sentence nobody had earned. Elise bought nothing, took nothing, and walked south hard enough that the wound under her bandage began to seep again.

***

By the time she came down the wine stair the quarry had entered its evening hour, when bodies tried to pretend routine could still hold. Broth smell from the cook fire. A child laughing too loud and then stopping because the room remembered itself. Men coming in dusty from odd work aboveground. Somebody shaking limestone grit from a blanket near the sleeping alcove. The familiarity of it struck her harder now that it was almost over. She had learned the shelter the way a thief learned a house: first for exits, then for habits, then unwillingly for the people breathing in it.

Clemence was waiting near the schoolroom arch with her arms folded under her apron. The brass marker had disappeared again. So had any room for postponement.

"He's in there," she said.

"Did you tell him?"

"Not my job." Clemence moved aside. "Don't take all night."

Lucien was alone except for Lucie's cracked slate on the bench and the white quarry wall behind him smudged with the day's lesson. He had looped thread around one split corner of the slate and was drawing it tight, trying to keep the wood from peeling farther. The work made his long fingers look older. A paper fox, softened by damp, leaned near the lamp on the ledge above his shoulder. Nadir had folded it weeks ago for Lucie out of a rejected broadside; one ear was starting to collapse.

Lucien glanced up when Elise entered. His face had gone quarry-pale again in the last two days, not from dust alone. The cough had thinned him. Grief had done the rest.

"Clemence said you needed to speak to me before supper," he said.

Elise shut the schoolroom door, and for a stupid second the movement reminded her of nights when shutting a door meant privacy instead of judgment. The memory was bodily, treacherous. His shirt open at the throat. Chalk still on his fingers when he put his hand between her shoulder blades. The rasp in his breath against her neck. She pushed it down before it could become begging.

"My name isn't Jeanne Morel," she said.

Lucien set the slate on the bench. He did not speak.

"It's Elise Valette."

Still he waited.

"I came into the quarry under orders from Inspecteur Vautrin, anti-anarchist section. I was meant to map the refuge, identify the press, report names, habits, entrances, anything that could be turned into arrests."

"This refuge in general," Lucien said, and even now his voice held that teacher's steadiness, the one that kept panic from getting its own grammar.

She could have lied a little longer. She could have said yes and left the sharpest blade wrapped. Clemence had already warned her off that instinct.

"You in particular," she said.

The stillness after that felt structural, as if some load-bearing strut inside the room had taken a crack.

Lucien looked at her a long moment. Then he said, "From the first day."

"Yes."

"From the schoolroom."

"Yes."

He turned his head aside and coughed once into his fist. The cough deepened, caught, came again. By the time it loosened, his eyes were wet with the force of it. He wiped the heel of his hand across his mouth and looked at the smear as though offended by his own body for choosing this moment.

"I knew parts of your story were false," he said. The sentences had shortened, just as pain always forced them to. "I knew there was something trained in you. I did not think the Prefecture had sent it to sleep in our beds."

Elise took the words without moving.

"How much did you send?"

"At first? Enough to matter. Not everything. Never everything." She forced herself to stay exact. "I left Clemence and Lucie out from my first report. I left out whole chambers when I could. After the raid I burned my cipher slips."

"But before that."

"Before that, yes. I sent names. Movements. What I believed was relevant."

"Relevant to whom?"

"To him."

Lucien nodded once. That was all. It was worse than if he had struck the wall.

"And Ferrand?" he asked.

"I learned in May that he was being paid to produce a bomb case. I warned you to move the press because of him. I should have told you the whole truth then. I didn't."

"No."

"Today Vautrin offered me the salary back. My mother's account at Charenton, money, protection. He wants the map and a statement that you directed Ferrand and Abel both."

"Did you give him either?"

"No."

"Why should I believe that now?"

She held up the hand with the reddened fingers. The burn was small, ugly, and immediate. "Because I burned the last report I had on his lamp."

Lucien looked at the fingers, then back at her face. He did not soften.

"That proves you burned one page."

"Yes."

"Not your soul."

"No."

She had not expected mercy, but hearing the refusal named so plainly made something inside her pull hard enough that the room blurred for a second. She steadied herself against the bench with the cracked slate.

"If you need details, ask them," she said. "I'll give you all of it now."

"All of it." He gave a short breath that was not laughter. "That would take longer than we have."

The paper fox on the ledge tipped further toward collapse in the quarry damp. Elise wanted stupidly to fix it. Her hands stayed where they were.

"Did you ever put Lucie in danger by what you sent?" Lucien asked.

"No."

"Clemence?"

"No."

"Nadir?"

That was harder, because the answer was not a clean sentence. "Not directly. But I wrote things that fed the machine that took him. I can't sort the percentages for you."

"Don't ask me to."

"I'm not."

He came away from the wall then, one step only, enough that the lamplight found the hollows grief had cut under his cheekbones. "What about me, Elise?"

He had not called her that in the quarry before. Not once. Hearing her own name in his mouth should have been relief. It felt like a blade entering where a bandage had been holding.

"What about you?" she said, because there was no safe version.

"Was I only work until it became inconvenient to say so?"

There it was. Not the grand political wound first. The bed. The body. Of course. That was where betrayal always stopped pretending to be theory.

"No," she said.

"No which part?"

"No, you weren't only work."

"But you were work."

She made herself answer in one piece. "Yes."

His eyes shut for a moment. When they opened again the steadiness had changed texture. Less patient. More final.

"I can understand being watched," he said. "I've been watched by the state since before Abel had whiskers. I can understand being studied, followed, priced. It's an old French habit. What I can't understand is lying down beside me with that in your pocket and wanting me to call it separate."

"It wasn't separate enough," she said. "That's the truest thing I can say."

"It wasn't separate at all."

She could have fought the wording. She did not. The quarry made liars of everyone eventually, but some sentences did not improve under handling.

"You saved lives," Lucien said after a moment, and the fairness of it made her throat hurt worse than accusation had. "I know that too. You kept Clemence and Lucie off your reports. You warned us about Ferrand, though not enough. You went into the tollhouse instead of leaving a boy to become a headline. I know what I saw."

Elise had not realized until then how badly she wanted at least that much kept intact. She looked down before gratitude could shame her.

"But a shelter isn't only walls," he went on. "It's who knows where a child sleeps, who knows which cough means blood and which one means damp, who knows where the map is wrapped and where the extra oil is kept, who gets listened to when the room is frightened. You came inside that knowledge under orders from the men who have already killed enough of us. You don't stay in the middle of it now."

The words were quiet. They hit harder because of that. This was not a public denunciation made for courage. It was an inventory of removal.

Clemence opened the schoolroom door then and stayed in the frame, not interrupting, only present to hear the terms.

Lucien spoke as much to her as to Elise now. "She sleeps in the outer gallery by the wine stairs. Not with the women and not near the children."

Clemence gave one short nod.

"She does not touch the map," Lucien said. "She does not sit in council. She does not hear routes, names, reserves, or the places we send anybody aboveground. If there is hauling to do, she hauls. If there are buckets to carry, she carries them. If the police come before dawn, she'll do what she's told like any other pair of hands, and not one thing more."

Elise heard her own future narrowed chamber by chamber.

"Lucien," she said.

He looked at her. Not cruelly. That would have been easier. With the exhausted exactness he gave bad lessons and deaths and budgets that did not balance.

"No," he said. "You don't ask me for a second ruling in the same hour."

She had meant to say the police would move soon, that Vautrin had mentioned engineers, blind searches, stone broken without care. The facts still mattered. She forced the words out plain.

"He said old surveys exist. If he can't get the current map, he may come in blind. He mentioned engineers."

That reached him. She saw the teacher disappear and the organizer take one hard step forward inside the same body.

"How soon?"

"He would not give me an hour. Only enough to tell me not to waste the afternoon."

Lucien nodded. Accepted it. Stored it where the useful things went.

"Anything else?"

"He wants you alive for the papers if he can manage it."

"Of course he does."

Silence again. Elise knew she should stop there, while the room still belonged to necessity and not to whatever was left between them. Instead she said the thing that kept scraping at her throat.

"What we had wasn't false."

Clemence's mouth hardened at once, but Lucien only looked tired beyond speech.

"Don't make me keep two ledgers for the same act," he said. "One for the tenderness and one for the use. I won't do it. I can't."

The answer was so completely his that it ended the question better than anger could have. Elise felt it settle.

From the gallery came the rattle of bowls and Lucie's voice asking somebody whether there was more salt. Ordinary life, still insisting. For one savage moment Elise wanted to go out there, take the bowls, pour broth, do some common task so the chapter of her life might look less singularly ruined. Then she remembered she had just been assigned a new place in the room, and it was not among the common things anymore.

Lucien put the repaired slate down on the bench and turned toward Clemence.

"Wake the two men who know the lower passage after they eat," he said. "I want the south cut checked tonight. If the old survey lines are already in police hands, it matters more than the wine stair. And bring me the map."

He did not ask Elise where it was. He did not ask whether she could still help read it. The exclusion was complete in its practicality.

Clemence jerked her head toward the door. Elise moved.

At the threshold she stopped once, because stopping hurt less than leaving cleanly.

"Lucien."

He did not turn.

"I am sorry" would have been obscene in that room. "I loved you" would have sounded like theft. "Trust me now" was impossible. No sentence she owned could cross the distance without insulting it.

So she left with nothing said.

Clemence took her down the side passage to the outer gallery, the one that held the wine-shop smell longest because it sat closest to the stair. Somebody had already put a pallet there, rolled from spare blankets and an old coat. Not cast into the street. Not kept inside. The exact measure of what remained possible. No one in the passage asked what it was doing there. That meant the story had already started moving without her.

"You heard him," Clemence said.

"Yes."

"If you bring them here before night, I won't wait for Lucien next time."

"I know."

Clemence studied her a second longer. Up close the lye had split the skin beside her thumbnails. There was flour on her sleeve from slicing bread and a new gray thread caught in the hair she had pinned up too fast. Little domestic accidents of a woman still keeping a world going while others broke it.

"Lucie asks after you," Clemence said. "I told her you were sleeping nearer the stair because of the cough in the inner room. That lie is for her, not for you. Don't build anything on it."

Then she went back toward the schoolroom.

Elise sat on the pallet carefully, teeth set against the pull in her side. The outer gallery breathed different air. Wine dregs, damp chalk, the cold draft that leaked from the stair, and farther in the shelter's living smell she was no longer inside of. Voices carried oddly here. Close enough to hear the shape of them, too far to catch the words once they dropped low.

After a minute Lucien's voice reached her anyway, practical now, already moving beyond the wound she had made. He asked for the map. He asked who among the adults could still carry weight in a flood passage. He asked which children had shoes fit for water and which would need rags bound round their feet.

He did not use her name again.


# Chapter 16

The lamps had been burning badly in the summer closeness, but this was not lamp smoke. Lamp smoke stayed greasy around the chimney glass. This came under the stone like a patient hand, tarry and deliberate, and found Elise in the narrow alcove near the ration room where Clemence had put her after the truth came out. By the time she opened her eyes it had already settled into the blanket under her cheek and roughened the back of her throat.

Limestone grit sifted from the roof. Someone was running in the gallery. Not one person. Several, and not with the blunt pounding panic of a raid already in the room. These steps stopped, turned, started again. Counting. Carrying. Deciding.

Lucie stood in the mouth of the alcove with one stocking fallen to her ankle and a paper swallow crushed in her fist so hard that Nadir's careful folds had gone soft at the beak. Her hair had been braided for sleep and had come half loose. She looked not at Elise's face but at the smoke flattening itself along the ceiling.

"They're making the air bad at the wine stairs," she said. "Is it the wine stairs?"

Elise was up before the question finished. Her dress was still stiff where the hem had been resewn after Clemence ripped out the brass marker. She shoved her feet into her boots without lacing them and stepped into the gallery.

The quarry had a dozen ordinary smells that fought one another every day: lamp oil, wet wool, cold bread, the sour iron of printer's ink that never quite left the deeper chambers, soap from Clemence's buckets, the mineral damp that sweated out of the walls no matter the season. This smoke sat over all of them. Wet straw. Pitch. Something sharper underneath, almost chemical. It had purpose. A raid with pistols had speed in it. This had masonry.

People were already dragging bundles into the sleeping gallery and then abandoning them when they realized what their own hands had chosen: blankets, a kettle with no lid, a heel of bread, Therese Bertaud's sewing basket. Therese herself had the basket hooked over her arm and the wooden darning mushroom she always kept tucked at her waistband knocked against her hip as she moved. She had once spent an entire supper explaining how she would mend a gentleman's coat so neatly he would never know the elbow had gone, and now she stood in her night jacket, coughing into one wrist, trying to decide whether thread counted as life.

Clemence came out of the ration room with Lucie's other stocking still looped through the apron string at her waist, as if she had meant to finish mending it before breakfast and the morning had split open first. Her hands were wet to the wrists. She had been at the water bucket already.

When she saw Elise she did not waste time on surprise or accusation. The accusation had been settled the night before and the night before that. She only said, "If you've still got anything hidden, now is the hour to name it."

"Not this," Elise said.

"No," Clemence said. "I suppose not."

At the turn toward the schoolroom Lucien was listening to the stone as if it were a pupil refusing to read clearly. He still had his tobacco tin of chalk ends in one hand. He saved the blunt white stubs because waste offended him more than inconvenience. A handkerchief was bunched in the other. Chalk dust had settled into the dark hairs at his wrist and along one cuff. Somebody must have pulled him out of a lesson or he had tried to begin one anyway, out of habit, out of defiance, out of the old schoolmaster's superstition that routine could keep a room human.

He looked at Elise once and then past her, toward the cellar entrance no one could see from here.

"Listen," he said.

She did. One muffled thud from the direction of the wine stairs. Another, farther north and lower, where an old slit passage had once taken air before a cave-in cut it almost useless. Then the scrape of stone against stone, men shifting weight together, and a shouted order blurred by distance and smoke.

"Three mouths," Elise said.

"At least," Lucien answered. His voice was plain already, stripped by the first coughing fit. "The cellar, the north slit, and maybe the cart shaft if they found it."

One of the older men from the gallery, a cooper named Marius who slept with dried mint in his vest because he believed it kept the damp out of his chest, spat into the chalk dust and said, "You'd know how they'd do it."

The words landed where they meant to. Half the room looked at Elise. The other half kept looking toward the smoke.

She said, "If they wanted bodies quickly, they'd come down and lose some of their own in the dark. This is cheaper. They'll bank smoke at every opening they can find on the survey, brick what they can brick, and wait for us to cough our way toward daylight."

"You say that like kitchen work," Therese Bertaud muttered.

"Because to them it's kitchen work," Elise said.

Another thud shivered through the wall. White dust came down in a fine sift over Lucie's hair. She wiped it away with the same hand that held the paper swallow and only blurred it into paste.

Lucien closed his eyes for one breath, not to steady himself but to count. When he opened them, the room had made its choice for him simply by watching. Nobody else here knew the old survey lines well enough. Nobody else had heard the roof for years the way he had. Nobody else had lived long enough underground to know which draft belonged to air and which belonged to collapse.

"Clemence," he said, "the sump."

Her mouth tightened. "That run's half drowned."

"Better drowned than smoked."

"The first squeeze takes the shoulder off anybody broad as me."

"I know."

She looked at Elise before she looked back at him. It was not a request for mercy. It was arithmetic.

Lucien turned to Elise. The smoke had already begun to scrape at his lungs. She could hear the damp drag in the center of each breath before the cough took it. "You remember the linen map."

She had seen it twice laid open, once by accident and once because he had wanted her to. Linen backing, survey hand beneath his corrections. Ink faded in the older cuts, newer pencil marks on top. She had studied it then as a professional instrument. Now the memory came back under pressure in sections: the sleeping gallery, the deeper print chamber, a ragged branch marked with Lucien's cramped note about floodwater and rotten footing.

"The drain below the ration room," she said.

"Yes. It was cut to take seepage toward the Bievre before the north fall changed the level. Most of it's still passable if the spring runoff didn't choke it."

"Most of it," Clemence said.

"That's the kindest answer I've got."

He coughed then, hard enough to bend him. The handkerchief came away dark at the center when he pressed it back into his palm. Lucie saw it. Elise saw Lucie see it.

"How far?" Lucie asked. Fear always made her ask for the measurable part. "Is it farther than the schoolroom? Is it farther than the print room?"

Lucien put the chalk tin down on a ledge so carefully that the tiny pieces did not rattle. "Farther than you want and shorter than staying here."

He gestured for Clemence to open the ration room niche. She shoved aside two sacks of flour gone gray at the seams and levered up a stone slab Elise had always taken for part of the floor. Cold air breathed from below, wetter and fouler than the quarry's usual damp. The hole was not a stair. It was a throat.

People recoiled from the smell.

The cooper said, "I'm not sending my lungs into that drain after a police whore."

"Then keep them here," Clemence snapped. "See how much tar they hold."

The man's mouth worked, but he did not move toward the exit either.

Lucien said, "Children and anyone who can't run go first. Therese. Pons. The boys. Clemence, you take the rear. Elise takes the front."

Clemence did not raise her voice. She did not need to. "You can ask me to crawl. You can't ask me to hand my girl over to her."

Elise felt every face come back to her.

Lucien said, "The first bend narrows. I'll jam there. You'll jam there. Elise won't."

"You don't know what she'll do once we're in it."

"I know what the smoke will do if you stay out of it."

That shut nobody up because nobody had anything useful left to say. The silence went thick with hatred and calculation.

Elise stepped past them to the open slab. The stone lip was slick under her palm. She hitched her skirt, put one boot down, then the other, and descended until the black water took her mid-calf. It was colder than the air above. The passage pinched at once, limestone on one side, old brick on the other where someone had once lined a runoff channel in a more hopeful decade. She crouched, moved forward three body lengths, then five. The roof stooped lower. Mud sucked at her boots. Ahead, where the passage forked, she could feel a thread of air touch the wet skin below her ear from the right-hand arm.

She backed out before the smoke behind her thickened further. When she rose through the floor hole the water ran black off her hem and pattered on the ration stones.

"It goes," she said. "The right branch still pulls air. The left is dead water."

The cooper looked at her skirt, at the muck streaking her stockings, and then away. It was not trust. It was proof.

Lucien crouched by the ration ledge and pulled a small oilcloth packet from behind the flour sack where Clemence kept the meaner necessities: extra lamp wick, the last hard soap, money nobody admitted aloud. He opened it. Notes and coins in one fold. Papers in the other: labor books, rent receipts, letters with names that could open one poor door and close another.

"The money goes with you," he said to Clemence.

"And the papers?"

"The wrong ones stay with me."

Elise understood before anyone asked. The decent forgeries had already been used or moved. What remained were the dirty copies, the emptied addresses, the names of men who had crossed the river or gone to ground months ago. Enough paper to make police feel rewarded. Enough paper to bend a search the wrong way for a few hours.

"Come with us," she said. It came out flatter than pleading. She did not know how to plead with him now without sounding like she meant to purchase something.

He met her eyes only long enough to refuse cleanly. "They know my face. They know the cough. Let them spend the morning on both."

"You'll be taken."

"Yes."

He said it like a number he had already carried once and knew the weight of.

Smoke rolled thicker along the ceiling. Somewhere back toward the cellar entrance a child began crying with the hiccuping outrage of a chest that could not fill properly. Clemence turned at once, swore under her breath, and dragged the smaller of the two boys toward the ration room by the wrist. Lucie did not need pulling. She had gone very still, which frightened Elise more than tears would have.

Lucien put the false papers into his coat. Then, as if remembering himself too late, he touched the chalk tin on the ledge with two fingers and left it there.

"Keep the right wall until the brick ends," he said to Elise. "Then duck. There's a root through the roof where the earth comes near the top. After that you'll hear the water split around a stone lip. Take the lower sound, not the louder one. The louder branch falls back into the old workings."

She repeated it once. He nodded.

"If the grate at the far end sticks, the third brick on the left is loose enough to use as a lever."

Clemence was already loading bodies toward the opening with the savage efficiency of a woman who had spent too many years making a household move on less than enough. "No bundles bigger than your own chest," she said. "Drop the pot. Drop it, I said. Therese, keep the needle roll if you must, but the basket stays. Lucie, where's your scarf? On. Now."

Therese Bertaud made one last small noise over the sewing basket before she dumped it. Spools hit the stone and rolled. One bright green thread ran a little way toward the floor hole like it meant to sew the quarry closed.

Elise held out her hand to Lucie.

Clemence saw it and gave a short, humorless breath through her nose. Then she took Lucie's hand herself, put it around Elise's scarred wrist instead of her palm, and said, "Hold there. Wrists don't slip."

Lucie tightened her fingers at once. "How far?" she asked again.

"Far enough to hate me," Elise said, "and not far enough to stop. Come on."

She went down into the throat of the drain and took the child with her.

The first stretch had to be done bent almost double. The water reached Elise's knees, then dropped, then rose again where the floor had sunk under years of runoff. Behind her she could hear the order of bodies by sound alone: Lucie's short breaths trying not to become panic, the wet drag of Therese's bad foot, the small smothered coughs of the boys, Clemence's voice behind everyone else cutting clean practical commands through the dark.

"Lower your head."

"Not there. My left, your left."

"If you vomit, vomit and move."

The roof came down farther after ten yards. Elise had to turn her shoulder and slide. The old brick lining gave way to raw limestone that felt greasy under her fingertips, rubbed smooth by water rather than use. Lucie's grip on her wrist tightened so hard it hurt.

"Is this under the bread room?" Lucie whispered.

A shudder passed through the stone overhead, not a full collapse but something heavy shifting where it had no right to. Silt thickened in the water around Elise's calves.

"Keep moving," Elise said.

"If it's under the bread room, Mother's jars are gone."

Elise pictured the ration ledge without wanting to: the sacks, the hard soap, the chipped blue bowl Clemence used for flour, Lucien's notebook once hidden there, the floor slab standing open like a mouth. She did not answer because an answer would have required either hope or precision, and she had neither to spare.

The passage opened just enough for her to straighten one vertebra at a time. She lifted the lamp she had borrowed from the ration room. The flame was low and mean inside its dirty chimney, but it caught the fork ahead. Left arm wider. Right arm narrower and darker. The wider branch breathed no air at all.

Behind her the cooper said, "Left climbs. I worked cuts like this before you were born."

Elise put the lamp down by the right wall and held her wet fingers up to the dark. The faint draft touched them again. On the limestone just beyond the right-hand bend, she found the edge of old brick under her palm, then lost it where the lining had broken away.

"Left climbs into a sump," she said. "Smell it."

The left branch stank of standing water and stone that never saw daylight. The right smelled worse in one sense and better in another: rot, mud, current, air from somewhere that had touched weeds and heat before it came back underground.

"You smell the outside?" Lucie asked, voice small and intent.

"A little."

"Then go."

Elise took the right arm. Therese Bertaud muttered something about saints she no longer believed in. One of the boys lost control of his stomach and vomited bright yellow into the black water, then began crying because his shoe floated off in the same moment. Elise bent, caught the shoe against the wall, and shoved it back into his hands without stopping.

The passage sank lower. Mud rose nearly to her thighs. Once the roof pressed so close she had to turn her face sideways to keep the lamp chimney from striking stone. Her shoulder blades rubbed rock. The lamp glass collected soot, then moisture. The air changed with each body added behind her, warmed by breath and fear until the drain seemed to be swallowing them and digesting them at once.

She counted without speaking. Herself. Lucie. Therese. Two boys. Pons with the bad eyes. Marius with the mint smell, making no sound now except when the cough caught him. Clemence at the back. Seven living weights connected by the dark.

This was what her reports had never contained. Not only names, not only entrances, not even the press or the false papers or the price of coal. This. How long it took a frightened child to lower herself into water she could not see. How much room an old woman's hip required at a turn. Which body had to go first because the wrong shoulders would stop all the rest.

Something struck stone behind them with a crack like a plate broken underfoot. The drain breathed inward hard. Lucie gave one sharp gasp and flattened against Elise.

"Was that the room?" she said. "Was that our room?"

Before Elise could answer, Clemence's voice came thin through the bend. "Forward, Lucie. Ask me outside."

The reply was so like her that the child obeyed it on instinct.

The next choke point took Therese Bertaud almost to pieces. Her skirt snagged on a protruding shard of brick and the remaining needle roll in her pocket slipped down into the water. She twisted after it with a cry of genuine grief, not because the needles were sentimental but because metal cost money and money cost labor and labor cost years.

"Leave it," Elise said.

"I paid for those."

"Then curse them later. Turn sideways."

Therese did not. Panic made her stupid and strong together. She shoved back against the bodies behind her. The whole line locked.

Elise wedged the lamp on a ledge, reached into the black water up to her forearm, found the sodden cloth roll by feel, and slapped it into Therese's hand.

"Now move."

Therese began to cry then, not elegantly, not with any old-woman dignity left. Wet, furious crying that turned to coughing and then to obedience. She turned. Elise dragged her by the sleeve through the brick teeth until the snag tore free and the passage took her.

The boys behind started crying in answer. Pons cursed them with a tenderness he tried to hide.

"Keep them low," Clemence said. "If you can talk, you can crawl."

They kept going.

The root Lucien had named came down through the ceiling twenty yards later, pale and blind in the lamp glow, thick as a child's wrist. Real earth smell clung to it. Elise ducked under, scraping her back, and felt the space change again. The water quickened around her ankles. Somewhere ahead it divided itself around stone, exactly as Lucien had said. One sound broad and showy. One narrow and lower, almost hidden by the first.

She paused only long enough to hear properly. Behind her the line breathed, waited, hated her, needed her.

The lower sound.

She bent into it.

The drain narrowed so severely after that that Lucie had to let go and crawl with both hands. Elise moved backward for a moment, guiding the child by elbows and shoulders, pushing her feet toward the right purchase in the mud.

"Don't look up," she said.

"I wasn't."

"Good."

"How much more?"

"More."

Lucie swallowed and nodded once, as if the answer had at least belonged to a world with measurements in it.

When the child got through, Elise sent the first boy after her, then the second. Pons came next and stuck halfway because terror made him arch his back against the stone. Elise put both palms flat on his ribs and pushed while Clemence cursed him from behind with kitchen precision so filthy it almost made the smaller boy laugh through his crying.

Then it was the cooper's turn. Halfway through the squeeze he said, very quietly, "If you drop us here, there won't be enough room to come back and finish it."

Elise knew what he meant. Not bodies. Betrayal.

"Then don't make yourself heavy," she said, and shoved until he came free.

The lamp went out two turns later. Not with a dramatic gutter, only a small surrendering hiss as dirty water slopped across the base. Darkness shut over them at once, total and physical. Lucie made a sound that was not yet a scream.

Elise put one hand on the wall and the other back until she found Lucie's hair, damp braid half undone.

"Here," she said.

Clemence's voice came from the rear, thinner now, farther than Elise liked. "Talk, all of you."

So they did, because silence in the dark would have made the drain too wide for the mind to hold.

Pons said, "I'm here."

Therese said, "My foot's in the wrong of it."

One boy said, "I lost the shoe again."

Lucie said, "I've got the bird."

The child had held onto the paper swallow through mud and panic and vomit and the squeeze where adults nearly got trapped alive. Elise felt something in her own chest give way from usefulness into pain and then harden again because pain was no use here.

"Keep your hand on my skirt," she said. "When it pulls, move."

The air freshened by degrees so slight she first mistrusted them. Then it came colder, not with quarry damp but with the outside world's filthy range in it: weed rot, horse piss, river slime, dye vats somewhere distant, hot stone touched by sun. The roof lifted. Mud gave way to gravel. Her fingers found iron ahead.

The grate.

It had been meant once to keep rubbish out of the drain mouth. Rust had eaten it nearly through at the lower bars, but the upper hinge still held. Elise felt along the wall to the third brick on the left, exactly as Lucien had promised. It shifted under her thumb.

She worked it free, used it as a lever, and drove the corner between iron and stone until the hinge gave with a shriek loud enough to sound like the police themselves had found them.

Daylight knifed through the gap.

Not full open sky. A ditch first, nettles, a wall beyond, then a slice of white-hot summer over the top of it. Enough.

Elise shoved the grate outward, crawled into black ditch water and weed stalks, then turned and hauled Lucie through by the forearms. The child came up coated to the elbows in mud, hair hanging in ropes, face streaked white where chalk dust had turned to paste on her skin.

"Outside," Lucie said, not as joy but as fact checking.

"Yes."

They dragged the others out one by one. Therese emerged weeping and clutching the salvaged needle roll like relics. Pons blinked in the light as if it insulted him personally. One boy collapsed onto the bank and retched river water and bile into the nettles. The cooper lay on his back for three breaths with his mint leaves plastered to his chest.

Clemence came last, shoulder and hip scraping hard enough against the stone lip to leave skin on it. She rolled clear, got to her knees at once, and counted heads aloud, tapping each one with two fingers as if she were checking linen at the wash.

"Lucie. Therese. Pons. The boys. Marius. Me."

Her eyes cut to Elise.

"You count yourself."

Elise did. Seven.

The ditch ran behind a dyer's boundary wall where stripped willow branches leaned in a heap and somebody had dumped broken crockery years ago. The Bievre lay somewhere beyond the next row of yards. The air was foul in the ordinary aboveground way, broad and layered and alive. After the drain it felt obscene in its abundance.

From the direction of the quarry came the dull, continuous labor sound of men sealing something they intended to own afterward.

Lucie twisted to look back toward the wall, toward nothing she could actually see from here. "Where's Lucien?"

Clemence wiped mud off her mouth with the back of one hand. "Buying us time."

"How much?"

Clemence did not answer because no answer would have kept the child quiet.

Elise stood. Her legs were shaking, not with generalized fear but with the cold still locked into her muscles from the drain water. Mud sucked at her hem. Her scarred wrist throbbed where Lucie had gripped it. Beyond the wall another shout carried, then another. Not close. Not yet.

"If they take the papers and believe them," she said, "they'll push east first. They think in addresses once they think they've won."

Clemence's mouth hardened. "And if they don't?"

"Then they sweep the yards."

"You sound cheerful."

"I sound exact."

For a moment Clemence only stared at her. Then she nodded once toward the children huddled in the ditch bank shadow.

"So be exact usefully. See what the cordon's doing."

It was not forgiveness. It was not even trust. It was labor assigned where labor might help. Elise took it for what it was.

"Keep them low," she said. "If I don't come back in ten minutes, you move west and don't wait."

"We were moving west before you were born," Clemence said.

Elise left them under the nettles and climbed the ditch with both hands.

The ground above the wall was a ragged strip of scrub between yards, hot already under the noon sun. The sudden brightness after the drain made her eyes water. She moved bent at the waist through waist-high weeds and broken plaster until she had a view over a stack of rotting barrels toward the slope where the wine-shop entrance lay hidden beneath ordinary Paris business on other days.

There was nothing ordinary there now.

Smoke drifted out low and brown from the cellar stair mouth and from a second opening farther north where old quarry stone had once been disguised by rubbish and scrub. Police stood back from both, not charging, not brave, cloths tied over nose and mouth, sleeves rolled, more work gang than assault party. Beside them municipal laborers and hired masons were already setting rubble and mortar at the wine stairs. A cart of broken stone waited uphill. Another had tipped its load beside the north slit. This was how the state did heroism when it expected no witnesses worth respecting: with shovels, contractors, lime.

At the edge of the cordon stood Vautrin in a summer coat the color of old bone. Even here, even with smoke drifting across his polished boots, he kept his hat level and his cuffs cleaner than the men who obeyed him. One sleeve carried a streak of mortar dust where he had brushed against the unfinished wall. He had not noticed it yet.

Elise flattened herself behind the barrels and looked for Lucien.

She found him where the mapped north slit opened into light between two heaps of spoil. He did not burst out. He climbed. One hand on the stone, one on the bank, coat blackened at the front, his body pausing halfway for a cough that looked big enough to tear him loose from himself. When he reached air properly he got one breath. Then the police were on him.

He did not fight them. Fighting would have spent the wrong currency. He let them wrench his arms back. The false papers came half out of his coat in the struggle, a fan of damp labor books and receipts and folded letters dropping into the dust.

Vautrin crossed the last yards at a walk, as if arriving late to a meeting he nevertheless controlled. One officer held Lucien upright by the elbows while another kicked the scattered papers together with his boot.

Even at this distance Elise could read Lucien's exhaustion in the set of his shoulders. He had spent what lungs he had left underground and saved the rest for this.

Vautrin stooped, picked up a labor book, opened it, then another. His posture changed almost invisibly. Not surprise. Appetite.

He said something Elise could not hear over the scrape of the masons at the wine stairs. Whatever it was sent two men and a sergeant running east at once, down toward streets where the rooms named in those papers no longer held the people attached to them. Good. Lucien had bought them something after all.

Then Vautrin said another sentence, quieter. The officer nearest him nodded and pointed at the cellar mouth. The masons resumed. Stone on stone. Mortar slapped wet between the courses. A man with a shovel began throwing loose rubble into the north slit behind Lucien as if filling a rut in the road.

Lucien turned his head once, not far enough to search the yards, only far enough to look toward the quarry hill itself while they tied his wrists. Smoke streaked his cheek. White limestone dust clung to the silver in his hair. Elise could not tell whether he was listening for collapse, for voices below, for nothing at all.

Vautrin took hold of Lucien's chin and lifted it in a gesture so controlled it was worse than a blow.

This time Elise heard him.

"You have been expensive," he said.

Lucien answered, and the answer came back to her only in fragments because a cart wheel hit stone and drowned the middle of it.

"... cheaper than your lie ..."

The officer behind him struck him in the kidneys for that, efficient as closing a drawer. Lucien folded at the waist, coughed hard, and nearly went to his knees. Vautrin stepped back so the mud from the boots would not touch him.

The quarry gave a low interior thunder then, some partial fall or deliberate dump of rubble into an already weakened shaft. The ground under Elise's palms trembled. One of the laborers swore and jumped back from the north slit. Dust breathed out around the stones they were shoveling in, white and sudden as flour from a burst sack.

There it was. Not a dramatic annihilation, not the clean ending the newspapers would prefer. A home being made uninhabitable by inches. The schoolroom wall where Lucien had written sums in chalk. Clemence's ration ledge. The sleeping gallery with its patched blankets and bad dreams and bodies packed close through winter. The copper hand press left in the deeper chamber because metal had become less urgent than breath. The floor slab over the drain. The tobacco tin of chalk stubs on the ledge. All of it staying under the hill while men above argued over paperwork and mixed mortar.

Lucie had once asked Elise whether caves remembered voices. Elise had almost laughed then at the childishness of it. Now, watching the wall go up across the wine stairs and the rubble choke the slit, she understood the better question was what happened when the voices had nowhere left to go.

Vautrin handed the damp false papers to a sergeant. Lucien's wrists were tied. A wagon had been brought up, plain and municipal, the sort used to move confiscated furniture or sacks of spoiled grain. They put him in it with the same economy.

Elise stayed where she was until the cart jolted once and started toward the city. Only then did she back down from the barrels and return through the weeds.

Clemence had the children moving already, west along the ditch where willow shadow broke the line of sight from the yards. Therese limped and cried at intervals without admitting she was crying. One of the boys had Lucie's paper swallow now because the child's hands were needed to keep hold of her mother's skirt. The swallow had gone soft enough to tear at the next hard touch.

Clemence looked once at Elise's face and read the answer there.

"Taken?" she asked.

"Yes."

"Did he turn them?"

"East first."

Clemence nodded. That was all either of them could afford from the news.

Lucie, still clutching the back of her mother's skirt, said, "Are we going home after dark?"

No one corrected the word. No one said there was no home left under the hill, only chambers filling with smoke and dust and the slow state craft of walling up what poor people had made useful.

Elise looked back once through the gap between two yards. Above the rubble line the summer sky had gone brilliantly empty. Below it the quarry mouth vanished course by course under a mason's trowel. The men worked steadily, as if closing a pantry.

Then Clemence hissed for the line to move, and Elise moved with them because the living were still on this side of the wall.


# Chapter 17

The Charenton papers lay squared to the edge of Vautrin's desk as neatly as cartridges. Blue account stock, red pencil, the asylum seal pressed into two of them so hard the paper had gone white around the stamp. Elise saw the total before she sat down. Three hundred and twelve francs overdue. Twenty-eight due again at the month's turn. Her side pulled when she bent, the half-healed damage from the tollhouse catching under the stays of a dress that still smelled faintly of old smoke no matter how often she beat it out.

Vautrin remained standing for a moment, one hand on the back of his chair, as if he meant to let the numbers do the first part of the work. His cuffs were white. The room beyond the glass partition was not. Clerks moved through heat and dust with ink on their thumbs and sweat dark under their arms, but his office held the stale cool of shut shutters and authority. Even the spittoon had been polished.

"Sit down, Mlle Valette," he said. "Standing will not improve the arithmetic."

She sat because the pain had already decided for her. "You stopped paying."

"I stopped advancing funds against an operative who had burned state material, vanished from supervision, and declined to deliver the map or the statement subsequently requested of her. The Charenton office was patient for a fortnight. It has now become exact."

He pushed the top sheet toward her with one clean finger. There was another paper beneath it, not a bill but a transfer note. If the arrears were not covered, her mother would be moved out of the paying wing and into the common women's ward. Elise did not need the clerk's hand to imagine the rest. She had once followed an attendant through the wrong door while looking for a doctor and had seen the ward by mistake: iron bedsteads close enough for the blankets to touch, chamber pots shoved under frames, a woman with a shaved patch over one ear rubbing her gums on the blanket edge as if she could grind the cloth into food. Her mother had not known her in her small room either, not on that day, but the private room had still left a scrap of dignity to fail inside. The ward took even that.

"What do you want said?" Elise asked.

Vautrin sat at last. He folded his hands over the blotter. "That Lucien Delatour directed the atmosphere that produced the tollhouse outrage. That he knowingly harbored Ferrand, encouraged Saint-Clair's appetite for theater, and used his authority over that refuge to make violence inevitable."

"That is not what happened."

"It is the version a court can use."

She looked at him. "Ferrand worked for you."

"Ferrand received money from several directions, as such men do. He is dead. Delatour is alive, which means he remains available for public meaning."

The phrasing was almost enough to make her laugh. Public meaning. Abel in pieces under brick and chalk, Nadir dead in a room no comrade had seen, the quarry sealed like a grave with people still coughing in her memory, and Vautrin called it meaning. She kept the laugh down because he liked evidence of strain.

"You already have witnesses," she said. "You have police. You have whatever Ferrand told you before he died."

"I have policemen who entered after the fact, a provocateur whose usefulness ended in fragments, and headlines that must be nailed to something sturdier than rumor. You can give me sequence, access, intimacy. You slept in Delatour's rooms. You heard him in private. A woman who has shared a bed is believed in ways a sergent-de-ville is not."

He said it without savoring it. That made it worse. He reduced her body to evidentiary value as neatly as he reduced a dead printer to a category and a boy to an appetite. She thought of the quarry's narrow sleeping gallery, blankets slung from hooks, Lucien waking once with his hand spread stupidly warm across her stomach as if the world were not built to bill and seize and classify. She had betrayed him there before she ever admitted it out loud. Vautrin wanted to invoice the rest.

"And if I do it?" she said.

"Your stipend resumes. The Charenton arrears are settled. The monthly account continues. The file records that you overextended yourself in difficult circumstances but returned to usefulness before lasting damage was done."

"My file."

"Among other things."

He let that sit between them. He did not need to say accomplice. He did not need to say destroyed state property, withheld intelligence, concealed known radicals, lied under instruction, lay with a wanted organizer and came back with nothing but a scorched dress and a talent for making herself pitiable. The forms existed. He had told her so in other rooms about other people, and she had been cold enough then to admire the mechanism.

She touched the bill with the backs of her fingers. The paper was thick, better quality than anything in the quarry except Lucien's notebook and the map on linen backing. Three hundred and twelve francs. More than three months of her old pay, if Vautrin paid on time. More than a season of Clemence's laundry work when the weather was against her. More money than Elise could raise quickly by honest means, which was to say by none. Her purse held eleven francs and some sous, enough for food if she began counting each loaf, not enough to buy a mother an extra month of calm.

"How long before they move her?" she asked.

"The note says Thursday."

He did not say the date. He did not need to. Thursday now meant a bed change, a door opening, an attendant with a ledger and a tired face telling her mother to take her things when most of those things were not her own and half of what was hers had already been lost to damp or another woman's hands.

"If I refuse?"

"Then the account remains what it is, and I am obliged to think more carefully about your own exposure. You understand the difficulty. A confidential operative is a discreet category only while she remains productive."

Elise looked at the transfer note again. The clerk at Charenton had blotted one word too early, so that common had a blue tail beneath it like a stain spreading through linen. She thought of her mother's hair being redone by whichever attendant had the least disgust that day. She thought of the last time she had paid for oranges on the way out because her mother had once liked the smell even after names went loose in her head. She thought of walking into some newspaper office and saying exactly what Vautrin needed in the tone a jury would trust: regretful, factual, intimate. Delatour believed this. Delatour ordered that. Delatour made boys dangerous. It would work because so much of it could be laid over the truth like tracing paper.

Vautrin watched her think. Not hungrily. Administratively. He had always understood that the body broke before the creed did, and bills before either.

"No," she said.

His eyelids lowered a fraction. "You should not answer quickly. Quick answers belong to people with savings."

"I said no."

"Because you love him?"

"Because Ferrand has had enough of the page."

That did it. Not rage. Something flatter and meaner. Vautrin leaned back and smoothed an invisible crease from his cuff. "Love is often only vanity with a bruise on it. Still, I am not unreasonable. You may wish to see what you are buying with this refusal."

He opened a drawer and took out a visitor's authorization. La Sante prison, infirmary corridor, same day, signed with the swift pressure of a man used to making doors open for people he would never touch. He laid it over the Charenton bill.

"He asked for you once," Vautrin said. "That was yesterday. I have delayed answering in case you recovered your judgment. You may go now. If you return before six with a statement I can use, the note to Charenton goes out tonight and the account continues without interruption. If you do not return, I shall assume you prefer principle to comfort. Principle is not an economy I have ever seen balance."

Elise took the prison authorization. She left the bill where it lay because touching both papers at once felt too much like consent.

The corridor outside was all scratch and murmur. A copy clerk with a nicotine-yellow moustache was trimming the edge of a stack of warrants with a paper knife as carefully as if he were making devotional cards; when he looked up at Elise his left eye watered without blinking, and he dabbed it with the same square of linen he used on the blade. He had probably gone home each night to someone who asked whether the city was calmer now. Elise wondered what answer he liked best.

She went down the stairs slowly, one hand on the rail when the pain made the wall seem less dependable than metal. By the time she reached the street, sweat had soaked the waistband of her chemise. Paris smelled of horse heat, drains, and hot bread from somewhere she could not afford to stop. She counted her coins in her pocket while she walked, not because the count might improve but because the fingers wanted work. Eleven francs, four sous, a bent five-centime piece, and the small brass thimble Clemence had once thrown at her during mending because Elise held a needle like a woman born to copying and not to cloth. She had forgotten to give it back. It had ridden out the quarry in her pocket like a petty theft from a life that no longer wanted her.

La Sante rose out of the afternoon in straight lines and official brick, every wall declaring that men had measured this space and meant to keep it measured. The quarry had closed around bodies because history and theft had eaten it hollow. The prison closed around bodies because the state preferred its holes numbered.

Women waited beneath the outer arch with baskets on their arms and food smells clinging to them: rabbit fat, onion, stale coffee, a packet of cherries going soft in the heat. Elise had brought nothing. The lack shamed her more than the search that followed. A wardress with thyme tucked brown and brittle inside her collar took Elise's permit, squinted at the signature, and said, "Hands out. Pins, paper, coin."

Mme Drouet was stitched in fading thread above the woman's pocket, though the wardress herself was broad as a washtub and old enough to have sons. One boot was laced with blue string instead of leather, and she kept hitching at the hem of her skirt between each instruction, not from modesty but because the seam had come loose and worried her more than the prisoners.

Elise emptied her pocket into the tray. Coins. Handkerchief. The brass thimble. Drouet turned the thimble over in her palm.

"Useful thing to hold on to," she said, and set it aside with the rest. "You get ten minutes if he does not start bleeding again. If he starts bleeding, you step back and let me work."

"Is he in the infirmary?"

"If he were fit for the regular room, you would be in the regular room."

No cruelty in it. Only practice. Drouet waved her through a second gate.

The prison took her inward by measured intervals: one courtyard, one iron door, a short passage that smelled of limewash and piss, another gate, the echo of keys moving ahead of them before the man carrying them came into view. Elise had learned the quarry by wrong turns, by scraped knuckles and counting breaths between drips. Here the state had done the counting for her. Fourteen paces to the stair. Nine steps up. Corridor to the left. Window high enough to admit light without horizon. Nothing in La Sante asked a body to trust instinct. The place existed to replace instinct with procedure.

Drouet stopped outside a narrow room where the infirmary overflow had been made into a visiting space by means of a table, two chairs, and the pretense that anything supervised was therefore orderly. "Ten minutes," she repeated. "If you pass paper, I see it. If you shout, I end it. If he falls, do not catch him unless I tell you."

She did not leave altogether. She settled on a stool by the door, drew a sock from her pocket, and resumed darning the heel with a bone needle, her lips moving as she counted stitches under her breath.

Lucien stood when Elise entered, then stopped halfway upright and put one hand on the table until the coughing passed.

He looked smaller because prison had taken the room around him that usually completed him. In the quarry he had always seemed to belong to whatever wall or bench he leaned against, as if stone had accepted his measure. Here the chair, table, and iron bed beyond the half curtain all refused him. His coat hung wrong on his shoulders. There was dried blood in the seam of one nostril and an old yellow bruise under his right eye that had gone the color of weak tea. His beard had come in more silver than she remembered. When he straightened fully, she saw where the wrists had been rubbed raw above the bones, not dramatic, just thorough.

"Did Lucie get clear?" he asked.

No greeting. No accusation either. Straight to the child. His voice had thinned but not lost its shape.

"Yes."

"Clemence?"

"Yes."

He nodded once. "Good. Then we can waste the rest on harder things."

Elise sat because standing made her feel like a witness already. The chair leg was shorter than the others and rocked on the floorboards. "Vautrin gave me the permit."

"I assumed as much."

"He wants testimony."

Lucien smiled with one corner of his mouth, a habit she had first mistaken for ease. "Of course he does."

"He wants me to say you ordered the tollhouse. That Ferrand acted with your knowledge. That Abel followed your instruction. If I do it, Charenton stays paid."

That last part changed his face more than the first. Not to pity. To attention. He lowered himself into the other chair carefully, as if his lungs were glass vessels being carried by a clumsy servant.

"How much?"

"Three hundred and twelve overdue. Twenty-eight more by the month's turn. Thursday for the transfer."

He looked at the table while he counted it. She could see him doing the math, not because he loved numbers but because numbers were what poor people got instead of mercy. At length he said, "That is a filthy clean bargain."

"It would keep her out of the common ward."

"I know what it would keep her out of."

Drouet's needle clicked once against her bone thimble by the door. Outside, someone coughed behind a wall with the loose, wet sound of a man not yet done being beaten.

Elise kept her hands flat in her lap. "If I tell them Ferrand worked for the police, it will sound like a lover trying to save the man she helped ruin."

"Because that is what it will be."

The sentence landed plain, without force raised for effect. He had always sounded most dangerous when he stopped dressing pain in wit.

"I came anyway," she said.

"Yes."

"I don't know why that still surprises me."

"It should not. You have never lacked nerve. Only a place to spend it that did not rot the hand using it."

He coughed again, turned aside, pressed a cloth to his mouth, folded it before she could see too much, and set it down with schoolmaster neatness. The cloth had been white in some earlier life. Now it carried the rust-dark center of the prison infirmary.

"Listen to me," he said. "If you mean to save me by saying I'm innocent, don't."

She almost answered on reflex and stopped herself because Drouet was in the room and Vautrin might as well have been in the walls. "You're not guilty of that."

"Of the blast? No. Of plenty else, yes. I hid men the Republic wanted. I kept false papers. I printed names they preferred to lose. I let boys talk themselves into being history because I thought time and work would cool them before vanity or grief set fire to them. I was wrong about one of them, and he's dead. Don't drag me into court as some harmless tutor caught among wolves by accident. They won't believe it, and I won't wear it."

"Then what am I supposed to call you?"

"What I am."

"And what is that today?"

For the first time something like tired amusement passed over his face. "A prisoner with a bad cough and a poor opinion of strategy."

She should not have smiled. The smile came anyway and hurt more than the wound under her stays. Drouet glanced up from the sock, saw nothing actionable, and went back to counting stitches.

"He can make her life smaller," Elise said quietly. "My mother's. He has already started. He knew exactly which paper to put on top."

"I know."

"Don't say you know unless you mean Charenton by smell."

That brought his eyes fully to her. She had not raised her voice. She had not needed to. The memory did it for her: chloride, cabbage water, old linen boiled too often, the sweet wrong odor of women who had forgotten when to ask for the pot. Her mother's fingers moving on the blanket as if they still sorted ribbon lengths in the shop where she had once worked before grief and fever and whatever else had cracked her loose from the world. Elise had learned the price of calm there. Twenty-eight francs a month for a room with a latch and one attendant who still called her mother Madame on good days.

"I mean it," Lucien said. "If you had sold me for that, I would've understood the arithmetic. I wouldn't have called it clean. But I would've understood it."

There it was. Not absolution. Not condemnation either. Only the ugliest kind of mercy, the kind that left the act ugly.

"That's not what I came for."

"No." He leaned back, shut his eyes for a second, then opened them again. "You came because part of you still wants me to sort the truth so you can live inside the better half."

She looked at the door, at Drouet's bent head, at the sock growing beneath the wardress's blunt fingers. "Was any of it true?"

Lucien did not pretend not to understand. The quarry had stripped them of that sort of cowardice months ago.

"You know it was," he said. "You also know what else was true. You loved me. You reported on me. You came back after you knew what I was and after you knew what you were. I'm not going to take those things apart and hand you the clean pieces."

Her throat filled with something mean and hot that was not quite tears and not quite anger. She thought of his mouth on the scar inside her wrist, of the way he had once read proofs aloud while buttoning his shirt because there had been no hour left in the day that belonged only to desire, of Abel dead before nineteen had learned how ordinary his hands were, of Clemence's face when the brass marker dropped from the torn hem. There were no clean pieces. She had known that before she asked. She had asked anyway because wanting the lie did not make her foolishness any less real.

Lucien drew a breath that turned into another shallow cough. When it passed, he glanced toward Drouet. The wardress was knotting thread with her teeth now, giving them the professional privacy of someone who had seen too many people say worse in lower voices.

"There's something else," he said.

He reached inside the lining of his coat and brought out a small notebook no bigger than his palm. The blue paper cover had been rubbed to gray at the corners. One edge was warped from damp. Elise knew it at once. She had seen it in the print chamber, in Lucien's coat pocket, under his hand while he copied corrections from prison notices or burial scraps or the names children gave him for adults who had stopped coming home. He set it on the table between them as if it were a last crust.

She stared. "They let you keep it?"

"They looked at it and saw poor accounts. Dates. Names. Men like Vautrin always believe numbers serve whoever writes them first."

"What's in it now?"

"Enough."

He pushed the notebook toward her two inches. Drouet did not look up. The bone needle moved in and out of wool.

"Nadir is in there properly," Lucien said. "Not the way the papers had him. Abel too. Henri Vasseur. Two women from the lodgings on rue des Plantes who were called prostitutes in print because the police found men in the room and could not imagine cheaper reasons for bodies to share space. A mason buried under the wrong name because his brother could not pay to correct the clerk. Prison dates. Arrest dates. The little thefts official language makes when it thinks nobody important is watching."

Elise put her hand over the notebook but did not take it yet. "Why give it to me?"

"Because you know how their files move. Because you can still read a ledger and hear where it lies. Because if I keep it, they will either take it properly next time or let it rot with me, and both are waste."

"I can try to get you out."

His expression changed so little that the refusal seemed to arrive before the words. "No."

"You don't know what I still have."

"You don't have enough."

"I've got old routes. I know people who owe me."

"Then spend none of that on a rescue fable." His voice stayed low, but it gathered force by refusing drama. "The quarry is gone. Abel is dead. Nadir is dead. Ferrand has already been turned into whatever the police need a dead agent provocateur to be, which is to say not himself. If they kill me here or try me first and kill me after, I don't need a lawyer's little innocence. I need witness."

The word settled in the room heavier than plea would have.

"With what?" she asked. "A notebook?"

"With the notebook. With what you know about Ferrand's wages, if you still have any of it in your head. With your own hand, if that's all that's left to you. Put the names beside the money. Put the dates beside the headlines. Make them step over the people they have been calling anarchist, unknown, female accomplice, Arab compositor, as if a label were the same thing as a life. Do that instead of trying to fetch me out through doors that are already shut."

He slid the notebook fully into her reach.

She picked it up. The cover was warm from his body.

"You're asking me to outlive this neatly," she said.

"No." The dry corner smile again, gone almost before it formed. "I'm asking you to do one thing badly and all the way through. Neatness is for clerks."

Drouet rose from her stool. "One minute."

Lucien looked at Elise's hands around the notebook. "Hide it."

She slipped it inside her bodice, under the stays, where the pressure of the cover lay hard against the tender line of healing under her ribs. The pain made her suck a breath between her teeth.

"Still bad?" he asked.

"I'm walking."

"That was not an answer before either."

The tenderness in it came too late to be mercy and too early to be farewell. She hated him a little for managing that.

"Lucie asks practical questions at night," she said, because if she did not speak now she would do something idiotic like touch his hand in front of the wardress. "Clemence still counts bread by halves even when there is nobody left to impress. They got through because you stayed."

"Good."

"That's all you have to say?"

He met her eyes. Prison had thinned him, but it had not gentled him into the kind of man she might have deserved more easily. "No. It's only all that fits before she throws you out."

Drouet came to the table, sock in one hand, key ring at her belt knocking lightly against her thigh. Up close Elise saw that the blue string in her boot had been braided from embroidery silk, probably cut out of some child's ruined cuff. Drouet looked from one face to the other, not curious, merely tired.

"Time."

Lucien stood because habit was stronger than pain. He had to put his hand on the chair back again when the cough caught him. Elise did not move to help. Drouet had told her not to, and besides, the room had become too full of rules to risk one more human reflex.

"Elise," Lucien said.

He had not used her true name aloud since the rupture in the quarry.

She stopped in the doorway.

"Do not let them make him into the only dead man in the story," he said. "That is how they win these things. One monster, one file, one grave broad enough to cover everybody."

She nodded because her mouth had gone useless.

Drouet escorted her back through the measured passages. At the search table she returned her handkerchief, coins, and thimble to her pocket. The wardress glanced once at Elise's bodice, where the notebook's edge must have altered the line by a finger's width, but all she said was, "Do not come in this heat with black wool again. You will faint and I will have to carry you."

It was an opinion, not kindness, but it belonged to Drouet and not to the prison, which made it feel larger than it should have.

Outside, the light struck hard off the paving stones. The women with baskets had changed places with another line of women, newer parcels, same faces of waiting worn into different shapes. Elise moved into the narrow strip of shade along the wall and opened the notebook just enough to see the first page.

Lucien's hand was as she remembered it: legible even in haste, each entry given room enough to remain human.

Nadir Belkacem. 3 June. Arrested on delivery, not in flight. No knife. No escape. Body not returned.

Abel Saint-Clair, nineteen. 4 June. Printer. Burn marks on two fingertips. Dead in the tollhouse cellar before the papers made him a doctrine.

Henri Vasseur. 27 March. Taken alive. Not drunk. Not missing by choice.

Below the names were dates, prisons, wrong spellings crossed out and corrected, burial grounds, the names of mothers and sisters where Lucien had been able to find them. Not unknown female. Not Arab male. Not agitator dead in police action. Name, date, where taken, how lied about.

The book in her bodice weighed almost nothing. The pressure of it on the wound made every step toward the street distinct. The quarry map had once been the object that mattered, a way through stone, a route for bodies still alive enough to move. This was smaller, meaner, and in its way more final. It offered no path back, no clever exit, no hidden turn under the city. Only the work of not letting the state finish the theft it had begun.


# Chapter 18

The first week of July had gone hot enough to make La Sante smell of limewash and old piss before noon. By the time Elise reached the prison gate, the stone on the outer wall was already sweating under a film of dust, and the line of visitors had closed up so tightly that every basket handle and shawled shoulder kept brushing her sleeves. A woman ahead of her carried two hard eggs in a handkerchief. Another had a loaf under her arm and a bruise yellowing along the edge of her jaw. Nobody spoke above the level required by the walls. The prison took sound and flattened it.

The registry window stood open to a room no larger than a pantry. The clerk behind it had one ear puffed and folded from an old blow, and he kept scratching at it with the feathered end of his pen between names. He took visiting slips, glanced once, stamped them, and pushed them back with the same indifferent wrist. When Elise gave him hers, his eyes dropped to the line, then moved sideways to a ledger thick enough to stop a bullet. He found the page, wetted his thumb, and turned another.

"Delatour," he said. He pronounced it cleanly, as if accuracy cost him nothing. "No visit."

"I was told today."

"You were told badly, then."

He drew a small printed form from beneath the ledger and wrote on it without hurrying. The nib scratched. A fly kept touching the inkstone and lifting away again. At Elise's back, the line compressed by another inch.

"What does that mean?" she asked.

He sanded the line, tipped the excess onto the floor, and slid the form through the gap. "It means the prisoner expired in the infirmary at four-ten this morning. Pulmonary congestion, according to the doctor. Effects withheld pending review. Next."

The word expired sat on the page like a tradesman's bill settled and filed. Elise looked at the hour first. Four-ten. He had still been alive when the bell struck the quarter before dawn somewhere over the city. She had been asleep in her room on rue du Cherche-Midi with the notebook under her pillow and a chair shoved under the latch because there was nobody left she trusted not to come in.

"I want to see him."

The clerk had already reached for the next basket. "That isn't done."

"I saw him two days ago."

"You don't see him now."

"I want the doctor."

"You want a petition." He tapped the printed form with the pen. "If you're family, state so in writing. If you're not, don't waste the paper."

The woman behind Elise sucked air through her teeth. Somebody farther back asked whether the beef in a parcel would spoil before the turn came. The clerk lifted his head a fraction and called, "Next," over Elise's shoulder with the steady patience of a man who believed that every grief came in the same size once it reached his window.

She stepped aside because the line made her step. There was no ceremony to refuse. A big woman with red knuckles planted her basket where Elise's hands had been and began asking after a son in the remand wing. The clerk scratched at his broken ear and opened the ledger again.

Outside the gate, the heat hit with more force than it had in the queue. The street had the white glare of afternoon coming early, and a cart full of cabbages was backing badly around a watering trough while the driver cursed the horse with flat professional hatred. A boy ran past shouting headlines about a ministerial quarrel. Another was selling a racing sheet. Nobody shouted about the man who had coughed blood in a cell until the prison doctor wrote congestion on a line and the clerk passed the day forward.

Elise stood with the paper in her glove until the sweat from her palm softened it. There was no body for her to look at, no last confession forced into a scene fit for papers, no witness except a ledger and a clerk with an old damaged ear. Lucien had not died under flags or speeches. He had died under inventory. She could almost see the room anyway: the narrow iron bed, the basin furred with lime, the blanket that never dried properly once the lungs started giving way. He had spent months teaching children to read on quarry stone, and the Republic had found a way to reduce him to a time of death and a cause written in four quick words.

She folded the paper once and walked because standing still would have made her visible.

Her room was under the roof of a widow who rented by the week and watered the wine she sold downstairs more shamelessly with each rise in temperature. The stairwell smelled of boiled onions and dust from the carpet runner. In the room itself, the air had not moved since dawn. Elise shut the door, drew the bolt, and put the printed death notice beside the washbasin. Then she took Lucien's notebook from under the pillow and the other paper from the hem she had stitched inside her valise weeks ago, the one she had copied in police shorthand after slipping through Vautrin's office in May.

That second paper was thin as onion skin and filled with numbers nobody outside a bureau was supposed to care about. Three cash disbursements to Jules Ferrand. One advance routed through a tobacconist on rue Saint-Jacques. One reimbursement for lodgings. One bonus marked after event, the kind of phrase Vautrin liked because it let men do murder inside grammar.
